Commentary
This passage begins the seventh proof presented by the philosophers. They argue that all perceptual faculties that rely on bodily organs (like the senses) suffer fatigue and weakening from continuous use, because physical motion disrupts the bodily temperament. Strong stimuli can even temporarily impair them. This is a premise to argue that the intellect, which does not tire from prolonged or intense thinking, must be non-physical. The proof is incomplete in this window; it ends mid-sentence, presumably continuing with the conclusion that the intellect does not fatigue, therefore it is not bodily. Ghazali's refutation would follow in the next pages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Have you ever used that sleepless moment to remember Allah, or do you let your mind wander aimlessly? Seventh Proof: Intellect Not Weakened by Intense Use
Ghazali presents and refutes the philosophers' seventh proof for soul's independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that the intellect's resistance to fatigue proves it is non-bodily.

Core Idea
Bodies can vary; one rule does not fit all.

The philosophers argued that since bodily senses tire from overuse but the intellect strengthens, the intellect must be non-physical. Ghazali responds by pointing out that bodies themselves can have diverse reactions to motion and stimuli, so the difference does not prove immateriality. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the philosophers present their proof that the rational power does not weaken with use, unlike bodily senses.
Notice the examples of loud sound and bright light.
2
Identify the key contrast
The philosophers contrast bodily senses (which tire) with the rational power (which strengthens). Ask yourself why they think this difference is significant.
3
Understand Ghazali's refutation
Read Ghazali's response starting from 'وهذا، من الطراز السابق'. He argues that bodies can differ in their properties, so what holds for one sense may not hold for another or for the intellect.
Ghazali's key point: 'الحكم الثابت لبعض الأشياء، ليس يلزم أن يثبت للكل'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation undermines the philosophers' proof. Does it prove the intellect is bodily? No, but it shows the proof is inconclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل سابع قالوا : القوى المدركة بالآلات الجسمانية، يعرض لها من المواظبة على العمل بإدامة الإدراك، كلال، لأن إدامة الحركة، تفسد مزاج الأجسام، فتكلها. وكذلك الأمور القوية الجلية الإدراك، توهنها، وربما تفسدها، حتى لا تدرك عقيبها، الأخفى والأضعف، كالصوت العظيم للسمع، والنور العظيم للبصر، فإنهما ربما يفسدان، أو يمنعان، عقبيهما، من إدراك الصوت الخفى، والمرئيات الدقيقة، بل من ذاق الحلاوة الشديدة، لا يحس بعدها بحلاوة دونها. والأمر فى القوة العقلية بالعكس، فإن إدامتها للنظر إلى المعقولات، لا يتعبها ودرك الضروريات الجلية يقويها، على درك النظريات الخفية، ولا يضعفها، وإن عرض لها فى بعض الأوقات كلال، فذلك لاستعمالها القوة الخيالية، واستعانتها بها، فتضعف آلة القوة الخيالية، فلا تخدم العقل. وهذا، من الطراز السابق، فإنا نقول : لا يبعد أن تختلف الحواس الجسمانية، في هذه الأمور، فليس ما يثبت منها للبعض، يجب أن يثبت للآخر بل لا يبعد أن تتفاوت الأجسام، فيكون منها ما يضعفها نوع من الحركة، ومنها ما يقويها نوع من الحركة ولا يوهنها، وإن كان يؤثر فيها، فيكون ثم سبب يجدد قوتها، بحيث لا تحس بالأثر فيها. فكل هذا ممكن، إذ الحكم الثابت لبعض الأشياء، ليس يلزم أن يثبت للكل.
Seventh Proof: They said: The perceptive powers that operate through bodily instruments suffer fatigue from continuous work by persisting in perception, because continuous movement corrupts the temperament of bodies and thus tires them. Likewise, strong and clear perceptible things weaken them and perhaps corrupt them, so that after them they do not perceive what is fainter and weaker, like a loud sound for hearing and a great light for sight—for they may corrupt or prevent, after them, the perception of a faint sound and fine visible things. Rather, one who tastes an intense sweetness does not feel a lesser sweetness after it. But the case of the rational power is the opposite: continuous contemplation of intelligibles does not tire it, and perception of clear necessary truths strengthens it for perceiving subtle theoretical truths and does not weaken it. If at times fatigue occurs, that is due to its use of the imaginative power and its reliance on it; thus the instrument of the imaginative power weakens and does not serve the intellect. This is of the same pattern as before. So we say: It is not unlikely that bodily senses differ in these matters; what is established for some does not have to hold for others. Rather, it is not unlikely that bodies vary: some are weakened by a type of motion, while others are strengthened by a type of motion and it does not weaken them, even if it affects them. Then there may be a cause that renews their strength, such that one does not feel the effect. All this is possible, for a judgment established for some things does not necessarily hold for all.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the seventh proof of the philosophers for the soul's independence from the body. The philosophers argue that bodily senses tire from continuous use and intense stimuli, but the rational faculty is strengthened by such activity. This difference, they claim, shows the soul is not a body. Ghazali refutes this by saying it is not unlikely that different bodies have different reactions; some motions strengthen rather than weaken. The judgment about one type of body does not apply to all. Therefore, the proof is not conclusive.

Key Terms
قوى مدركة
آلات جسمانية
كلال
قوة عقلية
قوة خيالية
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect's immunity to fatigue proves it is a non-bodily substance.
Correction: Ghazali shows that bodies can vary in their response to motion, so the difference does not prove the intellect is non-bodily.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ghazali's refutation of the seventh proof?
A) The rational faculty is actually a bodily power.
B) The differences between senses and intellect prove the soul is immaterial.
C) It is possible for bodies to differ, so the rule for one does not apply to all.
D) Fatigue is always caused by the imaginative power.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the proof by arguing that different bodies can have different properties, so the intellect's behavior does not prove it is non-bodily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you dismissed someone else's argument because you assumed 'what is true for me must be true for them.' Where in your life are you making the same mistake the philosophers made—generalizing from one type of body or experience to all? What would it take to apply Ghazali's principle of variation to your own judgments?
?
The philosophers used the intellect's strength as 'proof' of something non-material. Are there areas of your life where you mistake a difference in degree for a difference in kind? How can you guard against drawing overconfident conclusions from limited evidence—like the philosophers did?

Eighth Proof: Intellect and Body After Forty
Philosophers present eighth proof; Ghazali's response begins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' eighth proof for the soul's independence and Ghazali's initial objection to it.

Core Idea
Intellect strengthens when body weakens—but does that prove independence?

The philosophers argue that after age forty, bodily powers decline while the intellect grows stronger, suggesting the soul's subsistence is independent of the body. Ghazali begins his response by questioning whether this correlation suffices as proof. This passage presents the proof and the start of his critique.

The Logical Error of the 'Exact Consequent' Exception
Ghazali presents a conditional argument about the rational faculty and the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the exception of the exact consequent does not yield a valid inference, and how this relates to the dependence of the rational faculty on the body.

Core Idea
Not every conditional's consequent can be used to infer the antecedent – especially when the consequent is only sometimes true.

Ghazali is engaged in a logical refutation of the philosophers who claim the rational faculty is dependent on the body. He carefully analyzes a conditional argument: if the rational faculty is in the body, then bodily weakness would weaken it necessarily. Since we observe that the intellect is not always weakened, the consequent appears impossible. But Ghazali points out a subtle logical flaw in this reasoning. Let's examine his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts mid-sentence? The phrase 'كونه قائماً بالبدن' likely continues a previous thought.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the conditional: 'If the rational faculty is in the body (mugaddam), then bodily weakness weakens it in every case (tali).' Note how the argument uses the impossibility of the consequent to infer the impossibility of the antecedent.
This is a classic modus tollens form: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
3
Grasp the 'exception of the exact consequent'
Ghazali says this exception does not yield a valid inference. The 'exact consequent' likely refers to the claim 'weakness in every case.' The exception would be 'not in every case.' Inferring the antecedent's impossibility from that is what he critiques.
4
See Ghazali's correction
Ghazali then clarifies: if the consequent exists only in some states (i.e., sometimes the intellect is weakened, sometimes not), then the antecedent does not necessarily follow. This is a refinement distinguishing necessary from contingent consequences.
The careful wording 'في بعض الأحوال' is key – the consequent is not universally false, just not always true.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This logical point undermines a key philosophical argument for the soul's dependence on the body. Reflect on how precise reasoning is essential in kalam and falsafa debates.
Ghazali's logical training here shows his deep engagement with Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه قائماً بالبدن، فإن استثناء » عين التالي « لا ينتج، فإنا نقول : إن كانت القوة العقلية قائمة بالبدن، فيضعفها ضعف البدن بكل حال، والتالى محال، فالمقدم محال، وإذا قلنا التالي موجود في بعض الأحوال فلا يلزم أن يكون المقدم موجوداً
...its being subsistent in the body, for the exception of the 'exact consequent' does not yield. For we say: If the rational faculty is subsistent in the body, then the weakness of the body would weaken it in every case, and the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. And if we say the consequent exists in some states, it does not necessitate that the antecedent exists.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument critiquing an inference about the rational faculty's dependence on the body. The author states that the 'exception of the exact consequent' does not produce a valid inference. He considers the conditional: 'If the rational faculty is in the body, then bodily weakness weakens it in every case.' Since the consequent (that it is always weakened) is impossible (because we observe cases where the intellect is not weakened), one might conclude that the antecedent (that it is in the body) is also impossible. However, the author refines this: if the consequent is only sometimes true, it does not logically follow that the antecedent is true. This is a careful logical point distinguishing between necessary and contingent consequences.

Key Terms
القوة العقلية
المقدم
التالي
عين التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the consequent (Q) is sometimes true, then the antecedent (P) must be true.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the existence of Q in some cases does not necessitate the existence of P. This is the point of the exception of the exact consequent.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the exception of the exact consequent?
A) It produces a valid inference from consequent to antecedent.
B) It does not produce a valid inference.
C) It is the only valid logical form.
D) It proves the rational faculty is in the body.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the 'exception of the exact consequent' is invalid because a consequent that is only sometimes true does not force the antecedent to be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read claims from scholars or philosophers, do you assume their conditionals are necessary or do you test them? This lesson shows one hidden fallacy—where in your own reasoning might you be committing 'exception of the exact consequent' without realizing it?
?
Think of a time you assumed that because a consequence does not always occur, the cause must be absent. Could that assumption have led you to reject a truth about Allah's attributes or actions? How will you guard against this fallacy in your pursuit of knowledge?
?
Ghazali exposes a subtle logical error. Are you willing to examine your most cherished beliefs with this same rigorous logic, even if it makes you uncomfortable? Or do you prefer the comfort of familiar inferences, regardless of their validity?

The Objection from Variation of Powers
An objection argues that the causes of variation in powers are too many for certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that the many causes of variation in human powers undermine the claim of certain knowledge independent of the body.

Core Idea
Variation in bodily powers shows that certain knowledge cannot be based on empirical observation alone.

This passage presents an objection to the claim that bodily states do not affect intellectual knowledge. The objector points out that the senses and intellect vary in strength due to numerous causes, making it impossible to build reliable knowledge without appealing to habitual patterns. This challenges the idea that the soul's knowledge is independent of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the full English translation. Get a sense of the argument before analyzing.
Notice the repetitive structure of examples.
2
Identify the examples of variation
The objector gives several examples: powers strengthening at different ages, smell vs. sight, differences among animals. List them and note how they support the objection.
All examples show that bodily conditions cause variation.
3
Understand the conclusion about certainty
The objector concludes that without referring to habitual patterns (مجارى العادات), one cannot build reliable knowledge because possibilities are unlimited. See why this undermines the previous argument.
This is the crucial move: certainty requires ruling out unlimited possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض أن نقول : نقصان القوى وزيادتها لها أسباب كثيرة لا تنحصر، فقد يقوى بعض القوى، فى ابتداء العمر، وبعضها فى الوسط، وبعضها في الآخر، وأمر العقل أيضاً كذلك، فلا يبقى إلا أن يدعى الغالب . ولا بعد فى أن يختلف الشم والبصر، فى أن الشم يقوى بعد الأربعين، والبصر يضعف، وإن تساويا في كونهما حالين في الجسم، كما تتفاوت هذه القوى في الحيوانات، فيقوى الشم فى بعضها، والسمع في بعضها، والبصر في بعضها، لاختلاف فى أمزجتها لا يمكن الوقوف على ضبطه. فلا يبعد أن يكون مزاج الآلات أيضاً يختلف، في حق الأشخاص، وفى حق الأحوال، ويكون أحد الأسباب فى سبق الضعف إلى البصر، دون العقل، أن البصر أقدم، فإنه مبصر في أول فطرته، ولا يتم عقله إلا بعد خمس عشرة سنة، أو زيادة، على ما يشاهد اختلاف الناس فيه، حتى قيل : إن الشيب إلى شعر الرأس، أسبق منه إلى شعر اللحية، لأن شعر الرأس أقدم . فهذه الأسباب إن خاض الخائض فيها، ولم يرد هذه الأمور إلى مجارى العادات، فلا يمكن أن يبنى عليها علماً موثوقاً به، لأن جهات الاحتمال يد به القوى أو تضعف، لا تنحصر، فلا يورث شيء من ذلك يقيناً .
And the objection is that we say: The decrease and increase of powers have many causes that are not limited. For some powers may strengthen at the beginning of life, others in the middle, others at the end, and the matter of the intellect is likewise. So nothing remains except to claim the predominant. And there is no improbability in the difference between smell and sight, in that smell strengthens after forty while sight weakens, even though both are states in the body. Similarly, these powers vary among animals: smell is strong in some, hearing in others, sight in others, due to differences in their temperaments that cannot be precisely determined. So it is not improbable that the temperament of instruments also differs with respect to persons and states, and one of the reasons for weakness preceding sight before the intellect is that sight is older, for it sees from the first of its natural constitution, whereas the intellect is not completed until after fifteen years or more, as observed in the variation among people, to the extent that it is said that grayness reaches the hair of the head before the hair of the beard because the head's hair is older. So if one delves into these causes and does not refer these matters to the course of habit, one cannot build upon them reliable knowledge, because the possibilities of why powers strengthen or weaken are not limited, and none of that yields certainty.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is an objection to the previous argument that bodily illness does not affect knowledge. The objector argues that the increase and decrease of powers (senses, intellect) have many causes that cannot be enumerated. He gives examples: some powers strengthen at different ages; smell strengthens after forty while sight weakens, even though both are bodily states; animals differ in their powers due to temperament. He even notes that sight weakens before intellect because sight is active from birth while intellect matures later. The objector concludes that if one does not refer these variations to the 'course of habit' (regular empirical patterns), one cannot build reliable knowledge, because the possibilities for why powers strengthen or weaken are unlimited, and thus no certainty arises. This objection challenges the proof from recovery by asserting that bodily states can affect the powers in complex ways, undermining the claim that knowledge is independent.

The many causes of variation in bodily powers prevent us from achieving certain knowledge through empirical observation, unless we rely on habitual regularities.

Ghazali's Two Objections to the Soul's Immortality
Ghazali enumerates two objections to the philosophers' proof of the soul's immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two objections against the philosophers' proof of the soul's immortality: the challenge to the premise that the soul is not a state in a body, and the argument that the soul's relationship with the body implies its origination with the body.

Core Idea
Ghazali attacks the philosophers' proof by questioning its premise and showing that the soul's link to the body prevents its pre-existence.

In this passage, Ghazali presents two powerful objections to the philosophers' argument for the soul's immortality. The first directly challenges the foundational premise that the soul is not a state in a body. The second goes deeper, showing that even if the soul is immaterial, its relationship with the body and the problem of multiple souls arising from one pre-existent soul undermine the philosophers' position. These objections strike at the heart of the rationalist proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that Ghazali uses a dialectical style, presenting objections in a structured manner.
2
Identify the two objections
Look for the words 'الأول' and 'الثاني' to mark the two distinct objections. Notice how each objection is introduced and developed.
The first objection is brief; the second is more elaborate.
3
Understand the first objection
The first objection challenges the premise that the soul does not die because it is not a state in a body. This premise was debated in the previous issue, so it is not granted.
This is a dialectical move: the argument rests on an unproven premise.
4
Examine the second objection
The second objection argues that the soul has a relationship with the body and only originates with it. It then uses the impossibility of a single pre-existent soul becoming multiple to support this.
Pay attention to the sub-argument: if souls were one, their knowledge would be shared; therefore they must have originated with bodies.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these objections undermine the philosophers' proof. They show that the soul's independence is not absolute and that its origin is tied to the body.
This connects to broader debates about the soul's nature and immortality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الأول : أنه بناء على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن ، لأنها ليست حالة في جسم ، وهو بناء على المسألة الأولى ، فقد لا نسلم ذلك الثاني : أنه مع أنها لا تحل البدن عندهم ، فلها علاقة بالبدن ، حتى لم تحدث إلا بحدوث البدن ، وهذا ما اختاره ابن سينا والمحققون منهم وأنكروا على أفلاطن قوله : إن النفس قديمة ، ويعرض لها الاشتغال بالأبدان ، بمسلك برهاني محقق أن النفوس قبل الأبدان ، إن كانت واحدة ، فكيف انقسمت ؟ ! وهو وما لا حجم له ولا مقدار ، لا يعقل انقسامه ، وإن زعم أنها لم تنقسم فهو محال ، إذ يعلم ضرورة أن نفس زيد ، غير نفس عمرو ، ولو كانت واحدة ، لكانت معلومات زيد ، معلومة لعمرو ، فإن العلم من صفات ذات النفس ، وصفات الذات تدخل مع الذات فى كل إضافة ، وإن كانت النفوس متكثرة فماذا تكثرت ؟! ولم تتكثر بالمواد ، ولا بالأماكن ، ولا بالأزمنة
And the objection to it is from several aspects. The first: that it is based on the fact that the soul does not die with the death of the body, because it is not a state in a body, and that is based on the previous issue. So perhaps we do not concede that. The second: that even if, according to them, it does not reside in the body, it has a relationship with the body, such that it only originated with the origination of the body. This is what Ibn Sina and the investigators among them chose, and they denied Plato's statement that the soul is eternal and it becomes occupied with bodies, using a verified demonstrative method: that if souls before bodies were one, how did they become multiple? And that which has no size and no quantity cannot be conceived to divide. And if one claims that they did not become multiple, it is impossible, for it is known necessarily that the soul of Zayd is other than the soul of 'Amr. And if they were one, then the known things of Zayd would be known to 'Amr, for knowledge is among the attributes of the essence of the soul, and attributes of the essence enter with the essence in every relation. And if the souls were multiple, then what caused the multiplicity? And they did not become multiple by matters, places, or times.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's objections to the philosophers' proof for the soul's immortality. The first objection is a direct challenge to the premise: the entire proof rests on the claim that the soul does not die with the body because it is not a state (hāl) in a body. But this premise itself was the subject of the previous issue (issue 18), and it may not be granted. In other words, the very foundation of the argument is debatable. The second objection goes deeper. Even if the soul does not reside in the body, it still has a relationship with the body—so much so that it only originated with the origination of the body. This is the position of Avicenna (Ibn Sina) and other leading philosophers, who rejected Plato's view that souls are pre-eternal and merely become occupied with bodies. They provide a demonstrative proof: If souls existed as one before bodies, how did they become multiple? That which has no size or quantity cannot be divided. If one claims they did not become multiple but remain one, that is impossible because we know necessarily that Zayd's soul is different from 'Amr's soul. If they were one, then Zayd's knowledge would be 'Amr's knowledge, since knowledge is an attribute of the soul's essence and follows it in every relation. If souls were multiple prior to bodies, what caused the multiplicity? It cannot be matter, place, or time, as souls are immaterial. Thus, the objection shows that the soul's origin is tied to the body, undermining the philosophers' argument for absolute independence.

Key Terms
اعتراض
علاقة
حدوث
قديم
انقسام
صفات الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers believe the soul pre-exists the body.
Correction: The passage shows that Ibn Sina and other leading philosophers reject Plato's view and argue the soul originates with the body.

Checkpoint

A) The soul is not a substance but an accident.
B) The soul has a relationship with the body and originates with it, as shown by the impossibility of a single pre-existent soul becoming many.
C) The philosophers cannot prove the soul's existence after death.
D) The soul is material and thus perishable.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objections show that the philosophers' proof for the soul's immortality rests on unproven premises and fails to account for the soul's relationship with the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul originates with your body, then your entire existence is a gift that began at a specific moment. Does your daily life reflect that you are a creature with a beginning, or do you live as if you have always been and will always be without accountability?
?
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' confident proof for the soul's immortality is fragile. Yet you probably act as if your soul is immortal. What would it mean for your choices today if you truly doubted that?
?
The argument that souls cannot be multiple before bodies proves your soul is uniquely yours, created for you alone. Do you treat your soul as a sacred trust from Allah, or do you neglect it by following the masses in spiritual complacency?

Ghazali refutes annihilation by opposite and by power
Ghazali refutes two false claims about the annihilation of substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that substances cannot be annihilated by an opposite or by divine power, and how this relates to his broader critique of the philosophers.

Core Idea
Substances have no opposite; non-existence is not a thing that power can produce.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' claims about annihilation. In this passage, he refutes two specific proposals for how substances might cease to exist: by the action of an opposite, or by the direct exercise of power. Both, he argues, are conceptually impossible. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Ghazali's two refutations.
Pay attention to the terms 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' ('arad).
2
Identify the two false claims
Note the two claims Ghazali refutes: annihilation by opposite and annihilation by power.
3
Understand the first refutation
Why does Ghazali say annihilation by opposite fails for substances? Focus on the concept of substrate.
Only accidents in a substrate can be annihilated by an opposite.
4
Understand the second refutation
Why does Ghazali reject annihilation by power? Focus on the nature of non-existence.
Non-existence is not a thing that power can act upon.
5
Connect to broader context
How does this passage tie into the earlier discussion on the eternity of the world?
Ghazali explicitly references that discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباطل أن يقال : إنها تنعدم بالضد ، إذ الجواهر لا ضد لها ، ولذلك لا ينعدم فى العالم إلا الأعراض والصور المتعاقبة على الأشياء ، إذ تنعدم صورة المائية بضدها ، وهى صورة الهوائية ، والمادة التي هي المحل ، لا تنعدم قط ، وكل جوهر ليس فى محل ، فلا يتصور عدمه بالضد ، إذ لا ضد لما ليس فى محل ، فإن الأضداد هي المتعاقبة على محل واحد وباطل أن يقال : تفنى بالقدرة ، إذ العدم ليس شيئاً حتى يتصور وقوعه بالقدرة ، وهذا عين ما ذكروه في مسألة أبدية العالم ، وقد قررناه وتكلمنا عليه
And it is false to say: they become non-existent by the opposite, for substances (jawahir) have no opposite, and therefore nothing becomes non-existent in the world except accidents (a‘raḍ) and forms (ṣuwar) that succeed each other upon things, for the form of wateriness becomes non-existent by its opposite, which is the form of airiness, and the matter (al-māddah) which is the substrate does not become non-existent at all, and every substance that is not in a substrate cannot be conceived to become non-existent by the opposite, for there is no opposite for what is not in a substrate, for opposites are those that succeed each other upon a single substrate. And it is false to say: they perish by power (al-qudrah), for non-existence (al-‘adam) is not a thing such that its occurrence by power can be conceived, and this is exactly what they mentioned in the issue of the eternity of the world, and we have established it and spoken about it.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage refutes two false claims about how substances (jawāhir) could be annihilated. The first claim is that substances become non-existent by the opposite. Ghazali argues that substances have no opposite; only accidents and forms that succeed each other in a substrate can be annihilated by their opposites (e.g., water becomes air). The matter itself does not become non-existent. A substance not in a substrate cannot have an opposite because opposites succeed each other in a single substrate. The second claim is that substances perish by power (qudrah). Ghazali says this is false because non-existence is not a thing that power can bring about. He notes this was already discussed in the context of the world's eternity.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض ('arad)
ضد (ḍidd)
قدرة (qudra)
عدم ('adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think substances can be annihilated by their opposites like accidents.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that only accidents in a substrate can be annihilated by an opposite; substances not in a substrate have no opposite.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say a substance not in a substrate cannot be annihilated by its opposite?
A) Because all substances have opposites.
B) Because it has no opposite, as opposites succeed each other in a single substrate.
C) Because power cannot create non-existence.
D) Because only God can annihilate substances.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that substances cannot be annihilated by an opposite or by power, reinforcing his critique of the philosophers' cosmology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the permanence of substances and the transient nature of accidents, how does that affect your understanding of the world's stability? Are you more attached to the underlying substance of your life (your soul) or to the changing forms (wealth, health)? What does Ghazali's argument reveal about where you should anchor your trust?
?
Ghazali states that non-existence is not a thing that power can bring about. How does this challenge your daily assumptions about what you can control? Do you sometimes act as if you can 'cancel' or 'annihilate' something in your life (a habit, a relationship) by sheer will, forgetting that non-existence is not an object of power? How should this reshape your reliance on Allah's power?
?
If substances cannot be annihilated by an opposite, what does that say about the permanence of your own soul? Are you preparing for its eternal continuation, or are you neglecting it because you think it might simply cease? What specific change in your priorities does this refutation demand?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Why a Simple Thing Cannot Have Potentiality While Existing
Ghazali refutes the notion of potential existence in a simple thing during actual existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that potentiality and actuality cannot coexist in a simple thing, and how this refutes the philosophers' notion of potentiality in simple substances.

Core Idea
Potentiality and actuality are contradictory and cannot coexist in a simple thing.

Ghazali presents a tight argument against the philosophers who claim that simple things like matter or soul can have potentiality for existence or non-existence while actually existing. He uses the example of sight to show that potentiality is in a different subject, not in the act itself. In this passage, he demonstrates the contradiction of affirming potentiality in a thing that already exists, and connects this to his earlier arguments on the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the philosophers' claim
The philosophers hold that simple things (like matter or soul) have potentiality for existence and non-existence even while they actually exist. Ghazali begins by unpacking what this claim entails.
2
Follow Ghazali's logical steps
If something can non-exist, it is not necessary of existence, so it is possible of existence. Potentiality of existence means possibility of existence. Thus, affirming potentiality for non-existence implies affirming potentiality for existence.
This leads to the key contradiction.
3
Understand the example of sight
Ghazali uses sight: the potentiality of sight is in the eye, not in the act of seeing itself. This shows that potentiality and actuality are in different subjects. If they were in the same subject, a thing would be both potential and actual simultaneously, which is contradictory.
The eye is the substrate of potentiality, not the act.
4
Apply to simple things
For a simple thing (like a substance), if it has potentiality for non-existence before non-existence, that would imply it also has potentiality for existence while existing. But that is impossible because potentiality and actuality cannot coexist.
Thus, a simple thing cannot have potentiality while it exists.
5
Connect to broader argument
Ghazali links this to his earlier refutation of the philosophers' view that matter and elements can neither originate nor cease to exist. The source of their error is imagining possibility as something that requires a substrate.
This argument applies to both material and soul substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم قبل العدم حاصلا لذلك الشيء ، وهو المراد بالقوة ، فيكون إمكان الوجود أيضاً ، حاصلا لذلك الشيء ، فإن ما أمكن عدمه ، فليس واجب الوجود ، فهو ممكن الوجود ، ولا نعنى بقوة الوجود إلا إمكان الوجود ، فيؤدى إلى أن يجتمع في الشيء الواحد ، قوة وجود نفسه ، مع حصول وجوده بالفعل ، ويكون وجوده بالفعل ، هو عين قوة الوجود ، وقد بينا أن قوة الإبصار تكون في العين ، التي غير الإبصار ، ولا تكون فى نفس الإبصار ، إذ يؤدى إلى أن يكون الشيء بالقوة والفعل ، وهما متناقضان ، بل مهما كان الشيء بالقوة ، لم يكن بالفعل ومهما كان بالفعل لم يكن بالقوة ، وفى إثبات قوة العدم للبسيط قبل العدم إثبات لقوة الوجود ، فى حال الوجود وهو محال. وهذا نفسه الذى قررناه لهم في مصيرهم إلى استحالة حدوث المادة والعناصر واستحالة عدمها، في مسألتى أزلية العالم وأبديته. ومنشأ التلبيس وضعهم الإمكان وضعاً مستدعياً محلا يقوم به، وقد تكلمنا عليه بما فيه مقنع فلا نعيده، فإن يكون المتكلم فيه جوهر مادة أو جوهر نفس.
Non-existence before non-existence is something that belongs to that thing, and that is what is meant by potentiality. So possibility of existence also belongs to that thing. For whatever can non-exist is not necessary of existence, so it is possible of existence. And we mean by potentiality of existence nothing but possibility of existence. So it leads to that in one thing there should combine: the potentiality of its own existence along with the actual attainment of its existence, and its actual existence would be identical to the potentiality of existence. And we have shown that the potentiality of sight is in the eye, which is other than sight, and it is not in sight itself, because that would lead to a thing being both potential and actual, which are contradictory. Rather, whenever a thing is potential, it is not actual; and whenever it is actual, it is not potential. And in affirming potentiality of non-existence for a simple thing before non-existence, there is an affirmation of potentiality of existence in the state of existence, which is impossible. This itself is what we established for them regarding their conclusion about the impossibility of the origination of matter and the elements and the impossibility of their non-existence, in the two questions of the world's pre-eternity and its post-eternity. And the source of the deception is their positing of possibility as something that demands a substrate in which it inheres. And we have spoken on it with what is convincing, so we do not repeat it. For whether the subject is a material substance or a soul substance.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the philosophers' notion that simple things (like matter or soul) can have potentiality (quwwa) for existence or non-existence while actually existing. Ghazali argues that if something is possible of non-existence (i.e., not necessary), then it is possible of existence. He uses the example of sight: the potential to see is in the eye, not in the act of seeing itself, because a thing cannot be both potential and actual at the same time. In a simple thing (like a substance), if it had potentiality for non-existence before its non-existence, that would imply it also had potentiality for existence while existing, which is contradictory. He connects this to his earlier arguments about the world's eternity: the philosophers mistakenly think possibility requires a substrate (like matter) to inhere in, but this leads to absurdity. The argument applies equally to material substances and soul substances.

Key Terms
قوة (potentiality)
فعل (actuality)
بسيط (simple thing)
إمكان (possibility)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
ممكن الوجود (possible of existence)
جوهر مادة (material substance)
جوهر نفس (soul substance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali denies any potentiality in created things altogether.
Correction: Ghazali only denies potentiality in simple things during actual existence; potentiality can exist in composite things or in a different substrate.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why cannot a simple thing have potentiality for existence while it actually exists?
A) Because potentiality and actuality are two different times
B) Because potentiality and actuality are contradictory and cannot coexist in the same thing
C) Because simple things have no potentiality at all
D) Because potentiality is only in material substances

Key Takeaway
A simple thing that actually exists cannot have potentiality for that same existence, because potentiality implies non-actuality, and the two are mutually exclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence has no inherent potentiality but is entirely dependent on God's will, how does that change the way you view your own independence and choices? Will you today consciously acknowledge that every moment you exist is a direct act of God's preservation?
?
You often think of yourself as having potential—to become better, to change, to achieve. But this lesson says a simple thing cannot have potential while actual. In what area of your life are you relying on your own inherent potential rather than on God's direct creation of your actions? What would it mean to truly surrender that area to Him?
?
The philosophers' error was to think that possibility inheres in a substrate. In your own life, where do you mistakenly think that your possibilities are stored inside you, rather than being granted by God each moment? How can you train yourself to live in constant awareness of your utter contingency?

The Soul's Attachment and Temporary Punishment
Ghazali explains how bodily habits cause temporary suffering after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the soul's acquired bodily dispositions cause temporary suffering after death, not eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Punishment for bodily habits is accidental and fades, leading to eventual purification.

Ghazali explains the soul's relationship with the body as one of natural longing to manage it, not physical subsistence. When the soul departs, the bodily dispositions it acquired cause pain, but because they are accidental, this pain diminishes and eventually vanishes, allowing the soul to reach its perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, focusing on the key terms for the soul-body relation.
Note the terms: جوهر النفس, الشوق الجبلى, الهيأة البدنية.
2
Identify the argument structure
Ghazali argues that the bodily disposition is contrary to the soul's essence, causing pain, but this pain is accidental and fades when the actions reinforcing it cease.
This leads to the conclusion that punishment is not eternal.
3
Reflect on the implication
Contrast this with views that all sin leads to eternal punishment. Ghazali emphasizes the temporality of punishment for acquired habits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل عن جهته ، فإن التوسط يسلب عنها الطرفين دائماً ، ثم جوهر النفس ، إنما كان البدن هو الذي يغمره ويلهيه ، ويغفله عن الشوق الذى يخصه ، وعن طلب الكمال الذى له ، وعن الشعور بلذة الكمال ، إن حصل له ، أو الشعور بألم النقصان ، إن قصر عنه ، لا بأن النفس منطبعة في البدن ، ومنغمسة فيه ، ولكن بالعلاقة التى كانت بينهما وهى الشوق الجبلى ، إلى تدبيره ، والاشتغال بآثاره ، و بما يورده عليه من عوارضه ، وبما يتقرر فيه ، من ملكات مبدؤها البدن . فإذا فارق ، وفيه المملكة الحاصلة بسبب الاتصال به ، كان قريب الشبه من حاله وهو فيه ، ، فها ينقص من ذلك ، تزول غفلته عن حركة الشوق الذي له إلى كماله ، و بما يبقى منه معه ، يكون محجوباً عن الاتصال الصرف بمحل سعادته ، ويحدث هناك من الحركات المشوشة ، ما يعظم أذاه ثم إن تلك الهيأة البدنية ، مضادة لجوهرها مؤذنة هما ، وإنما كان يلهيها عنها أيضاً البدن وتمام انغماسها فيه ، فإذا فارقت النفس البدن أحست بتلك المضادة العظيمة ، وتأذت بها أذى عظيما ، لكن هذا الأذى وهذا الألم ، ليس لأمر لازم ، بل لأمر عارض غريب ، والعارض الغريب لا يدوم ولا يبقى ، فيزول ويبطل مع ترك الأفعال ، التي كانت تثبت تلك الهيأة بتكرارها ، فيلزم إذن أن تكون العقوبة ، التي بحسب ذلك ، غير خالدة ، بل تزول وتنمحى قليلا قليلا ، حتى تزكو النفس وتبلغ السعادة التي تخصها . وأما النفوس البله ، التى لم تكتسب الشوق ، فإنها إذا ما فارقت البدن ، وكانت غير مكتسبة للهيئات البدنية الردية ، صارت إلى سعة من رحمة الله ، وذوع من الراحة ؛ وإن كانت مكتسبة للهيئات البدنية الردية ، وليس عندها هيأة غير ذلك ، ولا معنى يضاده وينافيه ، فتكون لا محالة ممنوة بشوقها إلى مقتضاها ، فتتعذب عذاباً شديداً ، بفقد البدن ، ومقتضيات البدن ، من غير أن يحصل المشتاق إليه ، لأن آلة ذلك قد بطلت ، وخلق التعلق بالبدن قد بقى . ويشبه أيضاً أن يكون ما قاله بعض العلماء حقاً أن هذه الأنفس ، إن كانت زكية ، وفارقت البدن ، وقد رسخ فيها نحو من الاعتقاد فى العاقبة ، التى تكون لأمثالهم ، على ما يمكن أن يخاطب به العامة ، وتصور في أنفسهم من ذلك ، فإنهم إذا فارقوا الأبدان ، ولم يكن لهم معنى جاذب إلى الجهة ، التى فوقهم ، لإتمام كمال ، فتسعد تلك السعادة ، ولا شوق كمال ، فتشقى تلك الشقاوة ، بل جميع هيئاتهم النفسانية متوجهة نحو الأسفل ، منجذبة إلى الأجسام ، ولا منع في المواد السماوية ، عن أن تكون موضوعة لفعل نفس فيها ، قالوا فإنها تتخيل ما كانت اعتقدته ، من الأحوال الأخروية ، وتكون الآلة التي يمكنها بها التخيل ، شيئاً من الأجرام السماوية فتشاهد ما قيل لها في الدنيا ، من أحوال القبر ، والبعث ، والخيرات الأخروية ، وتكون الأنفس جميع
Rather, from its side, the middle state always strips away the two extremes. Then the essence of the soul: the body is what overwhelms it, distracts it, and makes it heedless of the longing (al-shawq) specific to it, and of seeking its perfection, and of feeling the pleasure of perfection if it attains it, or feeling the pain of deficiency if it falls short—not by the soul being impressed in the body and immersed in it, but by the relation between them, which is the natural longing to manage the body, and occupation with its effects, and with what befalls it of its accidents, and with what becomes established in it of faculties originating from the body. So when it departs, having the acquired kingdom (al-mamlaka) due to connection with it, it is similar to its state while in the body. Then from this, its heedlessness of the movement of longing towards its perfection disappears, and by what remains with it, it is veiled from pure connection to its place of happiness, and there occur from it disturbing movements that cause great harm. Then that bodily disposition (al-hayʼa al-badaniyya) is opposed to its essence, signifying [؟], and the body and its complete immersion in it used to distract it from it. So when the soul departs from the body, it feels that great opposition and suffers great harm. But this harm and this pain are not for a necessary matter, but for an accidental, foreign matter. The accidental foreign matter does not endure nor persist, so it vanishes and ceases with the cessation of the actions that used to confirm that disposition by repetition. It follows, then, that the punishment corresponding to that is not eternal, but rather vanishes and is gradually effaced until the soul becomes pure and attains its specific happiness. As for the simple souls (al-nufūs al-bulh) that did not acquire longing, when they depart the body, if they are not acquisitive of base bodily dispositions, they go into vastness of God's mercy and abundance of comfort; but if they are acquisitive of base bodily dispositions and have no other disposition to oppose it, then they are inevitably afflicted by their longing for its object, suffering severe pain from the loss of the body and its requirements without attaining what is longed for, because the instrument for that has perished while the natural attachment to the body remains. And it also resembles that what some scholars said is true: that these souls, if they are pure and depart the body, and there has become rooted in them some belief about the outcome that will befall their likes, according to what the common people can be addressed with, and they have formed a conception of that in themselves—then when they depart the bodies, and they have no meaning attracting them to the direction above them for the completion of perfection, they do not attain that happiness, and they do not have a longing for perfection so they suffer that misery; rather all their psychic dispositions are directed downwards, attracted to bodies. And there is no prevention in the celestial matters from being a subject for the action of a soul in them. They said that they then imagine what they used to believe about the states of the afterlife, and the instrument by which they can imagine is something from the celestial bodies, so they witness what was said to them in this world about the states of the grave, resurrection, and afterlife goods. And the souls all...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion of the soul's attachment to the body and the consequences after death. It begins by stating that the middle state (tawassuṭ) always removes both extremes, then explores how the soul's essence is affected by the body. The body overwhelms the soul and distracts it from its proper longing (shawq) for perfection. The relation is one of natural longing to manage the body, not subsistence in it. When the soul separates, it carries with it acquired dispositions (malakāt) from the body, which veil it from pure connection to its happiness and cause disturbing movements. These bodily dispositions are contrary to the soul's essence and cause intense pain after separation, but this pain is accidental (ʿāriḍ gharīb) and temporary, fading as the actions that reinforced it cease. Thus punishment is not eternal but gradually effaced. Next, Ghazali discusses simple souls (al-nufūs al-bulh) that did not acquire longing: if they have no bad dispositions, they enjoy God's mercy; if they have bad dispositions without any opposing good, they suffer intense pain from unfulfilled longing for the body. Finally, he reports a theory that some souls, if they had rooted beliefs taught to the common people, may experience those beliefs as imaginal realities through celestial bodies, witnessing what they were told about the grave and resurrection. This reflects the concept of imaginal resurrection (mithālī) found in Islamic philosophy. The text cuts off at 'جميع'.

Key Terms
جوهر النفس
الشوق الجبلى
الهيأة البدنية
الملكة
العقوبة غير خالدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the punishment for acquired bodily dispositions not eternal?
A) Because God is merciful and forgives all sins.
B) Because the pain is caused by accidental foreign dispositions that fade when the actions cease.
C) Because the soul is immortal and cannot be punished.
D) Because the body is not resurrected.

Key Takeaway
Punishment for acquired bodily habits is temporary because it stems from accidental dispositions that fade with the cessation of reinforcing actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific bodily habit or attachment has become so ingrained in you that you would feel its loss as a real part of your identity? If that habit is accidental, what would it take for you to begin loosening its hold on your soul today?
?
When you consider the pain you may feel after death from opposition to your essence, does that change how you view the small, repeated choices you make each day? What is one action you can stop doing right now to weaken a harmful disposition?
?
If the purification of your soul depends on the fading of accidental dispositions, what are you actively doing to hasten that purification while still in this life? Are you merely avoiding sins, or are you deliberately cultivating the longing for your true perfection?

The Fate of Simple Souls
Ghazali discusses two types of simple souls and their post-mortem experiences.

Learning Objective
This lesson establishes Ghazali's classification of simple souls (al-nufūs al-bulh) and their fate after death, based on whether they have acquired base bodily dispositions.

Core Idea
Simple souls without bad habits enjoy mercy; those with bad habits suffer unfulfilled longing.

Ghazali turns to souls that never acquired the longing for perfection—the simple ones. Their fate depends on whether they cultivated bad bodily habits. If they did, they experience intense pain from missing the body; if not, they find peace in God's mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on simple souls
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the two categories of simple souls.
Note the term 'النفوس البله'.
2
Identify the two categories
Ghazali distinguishes between souls that are not acquisitive of base dispositions and those that are. The former receive mercy, the latter suffer from longing for the body.
The key difference is the presence or absence of bad bodily habits.
3
Understand the mechanism of suffering
Souls with bad habits suffer because they long for the body but lack the instruments to satisfy that longing, as the body is gone.
This suffering is intense but not necessarily eternal—Ghazali implies it could fade, though he doesn't explicitly say here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل عن جهته ، فإن التوسط يسلب عنها الطرفين دائماً ، ثم جوهر النفس ، إنما كان البدن هو الذي يغمره ويلهيه ، ويغفله عن الشوق الذى يخصه ، وعن طلب الكمال الذى له ، وعن الشعور بلذة الكمال ، إن حصل له ، أو الشعور بألم النقصان ، إن قصر عنه ، لا بأن النفس منطبعة في البدن ، ومنغمسة فيه ، ولكن بالعلاقة التى كانت بينهما وهى الشوق الجبلى ، إلى تدبيره ، والاشتغال بآثاره ، و بما يورده عليه من عوارضه ، وبما يتقرر فيه ، من ملكات مبدؤها البدن . فإذا فارق ، وفيه المملكة الحاصلة بسبب الاتصال به ، كان قريب الشبه من حاله وهو فيه ، ، فها ينقص من ذلك ، تزول غفلته عن حركة الشوق الذي له إلى كماله ، و بما يبقى منه معه ، يكون محجوباً عن الاتصال الصرف بمحل سعادته ، ويحدث هناك من الحركات المشوشة ، ما يعظم أذاه ثم إن تلك الهيأة البدنية ، مضادة لجوهرها مؤذنة هما ، وإنما كان يلهيها عنها أيضاً البدن وتمام انغماسها فيه ، فإذا فارقت النفس البدن أحست بتلك المضادة العظيمة ، وتأذت بها أذى عظيما ، لكن هذا الأذى وهذا الألم ، ليس لأمر لازم ، بل لأمر عارض غريب ، والعارض الغريب لا يدوم ولا يبقى ، فيزول ويبطل مع ترك الأفعال ، التي كانت تثبت تلك الهيأة بتكرارها ، فيلزم إذن أن تكون العقوبة ، التي بحسب ذلك ، غير خالدة ، بل تزول وتنمحى قليلا قليلا ، حتى تزكو النفس وتبلغ السعادة التي تخصها . وأما النفوس البله ، التى لم تكتسب الشوق ، فإنها إذا ما فارقت البدن ، وكانت غير مكتسبة للهيئات البدنية الردية ، صارت إلى سعة من رحمة الله ، وذوع من الراحة ؛ وإن كانت مكتسبة للهيئات البدنية الردية ، وليس عندها هيأة غير ذلك ، ولا معنى يضاده وينافيه ، فتكون لا محالة ممنوة بشوقها إلى مقتضاها ، فتتعذب عذاباً شديداً ، بفقد البدن ، ومقتضيات البدن ، من غير أن يحصل المشتاق إليه ، لأن آلة ذلك قد بطلت ، وخلق التعلق بالبدن قد بقى . ويشبه أيضاً أن يكون ما قاله بعض العلماء حقاً أن هذه الأنفس ، إن كانت زكية ، وفارقت البدن ، وقد رسخ فيها نحو من الاعتقاد فى العاقبة ، التى تكون لأمثالهم ، على ما يمكن أن يخاطب به العامة ، وتصور في أنفسهم من ذلك ، فإنهم إذا فارقوا الأبدان ، ولم يكن لهم معنى جاذب إلى الجهة ، التى فوقهم ، لإتمام كمال ، فتسعد تلك السعادة ، ولا شوق كمال ، فتشقى تلك الشقاوة ، بل جميع هيئاتهم النفسانية متوجهة نحو الأسفل ، منجذبة إلى الأجسام ، ولا منع في المواد السماوية ، عن أن تكون موضوعة لفعل نفس فيها ، قالوا فإنها تتخيل ما كانت اعتقدته ، من الأحوال الأخروية ، وتكون الآلة التي يمكنها بها التخيل ، شيئاً من الأجرام السماوية فتشاهد ما قيل لها في الدنيا ، من أحوال القبر ، والبعث ، والخيرات الأخروية ، وتكون الأنفس جميع
Rather, from its side, the middle state always strips away the two extremes. Then the essence of the soul: the body is what overwhelms it, distracts it, and makes it heedless of the longing (al-shawq) specific to it, and of seeking its perfection, and of feeling the pleasure of perfection if it attains it, or feeling the pain of deficiency if it falls short—not by the soul being impressed in the body and immersed in it, but by the relation between them, which is the natural longing to manage the body, and occupation with its effects, and with what befalls it of its accidents, and with what becomes established in it of faculties originating from the body. So when it departs, having the acquired kingdom (al-mamlaka) due to connection with it, it is similar to its state while in the body. Then from this, its heedlessness of the movement of longing towards its perfection disappears, and by what remains with it, it is veiled from pure connection to its place of happiness, and there occur from it disturbing movements that cause great harm. Then that bodily disposition (al-hayʼa al-badaniyya) is opposed to its essence, signifying [؟], and the body and its complete immersion in it used to distract it from it. So when the soul departs from the body, it feels that great opposition and suffers great harm. But this harm and this pain are not for a necessary matter, but for an accidental, foreign matter. The accidental foreign matter does not endure nor persist, so it vanishes and ceases with the cessation of the actions that used to confirm that disposition by repetition. It follows, then, that the punishment corresponding to that is not eternal, but rather vanishes and is gradually effaced until the soul becomes pure and attains its specific happiness. As for the simple souls (al-nufūs al-bulh) that did not acquire longing, when they depart the body, if they are not acquisitive of base bodily dispositions, they go into vastness of God's mercy and abundance of comfort; but if they are acquisitive of base bodily dispositions and have no other disposition to oppose it, then they are inevitably afflicted by their longing for its object, suffering severe pain from the loss of the body and its requirements without attaining what is longed for, because the instrument for that has perished while the natural attachment to the body remains. And it also resembles that what some scholars said is true: that these souls, if they are pure and depart the body, and there has become rooted in them some belief about the outcome that will befall their likes, according to what the common people can be addressed with, and they have formed a conception of that in themselves—then when they depart the bodies, and they have no meaning attracting them to the direction above them for the completion of perfection, they do not attain that happiness, and they do not have a longing for perfection so they suffer that misery; rather all their psychic dispositions are directed downwards, attracted to bodies. And there is no prevention in the celestial matters from being a subject for the action of a soul in them. They said that they then imagine what they used to believe about the states of the afterlife, and the instrument by which they can imagine is something from the celestial bodies, so they witness what was said to them in this world about the states of the grave, resurrection, and afterlife goods. And the souls all...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion of the soul's attachment to the body and the consequences after death. It begins by stating that the middle state (tawassuṭ) always removes both extremes, then explores how the soul's essence is affected by the body. The body overwhelms the soul and distracts it from its proper longing (shawq) for perfection. The relation is one of natural longing to manage the body, not subsistence in it. When the soul separates, it carries with it acquired dispositions (malakāt) from the body, which veil it from pure connection to its happiness and cause disturbing movements. These bodily dispositions are contrary to the soul's essence and cause intense pain after separation, but this pain is accidental (ʿāriḍ gharīb) and temporary, fading as the actions that reinforced it cease. Thus punishment is not eternal but gradually effaced. Next, Ghazali discusses simple souls (al-nufūs al-bulh) that did not acquire longing: if they have no bad dispositions, they enjoy God's mercy; if they have bad dispositions without any opposing good, they suffer intense pain from unfulfilled longing for the body. Finally, he reports a theory that some souls, if they had rooted beliefs taught to the common people, may experience those beliefs as imaginal realities through celestial bodies, witnessing what they were told about the grave and resurrection. This reflects the concept of imaginal resurrection (mithālī) found in Islamic philosophy. The text cuts off at 'جميع'.

Key Terms
النفوس البله
الهيئات البدنية الردية
الشوق
الخلق التعلق بالبدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All souls experience the same afterlife regardless of their dispositions.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between simple souls based on their acquired bodily habits, leading to different fates: mercy for the pure, suffering for the corrupted.

Checkpoint
What happens to simple souls that have acquired base bodily dispositions?
A) They enjoy God's mercy and comfort.
B) They are annihilated.
C) They suffer intense pain from longing for the body.
D) They are reborn in new bodies.

Key Takeaway
Simple souls without longing for perfection face either mercy or suffering based on whether they acquired base bodily dispositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What base bodily disposition do you cling to most tightly—the one you would feel lost without if it were taken away? How does Ghazali's description of unfulfilled longing challenge your attachment to that disposition?
?
If you were to die right now with only your current habits, would you be among those who enter vast mercy or among those suffering from unfulfilled longing? What specific habit would tip the scale, and what will you do about it today?
?
You have natural attachments to your body, its comforts, and its desires. Are you cultivating any opposing disposition that could save you from the pain of losing them? If not, what is one small change you can make this week to begin building that opposing disposition?

Imaginal Punishment and Celestial Bodies
Ghazali reports a theory of imaginal punishment via celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's account of how souls with rooted beliefs about the afterlife experience those beliefs through imaginal forms in celestial bodies.

Core Idea
Souls with common beliefs may witness their imagined afterlife through celestial instruments.

Ghazali presents a theory from some scholars that souls, if they hold certain beliefs taught to the common people, can experience those beliefs as imaginal realities after death. Using celestial bodies as instruments, they witness what they were told about the grave and resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on imaginal punishment
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the theory and its conditions.
The text is cut off at the end, so the full theory may continue.
2
Identify the condition for this experience
The soul must have rooted beliefs (rasakha fīhā iʿtiqād) about the afterlife, and must be directed downward (towards bodies) rather than upward.
This explains why common believers experience punishment.
3
Understand the mechanism
Celestial bodies serve as instruments for imagination, allowing the soul to 'see' what it believed.
This reflects the concept of imaginal resurrection (mithālī).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
What downward-oriented attachments or habits (e.g., love of status, comfort, or material things) are currently pulling your psychic dispositions away from longing for higher perfection? What concrete step can you take today to reorient your inner compass?
?
This theory suggests that our deepest beliefs become the lens through which we experience the next world. Are you actively nurturing a vivid, actionable faith in the unseen, or are you coasting on vague hopes? If you were to die tonight, what would your soul's 'celestial instrument' project?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Impossibility of Return to the Same Body
Ghazali states the basic impossibility of returning the soul to the identical body due to decomposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that returning the soul to the exact same earthly body is impossible due to the body's decomposition and dispersal.

Core Idea
Dead bodies decompose and mix with the world, making re-identification impossible.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' theory of bodily resurrection. He presents the second division: the idea that the soul survives and returns to its identical body. He asserts that even if such a return were conceivable, it would be a return to administering a body that has long since decomposed and dispersed into the environment. This lesson examines Ghazali's initial argument from material dispersal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the key terms: معاد, محال, استحالة.
2
Understand the claim
Ghazali says the return is 'محال' (impossible) because the body turns into dust, is eaten, and becomes mixed with air and water.
He refers to natural processes of decomposition.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does mixture with the world make extraction unlikely? Think about how particles disperse and become part of other things.
This is a physical impossibility argument, not a theological one.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This is the first step in a series of arguments. Ghazali will later consider objections appealing to divine power.
Remember that philosophers relied on God's omnipotence to reassemble the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني، وهو تقدير بقاء النفس وردها إلى ذلك البدن بعينه، فهو لو تصور، لكان معاداً، أي عوداً إلى تدبير البدن بعد مفارقته، لكنه محال، إذ بدن الميت يستحيل تراباً، أو تأكله الذيدان والطيور، ويستحيل دماء وبخاراً وهواء، ويمتزج بهواء العالم، وبخاره، ومائه، امتزاجاً يبعد انتزاعه، واستخلاصه. ولكن إن فرض ذلك اتكالا على قدرة الله تعالى، فلا يخلو، إما أن يجمع الأجزاء التي مات عليها فقط، فينبغي أن يعاد الأقطع، ومجذوع والأذن، وناقص الأعضاء، كما كان، وهذا مستقبح لا سيما في أهل الجنة، الأنف وهم الذين خلقوا ناقصين، في ابتداء الفطرة، فإعادتهم إلى ما كانوا عليه، من الهزال عند الموت في غاية النكال هذا إن اقتصر على جمع الأجزاء الموجودة عند الموت وإن جمع جميع أجزائه التي كانت موجودة في جميع عمره، فهو محال من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الإنسان إذا تغذى بلحم إنسان، وقد جرت العادة به في بعض البلاد، ويكثر وقوعه في أوقات القحط. فيتعذر حشرهما جميعاً، لأن مادة واحدة كانت بدناً للمأكول، وصارت بالغذاء بدناً للأكل ولا يمكن رد نفسين إلى بدن واحد. والثاني: أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد، كبداً وقلباً، ويداً، ورجلا، فإنه ثبت بالصناعة الطبية، أن الأجزاء العضوية، يتغذى بعضها، بفضلة غذاء البعض، فيتغذى الكبد بأجزاء القلب، وكذلك سائر الأعضاء، فنفرض أجزاء معينة، قد كانت مادة الجملة من الأعضاء، فإلى أي عضو تعاد؟!! بل لا يحتاج في تقرير الاستحالة الأولى إلى أكل الناس الناس فإنك إذا تأملت ظاهر التربة المعمورة، علمت بعد طول الزمان، أن ترابها جثث الموتى، قد تتربت وزرع فيها وغرس، وصارت حبا وفاكهة، وتناولتها الدواب، فصارت لحماً، وتناولناها فصارت أبداناً لنا، فما من مادة يشار إليها، إلا وقد كانت بدناً لأناس كثيرين، فاستحالت وصارت تراباً، ثم نباتاً ثم لحماً، ثم حيواناً. بل يلزم منه محال ثالث، وهو أن النفوس المفارقة للأبدان، غير متناهية، والأبدان أجسام متناهية، فلا تفى المواد، التي كانت مواد الإنسان، بأنفس الناس كلهم، بل تضيق عنهم.
As for the second division, which is the supposition of the soul's survival and its return to that identical body—if it were conceivable, it would be a return (ma‘ād), i.e., a return to administering the body after separation, but it is impossible. For the dead body turns into dust, or is eaten by worms and birds, and turns into blood, vapor, and air, and mixes with the air of the world, its vapor, and its water, in a mixture that makes its extraction and recovery unlikely. But if that is supposed relying on the power of Allah the Exalted, then it is either that He gathers only the parts present at death, so the amputee, the one with a cut ear, and the one with missing limbs should be returned as they were, and this is repugnant, especially in the case of the people of Paradise—the nose?—[and those who were created deficient from the beginning of their natural constitution; returning them to what they were, of emaciation at death, is an extreme punishment]. This is if He restricts Himself to gathering the parts present at death. And if He gathers all his parts that existed throughout his entire life, it is impossible in two ways. One of them: that if a human feeds on the flesh of a human—as is customary in some lands and occurs frequently in times of famine—then the resurrection of both becomes impossible, because one matter was the body of the eaten and became through nutrition the body of the eater, and it is impossible to return two souls to one body. And the second: that a single part—like liver, heart, hand, foot—must be returned, for it is established by medical science that organic parts are nourished by the surplus food of other parts, so the liver is nourished by parts of the heart, and likewise the rest of the organs. So if we suppose specific parts that were the material of the whole aggregate of organs, to which organ should they be returned?! Rather, one does not even need cannibalism to establish the first impossibility. For if you reflect on the surface of the inhabited earth, you know that after a long time its soil consists of the corpses of the dead, which have become dust, then crops have been sown and planted in it, and become grain and fruit, then animals have eaten them and they become flesh, then we have eaten them and they become our bodies. So there is no material that can be pointed to that has not been the body of many people, so it has transformed and become dust, then plants, then flesh, then animals. Rather, a third impossibility follows from it: that the souls separate from bodies are infinite, while bodies are finite bodies, so the materials that were the materials of humans are insufficient for all the souls of people, nay, they fall short of them.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that the soul can return to its exact earthly body. Ghazali classifies this as the second division (the first being the denial of bodily resurrection altogether). He first states that even if conceivable, the return would be a resumption of the soul's administration of the body, but it is impossible because the body decomposes and its matter disperses into the environment. Even if we appeal to God's power, there are two options: gathering only the parts at death—which would lead to absurd and unseemly resurrections of amputees and the emaciated, especially in Paradise—or gathering all parts the person ever had, which is impossible for two reasons: first, due to cannibalism, where one body's matter becomes another's, making it impossible to assign the same particles to two souls; second, due to inter-organ nourishment within a single body, where a part like the liver may have been composed partly of material that was once part of the heart, so to which organ should that particle be returned? Ghazali then strengthens the argument by noting that even without cannibalism, normal biological cycles of growth, decay, and consumption mean that every particle of earth has been part of countless human bodies, making it impossible to allocate finite matter to infinite souls (a third impossibility). Thus, the return of the soul to its identical body is impossible in multiple ways.

Key Terms
معاد (return)
محال (impossible)
استحالة (transformation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can simply reassemble the exact same atoms from anywhere.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the atoms have dispersed and mixed with other matter, making it impossible to identify which atoms originally belonged to the body.

Checkpoint
What reason does Ghazali give for the impossibility of returning the soul to the identical body?
A) The soul cannot be separated from the body
B) The body decomposes and mixes with the environment
C) God's power is limited
D) The philosophers deny the soul's existence

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first argument against bodily re-identification is based on the material dispersion of the corpse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your body will one day decompose and its particles mix with the earth, what does that reveal about your attachment to your physical appearance or material possessions? Will you hold on to them or let go for the sake of what is eternal?
?
Al-Ghazali argues that returning to the identical body is impossible due to natural decomposition. If God instead recreates you in a new body, how does that reshape your understanding of your personal identity? Who are you truly, beyond your physical form?
?
The philosophers trusted in a naturalistic model of resurrection, but Ghazali shows it fails because the original particles are dispersed. Do you place your hope in God's unlimited power to recreate you, or do you secretly rely on some natural continuity? What is the foundation of your certainty about the afterlife?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Absurdity of Restoring Only the Parts at Death
Ghazali presents and refutes the first option under divine power: gathering only the parts at death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that if God only gathered the parts present at death, the resurrection would be repugnant and unseemly, especially for the people of Paradise.

Core Idea
Restoring only the parts at death would bring back amputees and the emaciated as they were.

Ghazali now considers the possibility that God's power could reassemble the body, but He might choose to gather only the parts that existed at the moment of death. This would lead to absurd consequences: amputees would be returned without limbs, and those who died emaciated would be resurrected in that state. Ghazali points out this is particularly repugnant for the people of Paradise, who are supposed to be in a state of perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for this lesson
Focus on the phrase 'إما أن يجمع الأجزاء التي مات عليها فقط' and the examples that follow.
2
Identify the consequences
Ghazali lists: the amputee, the one with a cut ear, and the one with missing limbs would be returned as they were. Also, those emaciated at death would be resurrected emaciated.
This is considered 'مستقبح' (repugnant) especially for Paradise.
3
Understand the theological issue
The philosophers claim that the resurrection body is the same earthly body. If only the parts at death are gathered, then imperfections like missing limbs or emaciation would persist, which contradicts the perfection of Paradise.
Ghazali is using a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Appreciate the rhetorical force
By mentioning 'أهل الجنة' (people of Paradise), Ghazali highlights how absurd this scenario is for the final abode of bliss.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that the soul can return to its exact earthly body. Ghazali classifies this as the second division (the first being the denial of bodily resurrection altogether). Key Terms
أجزاء (parts)
الأقطع (amputee)
مجذوع (one with cut ear)
هزال (emaciation)
غاية النكال (extreme punishment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could simply choose which parts to gather to make a perfect body.
Correction: Ghazali argues that if He gathers only the parts at death, He must gather exactly those, leading to imperfections.

Checkpoint
What is one repugnant consequence of gathering only the parts present at death?
A) The body would be too large
B) Amputees would be returned without limbs
C) The soul would not fit
D) The body would be too young

Key Takeaway
Returning only the parts present at death would restore imperfections, contradicting the perfection of the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your past sins or imperfections permanently define you, as if God could only resurrect you with those defects? How does Ghazali's argument challenge that mindset? What would it mean to trust that God can restore you completely, without any trace of your shortcomings?
?
The passage calls returning the emaciated to their state of emaciation 'extreme punishment.' In what areas of your life are you holding onto spiritual 'emaciation'—perhaps patterns of sin or self-doubt—as if they are inescapable parts of your identity? Will you let God strip those away and give you a new creation?
?
Ghazali's reductio shows that a wrong assumption leads to a repugnant conclusion. What false belief about yourself—maybe that you are unworthy of complete restoration—are you clinging to? Confront that belief today and ask: Do I really believe God can resurrect me without even a memory of my deficiency?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Impossibility of Gathering All Parts: Cannibalism and Inter-Organ Nourishment
Ghazali presents two impossibilities for gathering all parts from a person's life, then broadens the argument to natural material cycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two arguments against gathering all parts from a person's entire life: the problem of shared matter due to cannibalism and the problem of inter-organ nourishment within a single body.

Core Idea
Shared matter between bodies and within the same body makes re-assignment impossible.

Having shown the absurdity of gathering only the parts at death, Ghazali now considers the alternative: gathering all the parts that ever belonged to a person throughout their entire life. He presents two impossibilities. The first concerns cannibalism, where one person's body becomes another's through consumption, making it impossible to assign the same particles to two souls. The second is a more subtle point: within a single body, organs are nourished by the 'surplus' of other organs, so the same particle may have been part of multiple organs; to which should it be returned? He then strengthens the first impossibility by noting that even without cannibalism, normal biological cycles ensure that every particle has been part of many bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for this lesson
Pay attention to the two impossibilities: 'أحدهما: أن الإنسان إذا تغذى بلحم إنسان...' and 'والثاني: أنه يجب أن يعاد جزء واحد...'. Also note the final paragraph starting 'بل لا يحتاج...'.
2
Understand the cannibalism argument
If one human eats another, the same matter becomes part of both bodies. At resurrection, that matter cannot be returned to both simultaneously.
This shows a logical contradiction.
3
Understand the inter-organ argument
Medical science shows that the liver is nourished by parts of the heart, etc. So the same particle may have been in multiple organs. To which organ should it be returned?
This highlights the difficulty of identifying the 'original' placement.
4
Reflect on the broader cycle
Ghazali then argues that even without cannibalism, the natural cycle of earth, plants, animals, and humans means every particle has been part of many bodies over time.
This strengthens the cannibalism argument to a universal problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
