The Possibility of a Second Creation
Ghazali argues for the possibility of a second creation after annihilation, dependent on two issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali’s argument for the possibility of a second order of creation after the annihilation of the universe, and the two foundational issues on which it depends.

Core Idea
A new creation is possible only if the world is temporal and habits can be broken.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a concise theological argument about what God can do after the end of the world. He imagines a scenario where God exists alone, then creates the world, then initiates a second order (Paradise), and finally annihilates everything except Himself. But he immediately notes that revelation tells us this second order is eternal. Ghazali then identifies two key issues that underpin the entire discussion: the temporal creation of the world and the breaking of habitual causal patterns. As you read, notice how Ghazali ties his argument to previously established conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Pay attention to the sequence of events Ghazali describes: existence of God alone, creation of the world, second order, annihilation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two foundational issues
After stating the scenario, Ghazali says the whole issue depends on two questions. Locate them in the text: ḥudūth al-ʿālam (temporal creation) and kharq al-ʿādāt (breaking of habits).
These are the two issues he says he has already addressed.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Think about why these two issues are necessary for the possibility of a second creation. Without temporal creation, the world would be eternal and could not be annihilated. Without breaking habits, God could not create a new order different from the observed one.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation
Notice that Ghazali says the scenario is possible 'were it not that the Law has come' stating the eternity of Paradise and Hell. This shows that reason alone permits it, but revelation specifies the actual outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون الله تعالى موجوداً ، ولا عالم . ثم يخلق العالم ، على النظام المشاهد ثم يستأنف نظاماً ثانياً ، وهو الموعود في الجنة . ثم يعدم الكل ، حتى لا يبقى إلا الله تعالى ، وهو ممكن ، لولا أن الشرع قد ورد ، بأن الثواب والعقاب ، والجنة والنار ، لا آخر لها وهذه المسألة كيفما دارت تنبنى على مسألتين : إحداهما : حدوث العالم ، وجواز حصول حادث من قديم . والثانية : خرق العادات ، بخلق المسببات، دون الأسباب ، أو إحداث أسباب ، على منهج آخر غير معتاد ، وقد فرغنا من المسألتين جميعاً ، والله أعلم .
That God Almighty exists, and no world. Then He creates the world, according to the observed order. Then He initiates a second order, which is what is promised in Paradise. Then He annihilates everything, so that nothing remains except God Almighty. And that is possible, were it not that the Law has come that reward and punishment, and Paradise and Hell, have no end. This issue, however it is considered, depends on two questions: the first is the temporal creation of the world and the permissibility of an originated thing arising from the Eternal; and the second is the breaking of habits, by creating effects without causes, or by bringing about causes on another unconventional path. And we have finished both questions entirely, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument about the possibility of a second order of creation following the annihilation of the universe. The author states that it is possible for God to exist alone without a world, then create the world as we observe it, then initiate a second order (the promised Paradise), and finally annihilate everything except God. However, he adds that revelation (shar‘) has come to inform us that reward, punishment, Paradise, and Hell have no end, implying that the second order is eternal. The author then notes that this entire issue hinges on two fundamental questions: (1) the temporal creation (ḥudūth) of the world and whether an originated event can come from an eternal being, and (2) the breaking of habitual causal patterns (kharq al-‘ādāt) – that God can create effects without their usual causes or produce causes in an unconventional manner. The author concludes by stating that he has already addressed both questions, indicating that the argument rests on previously established premises.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
خرق العادات
النظام المشاهد
الموعود في الجنة
حادث من قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this passage endorses the eternity of the world or denies the possibility of a new creation.
Correction: The passage actually affirms that a new creation is rationally possible, but revelation specifies its eternal nature. It relies on the temporal creation of the world (not its eternity) and the breaking of habitual causal patterns.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which two issues does the possibility of a second order of creation depend?
A) The existence of God and the creation of angels
B) The temporal creation of the world and the breaking of habits
C) The eternity of the world and the breaking of laws
D) Reward and punishment and the hereafter

Key Takeaway
A second creation after annihilation is rationally possible because the world is temporally created and God can break habitual causal patterns, though revelation tells us this second order is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly allow revelation to override your rational assumptions about what is possible and impossible regarding the afterlife? Or do you secretly trust your own speculation more than what Allah has informed you?
?
When you imagine Paradise, do you find yourself focusing on your own desires, or do you submit to the fact that it is a 'second order' beyond your current comprehension? How does that submission affect your hope today?
?
If you knew that this world would be annihilated and replaced with something eternal, would you cling less to worldly possessions and relationships? What one attachment can you loosen today in light of this truth?

The First Proof and Ghazali's Objection on Eternal Will
Ghazali presents the first proof and his first objection via eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection to the philosophers' proof of the world's eternity, specifically how an eternal will can be attached to a temporal event.

Core Idea
An eternal will can choose a moment without absurdity.

This lesson introduces the first proof of the philosophers, who argue that a temporal event cannot come from an eternal cause. Ghazali objects by proposing that an eternal will can be attached to the existence of the world at a specific time, avoiding the philosophers' claimed impossibilities. This objection forms the core of the debate on divine will and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and objection
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: first the philosophers' proof, then Ghazali's first objection.
2
Identify the key claim
What is the philosophers' main claim about eternal cause and temporal effect? How does Ghazali challenge it?
Focus on the phrase 'إرادة قديمة' (eternal will).
3
Understand the logical move
Ghazali says that an eternal will can be attached to a specific time without contradiction. Why does this refute the philosophers' proof?
4
Consider the implications
If the world is created at a time by an eternal will, then the world is not eternal. This is Ghazali's main point.
This is the first of two objections mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الأول : مؤداه استحالة صدور حادث من قديم . اعتراض الغزالى على دليل الفلاسفة الأول بوجهين الوجه الأول : بيان إمكان أن لا يكون العالم قديماً ؛ بأن تتعلق إرادة قديمة بأن يوجد العالم فى الوقت الذى وجد فيه ، وعلى هذا الفرض لا يلزم شيء من المحالات التي ذكرها الفلاسفة في دليلهم الأول . دعوى الفلاسفة أن الفصل بين العلة التامة ومعلومها محال . رد الغزالى على الفلاسفة بأن دعوى استحالة تعلق إرادة قديمة بإحداث شيء ، بديهية أو نظرية ؟ فإن كانت بديهية ، فلم خالفكم فيها خصومكم وهم أكثر عدداً منكم ؟ وإن كانت نظرية فما دليلكم . رد الفلاسفة بأن استحالة تعلق إرادة قديمة بإحداث شيء معلوم لهم بالضرورة . أجاب الغزالي بأن من علوم الفلاسفة ما هو بالنسبة لطائفة الغزالي في غاية الاستحالة ، مثل اتحاد العقل والعاقل والمعقول . عود الغزالى إلى مسألة قدم العالم، وترتيب محالات على القول بقدم العالم
The first proof: its implication is the impossibility of an originated event proceeding from an eternal being. Ghazali's objection to the first proof of the philosophers in two ways: the first way: demonstration of the possibility that the world is not eternal; by an eternal will being attached to the existence of the world at the time it existed, and on this assumption none of the impossibilities mentioned by the philosophers in their first proof follow. The philosophers' claim that separation between complete cause and its effect is impossible. Ghazali's reply to philosophers: is the claim of impossibility of an eternal will being attached to bringing about something, intuitive or theoretical? If intuitive, then why do your opponents differ from you, and they are more numerous? If theoretical, what is your proof? The philosophers replied that the impossibility of an eternal will being attached to bringing about something is known necessarily to them. Ghazali replied that some of the philosophers' sciences are, for the group of Ghazali, utterly impossible, such as the unity of intellect, intelligent, and intelligible. Ghazali returns to the question of the world's eternity, and arranging impossibilities on the statement of the world's eternity.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the philosophers, which asserts the impossibility of a temporal event arising from an eternal being. Ghazali objects in two ways; here he presents the first objection. He argues that it is possible for an eternal will to be attached to the existence of the world at a specific time, and this does not lead to any absurdities claimed by the philosophers. He then challenges the philosophers' claim that separation between complete cause and effect is impossible, questioning whether their claim is intuitive or theoretical. If intuitive, why do many opponents disagree? If theoretical, what is their proof? The philosophers respond that it is known necessarily to them. Ghazali counters that some of their sciences are for him utterly impossible, using the example of the unity of intellect, intelligent, and intelligible. He then returns to the problem of the world's eternity and lists absurdities on that view.

Correction: Here he only denies the necessary link from eternal cause to temporal effect, allowing an eternal will to intervene.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What would it mean for you to truly live as if God's will is free, sovereign, and not constrained by your expectations?

Can you name one specific event you have struggled to accept, and right now, before God, consciously hand it back to His eternal will that chose that time for a reason?

The Debate on Intuition and Theory in Ghazali's Objection
Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim and they respond, with Ghazali's counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's challenge to the philosophers' claim that the impossibility of eternal will is intuitive, and the philosophers' response, as well as Ghazali's counterexample.

Core Idea
Is the impossibility of eternal will intuitive or theoretical? The debate reveals deeper epistemological divides.

After presenting his first objection, Ghazali challenges the philosophers' underlying claim that separation between a complete cause and its effect is impossible. He questions whether this is known intuitively or theoretically, setting up a debate on the nature of certainty and proof. The philosophers reply that it is necessary knowledge, but Ghazali counters with an example of their own doctrines that he finds impossible, such as the unity of intellect, intelligent, and intelligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
The philosophers claim that separation between a complete cause and its effect is impossible. Notice how this relates to their first proof.
2
Analyze Ghazali's challenge
Ghazali asks: is the impossibility intuitive or theoretical? If intuitive, why do so many disagree? If theoretical, what is your proof?
This forces the philosophers to justify their claim.
3
Examine the philosophers' reply
They say it is known necessarily to them. Ghazali then points out that some of their own doctrines (like the unity of intellect) are similarly impossible for him, showing that intuitive claims can be relative.
4
Reflect on the significance
This exchange reveals that what one group considers necessary another group may find impossible. This undermines the philosophers' proof.

The Impossibility of Infinite Cycles with Fractions and Parity
Ghazali questions how infinite cycles can have fractional parts and numerical parity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali's questions about fractional parts and numerical parity challenge the philosophers' notion of an actual infinite series of celestial cycles.

Core Idea
Infinite series cannot have fractions or parity—Ghazali's clever trap.

Ghazali presents a sharp rhetorical objection against the philosophers' claim of infinite past cycles of the celestial spheres. He points out that these cycles are said to have fractional components (a sixth, a quarter) and then asks about the numerical parity of the total count. If the cycles are infinite, how can they have parts? And what is the parity of an infinite number? These questions force the opponent into a logical dead end.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ghazali uses two separate but connected arguments: fractions and parity.
The passage is a single sentence with multiple questions.
2
Understand the fraction problem
The philosophers say past celestial cycles are infinite. Yet they assign fractions like a sixth and a quarter to these cycles. An infinite series cannot have fractional parts because fractions imply a finite whole.
A sixth of an infinite quantity is itself infinite, not a fraction.
3
Examine the parity dilemma
Ghazali then asks whether the total number of cycles is even, odd, both, or neither. Any answer leads to a contradiction for an actual infinite.
Even and odd are properties of finite numbers, not infinite ones.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical force
Ghazali is not stating a mathematical theorem; he is using common sense about numbers to expose the absurdity of an actual infinite. The opponent cannot answer without violating basic arithmetic.
This argument is part of a larger refutation of the philosophers' cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكيف تكون هناك دورات الفلك لا نهاية لها ، مع أن لها سدساً وربعاً وما تكونه أعداد هذه الدورات ؟ هل تكون شفعاً ؟ أم تكون وتراً ؟ أم تكون شفعاً و وتراً ؟ أم تكون لا شفعاً ولا وتراً ؟
How can there be cycles of the sphere that have no end, while they have a sixth and a quarter? And what are the numbers of these cycles? Are they even? Are they odd? Are they both even and odd? Or are they neither even nor odd?
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical objection against the philosophers' claim of infinite cycles of celestial motion. Ghazali points out that these cycles are said to have fractional parts (a sixth and a quarter), which is problematic for an infinite count. He then questions the numerical parity of the total number of cycles: whether the number is even, odd, both, or neither. This exposes an absurdity in the concept of an actual infinite.

Correction: Ghazali is deliberately using the philosophers' own assumptions: they treat the infinite cycles as a countable series (having fractions and parity) and he shows the contradictions that arise.

Answering the Dilemma of Pre-Creation Duration
Philosophers' dilemma on pre-creation duration and Ghazali's answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' dilemma about the duration before creation by asserting that time itself is created.

Core Idea
Time is created with the world—there is no 'before' creation.

Philosophers often posed a dilemma: either the duration before the world is finite, implying God began, or infinite, impossible to end. Ghazali cuts through this by denying the premise that time exists independently. In this passage, he delivers a concise response that time is created, a key move in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the two horns of the dilemma: finite duration implies God's beginning, infinite duration cannot end.
Notice how the dilemma forces a choice between two problematic options.
2
Identify Ghazali's answer
Ghazali states that duration and time are created. This means they do not exist prior to the world.
3
Analyze why the answer works
If time is created, the question of what happened 'before' time becomes meaningless. The dilemma presupposes an independent time container.
This shows Ghazali's strategy of attacking the philosophers' assumptions.
4
Connect to broader argument
Ghazali promises to return to this topic later, indicating its importance. Consider how this answer supports the doctrine of temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض الفلاسفة على القول بحدوث العالم : بأن المدة التي سبقت خلق العالم كانت متناهية ؟ أم غير متناهية ؟ إن قلتم متناهية ، كان لوجود الله أول وإن قلتم غير متناهية ، فكيف انقضى ما لا نهاية له ؟ الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض ، أن المدة والزمان مخلوقان ، وسيعود الغزالى إلى بحث المدة والزمان ، عند مناقشة دليلهم الثانى.
The philosophers' objection to the statement of the world's creation: Was the duration before the creation of the world finite or infinite? If you say finite, then God's existence had a beginning. If you say infinite, then how did that which has no end come to an end? The answer to this objection is that duration and time are created, and Ghazali will return to the investigation of duration and time when discussing their second proof.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a classic dilemma used by philosophers against the temporal creation of the world: either the duration before creation is finite (implying God began to exist) or infinite (impossible for an infinite period to end). Ghazali answers that both duration and time are themselves created, so the dilemma is based on a false presupposition. He promises to return to this topic later, showing that time is not an independent container but created with the world.

Ghazali's Second Objection: Distinguishing the Indistinguishable
Ghazali shows philosophers themselves must posit a distinguishing factor for the world's specific features.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali forces the philosophers to accept a distinguishing attribute by pointing to contingent features of the world that could have been otherwise according to their own causal framework.

Core Idea
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own examples to show they cannot avoid a divine distinguisher.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' denial of a distinguishing attribute (murajjih). He turns their own explanation of the world's specific features against them, arguing that since alternative configurations are equally possible yet one is actual, there must be a factor that tips the balance. This lesson unpacks that argument and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: a claim followed by examples.
2
Identify the central claim
The philosophers claim that a distinguishing attribute is unintelligible. Ghazali says they themselves rely on one. Find the sentence that states this.
Look for 'الفلاسفة أنفسهم ما استغنوا عن ترجيح الشيء عن مثله'
3
Examine the examples
Ghazali gives three examples: the world's specific forms, the position of the celestial poles, and the direction of motion. For each, note why it could have been otherwise yet produce the same effects.
The examples are in the latter half of the passage.
4
Reflect on the implication
If the philosophers must posit a distinguisher for these features, then their own denial of such an attribute is inconsistent. Consider how this strengthens Ghazali's overall argument for a voluntary agent.
This connects to the larger debate on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثانى : من رد الغزالي على ادعاء الفلاسفة أن وجود صفة من شأنها تمييز أحد الأمرين المتساويين على الآخر ، غير معقول ، ويقوم هذا الوجه على أن الفلاسفة أنفسهم ما استغنوا عن ترجيح الشيء عن مثله ؛ فإن العالم وجد عندهم عن السبب الموجب له على هيئات مخصوصة ، كان يمكن أن يوجد على خلافها ، فما الذي رجع هذه الهيئات على تلك ؟ وكذلك يلزمون بجهة الحركة .. وبتعيين موضع القطب في الحركة على المنطقة : أما موضع القطبين فكان يمكن أن يكون في أي نقطتين متقابلتين ، مع ترتب نفس الآثار . وأما جهة الحركة فكان يمكن أن تكون على العكس مع ترتب نفس الآثار.
The second aspect: from al-Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that the existence of an attribute whose function is to distinguish one of two equal things from the other is unintelligible. This aspect is based on the fact that the philosophers themselves have not dispensed with preferring a thing over its like; for the world came into existence according to them from the necessitating cause with specific forms, when it could have existed contrary to them. So what prefered these forms over those? And likewise they are compelled to [posit] the direction of motion... and the fixing of the position of the pole in motion on the sphere: As for the position of the two poles, it could have been in any two opposite points, while the same effects follow. And as for the direction of motion, it could have been the opposite while the same effects follow.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second objection: the philosophers themselves cannot avoid positing a factor that distinguishes one possibility from another. He argues that in their own system, the world's specific forms (such as the arrangement of the celestial spheres) are not necessitated by the cause alone, since alternative configurations could have produced the same effects. Thus, something must have selected these particular forms over others. He gives examples: the position of the celestial poles could be any opposite points, and the direction of rotation could be reversed, yet the same outcomes would occur. Therefore, the philosophers must admit an attribute that distinguishes between equals, refuting their own claim that such an attribute is unintelligible.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: He is using the philosophers' own causal framework to force them to posit a distinguishing attribute.

A) The existence of evil in the world.
B) The specific forms and motions of the world that could have been otherwise.
C) The inability of human reason to understand God's will.
D) The temporal nature of the world.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' own system requires a distinguishing attribute, undermining their denial of such a divine quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does that awareness reshape your sense of responsibility before Allah?

Al-Ghazali exposes the philosophers' hidden need for a distinguisher. Reflect on your own life: in what areas do you act as if there is no need for divine guidance because 'things just are the way they are'? Where have you unconsciously assumed that the status quo is the only possible reality, ignoring that Allah could have willed it differently?
?
The celestial poles and direction of motion could have been reversed yet effects remained the same. What aspects of your life—your habits, beliefs, or daily routines—could be entirely different but still lead to the same worldly outcomes? Are you clinging to them out of inertia rather than recognizing that Allah's choice for them is a sign of His specific will for you?

Second Objection: Philosophers Must Admit a First Event
Ghazali's second objection: philosophers must admit a temporal event from an eternal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali forces philosophers to concede that an eternal cause can produce temporal events, undermining their rejection of the first event.

Core Idea
If temporal events exist now, they come from an eternal cause—so why deny the first event?

This is a key moment in Ghazali's critique. He turns the philosophers' own admission against them: they agree that temporal events occur (like accidents) and that these events proceed from an eternal being (God). Yet they claim that the very first temporal event cannot arise from the eternal. Ghazali asks a simple, devastating question: what's the difference? This lesson unpacks that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali states his objection and anticipates the philosophers' reply.
The passage is short but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the philosophers' distinction
In the passage, the philosophers say they only reject the first temporal event from the eternal. Identify this distinction and think about why they might make it.
Think about the philosophical principle they rely on.
3
Understand Ghazali's retort
Ghazali asks 'What is the difference?' This is a powerful move. Consider why this question exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' position.
The burden is now on the philosophers to justify their arbitrary line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثاني : على أصل دليل الفلاسفة أن يقال : استبعدتم صدور حادث من قديم ، ولا بد لكم من القول به ؛ فإن في العالم حوادث بالاتفاق ، فهى صادرة عن قديم. إذا قال الفلاسفة نحن نستبعد فقط صدور حادث هو أول الحوادث ، من قديم . . . قيل لهم ما الفرق
The second objection: upon the basis of the philosophers' argument, it is said: you have deemed it improbable that a temporal event should proceed from the eternal, yet you must affirm it; for in the world there are temporal events by agreement, and they proceed from the eternal. If the philosophers say, 'We deem improbable only the emanation of a temporal event which is the first of temporal events from the eternal...' it is said to them, 'What is the difference?'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second objection to the philosophers' argument for the world's eternity. He argues that the philosophers themselves admit the existence of temporal events (accidents) in the world, and these events must originate from an eternal cause (God). If they claim that only the first temporal event is problematic, Ghazali asks for the difference between the first event and subsequent events. Implicitly, if a temporal event can proceed from the eternal after the world begins, there is no rational basis to reject the first event.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
صدور
أول الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies all causation.
Correction: Ghazali challenges the philosophers' arbitrary restriction on divine action, not causation itself.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Ghazali's second objection?
A) Philosophers cannot prove God's existence.
B) Philosophers accept temporal events from an eternal cause, so why reject the first?
C) The world is eternal like God.
D) Temporal events cannot come from anything eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second objection exposes the inconsistency in the philosophers' rejection of a first temporal event from an eternal cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you made an arbitrary distinction to avoid a difficult truth? Write down one specific example and commit to re-examining it today.
?
When you find yourself accepting some uncomfortable realities but rejecting others without reason, what does that reveal about your motives? Ask Allah to purify your heart from selective truth-seeking.
?
The philosophers had evidence but asked 'what is the difference?'—what difference do you fear admitting might change everything? Will you let that fear keep you from the full truth?

Second Objection: Must a Temporal Event Proceed from the Eternal?
Ghazali presents the second objection to the philosophers' denial of a first temporal event from the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the philosophers' denial of a first temporal event from the eternal is inconsistent with their acceptance of other temporal events.

Core Idea
If the eternal can produce any temporal event, why not the first?

Ghazali sharpens the attack on the philosophers' position. He points out that they cannot deny temporal events from the eternal altogether, because the world is full of them. Their only escape is to exclude the very first event, but this raises an obvious question: what makes the first event special? This lesson examines that inconsistency and the force of Ghazali's challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali frames the objection as a direct challenge to the philosophers' principle.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the inconsistency
Ghazali argues that the philosophers must admit temporal events from the eternal because they exist in the world. So their denial of only the first event is arbitrary. Identify this logical move in the passage.
3
Consider the philosophers' response
The philosophers attempt to limit their denial to the first event only. Ghazali quotes their response and then asks 'What is the difference?' Think about why this question is powerful.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if the eternal can cause a temporal effect after a period of non-causation, why can't it cause the very first event? This reveals a tension in the philosophers' concept of causality.
This objection sets up further discussions about the nature of divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثاني : على أصل دليل الفلاسفة أن يقال : استبعدتم صدور حادث من قديم ، ولا بد لكم من القول به ؛ فإن في العالم حوادث بالاتفاق ، فهى صادرة عن قديم إذا قال الفلاسفة نحن نستبعد فقط صدور حادث هو أول الحوادث ، من قديم . . . قيل لهم ما الفرق
The second objection: Regarding the fundamental proof of the philosophers, it should be said: You have deemed improbable the occurrence of a temporal event from an eternal being, yet you must necessarily affirm it; for in the world there are temporal events by agreement, so they proceed from an eternal being. When the philosophers say: 'We only deem improbable the occurrence of a temporal event that is the first of events from an eternal being...' It is said to them: 'What is the difference?'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the philosophers' argument that a first temporal event cannot proceed from an eternal being. The objector argues that since the world undeniably contains temporal events (such as movements and changes), these must originate from an eternal cause (God). The philosophers respond that they only deem improbable the very first event directly from the eternal, but they accept subsequent events through intermediaries. The objector then asks: 'What is the difference?' — implying that if the eternal can produce a temporal effect after an interval, why not the first event? This exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' position regarding the nature of divine causality.

Key Terms

Second Proof: The Dilemma of God's Priority
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second proof: dilemma of essential vs temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof for the eternity of the world, based on a dilemma about God's priority.

Core Idea
God's priority forces a choice: both eternal or impossible.

The philosophers present a powerful dilemma: either God is prior in essence or in time. Both paths are examined, and only one leads to a consistent conclusion. This passage is crucial for understanding the philosophical argument for the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Let the structure of the dilemma become clear.
2
Identify the two interpretations of priority
Notice that the passage begins with a shared premise and then splits into two scenarios: essential priority and temporal priority.
The philosophers use a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Understand the consequences of each
For essential priority, deduce that both must be eternal. For temporal priority, see why it leads to a contradiction.
The impossibility of temporal priority reinforces essential priority.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that the world is eternal, which is the philosophers' desired position.
This is not Ghazali's own view but a presentation of the opposing argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثان للفلاسفة في إثبات قدم العالم قال الفلاسفة : إن الله متقدم على العالم بالاتفاق ؛ فإن أريد أنه متقدم عليه بالذات لا بالزمان، لزم أن يكون الله والعالم إما قديمين أو حادثين ، وكونهما حادثين محال ، فثبت أن الله والعالم قديمان .. وهـو المطلوب وإن أريد أن الله متقدم على العالم الذى منه الزمان بالزمان ، لزم تقدم الزمان بالوجود على نفسه • وهو محال
A second proof for the philosophers in establishing the eternity of the world. The philosophers said: 'God is prior to the world by agreement. If what is meant is that He is prior to it in essence not in time, then it necessarily follows that God and the world are either both eternal or both temporal. Their being both temporal is impossible. So it is established that God and the world are both eternal. That is what is sought. And if what is meant is that God is prior to the world from which time proceeds, in time, then time's priority in existence over itself would be necessary, and that is impossible.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second proof for the eternity of the world, based on the universally accepted fact that God is prior to the world. The proof proceeds by analyzing two possible interpretations of this priority. First, if priority is understood as essential priority (prior in essence without time), then both God and the world share the same temporal status—either both are eternal or both are temporal. But the world cannot be temporal because it would then have a beginning, contradicting the philosophers' position. Therefore, both must be eternal. Second, if priority is understood as temporal priority (God existed before the world in time), then time must exist before the world, but since the world is said to be the source of time, time would have to precede itself, which is absurd. Thus, the only consistent interpretation is that God is prior in essence, and the world is eternal.

Key Terms
متقدم بالذات (prior in essence)
متقدم بالزمان (prior in time)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (temporal)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What conclusion do the philosophers draw from the dilemma about God's priority?
A) God and the world are both temporal.
B) God and the world are both eternal.
C) God is prior in time and the world is eternal.
D) Time is prior to God.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's priority must be essential, making both God and the world eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the philosophers' argument that God's priority must be essential, not temporal, does this change how you perceive your own prayers? Do you treat God's presence as merely above time, or do you feel His immediate response in your time-bound life?
?
The proof concludes that if God were temporally prior, time would have to precede itself—an impossibility. Have you ever lived as if God's guidance could be delayed, as if He is waiting in time? How can you today realign your sense of urgency with the reality that God is beyond time and always present?

Ghazali's Response: Time Is Created and God's Priority Without Time
Ghazali refutes the second proof by asserting time is created and explaining God's priority as existence alone without the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that time is created and that God's priority does not imply temporal precedence, and how he distinguishes between imagination and intellect.

Core Idea
God's priority is existence alone without the world, not existence in a prior time.

In this passage, Ghazali directly confronts the philosophers' claim that God's priority over the world implies the existence of time. He clarifies that the meaning of God being prior is simply that He existed alone without the world, and the world came into being later. The philosophers' objection, that this statement itself implies time, is shown to be a failure of imagination, not a rational necessity. Ghazali uses the example of the finitude of dimensions to illustrate how the intellect can accept what the imagination cannot picture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own rhythm and argumentation come through.
Notice the structure: claim, objection, counter-example.
2
Identify Ghazali's core claim
Find where Ghazali states that time is created and what he means by God's priority. Note that he defines priority as existence alone without the world.
3
Understand the distinction between imagination and intellect
Ghazali argues that the philosophers' objection arises from the limitation of imagination (وهم), not from rational intellect (عقل). The example of finite dimensions illustrates that intellect can accept what imagination cannot.
This is key to grasping Ghazali's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجيب الغزالى عن هذا بأن الزمان حادث ومخلوق . ومعنى كون الله متقدماً على العالم أن الله كان وحده موجوداً ، ولا عالم وجد العالم فهذا هو معنى حدوث العالم، ومعنى كون الله متقدماً عليه ، ثم فإن قيل من قبل الفلاسفة ، القول بوجود الزمان لازم فإن معنى قولنا : ... كان الله وحده موجوداً ، أن هناك زماناً كان الله فيه وحده موجوداً . قيل : إن هذا هو أتصور [؟] الوهم لا العقل ، وكثيراً ما يعجز الوهم عن أن يتصور الحقائق على ما هي عليه ، ألا ترى أنه قد ثبت تناهى الأبعاد وأنه ليس وراء العالم لا خلاء ولا ملاء ، ولكن الوهم لا يستطيع تصور ذلك :
Al-Ghazali answers this by saying that time is temporal and created. The meaning of God being prior to the world is that God existed alone, and there was no world, then the world came into existence. That is the meaning of the world's temporal origination, and the meaning of God being prior to it. Then, if it is said by the philosophers: 'The statement that time exists is necessary, for the meaning of our saying: God existed alone is that there was a time in which God alone existed.' It is said: 'This is an imagination [؟] of the fancy, not of the intellect. Often the fancy is incapable of conceiving realities as they are. Do you not see that the finitude of dimensions has been established, and that beyond the world there is neither void nor plenum, but the fancy cannot conceive that?'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage contains Ghazali's direct response to the philosophers' second proof. He begins by stating clearly that time is not eternal but created and temporal (حادث). The meaning of God's priority over the world is not that God existed in a prior time, but that God existed alone without any world, and then the world came into being. This is the correct understanding of the world's temporal origination (حدوث). The philosophers then raise an objection: even stating 'God existed alone' implies a time when God was alone, thus presupposing time. Ghazali counters that this objection arises from a failure of the imagination (وهم) as opposed to the intellect (عقل). He gives an example: reason has established that dimensions are finite and that beyond the world there is neither empty space (خلاء) nor filled space (ملاء), yet the imagination cannot picture this. Similarly, the imagination cannot conceive of God's existence without time, but the intellect can accept it. Thus, the philosophers' objection is based on a limitation of the imaginative faculty, not on a rational necessity.

Key Terms
زمان (time)
حادث (created/temporal)
وهم (fancy/imagination)
عقل (intellect)
تناهى الأبعاد (finitude of dimensions)
خلاء (void)
ملاء (plenum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'God existed alone' necessarily implies the existence of time.
Correction: Ghazali shows this is a failure of imagination, not a rational necessity. The intellect can understand God's existence without time, just as it understands finite dimensions without a beyond.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali explain the meaning of God being prior to the world?
A) God existed before the world in a timeless state, then created the world.
B) God existed alone without the world, then the world came to be.
C) God was in a previous time before the world.
D) The world has always existed alongside God.

Key Takeaway
God's priority over the world does not require time; it is a priority of existence alone, and the intellect can accept what the imagination cannot picture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's existence before the world, do you automatically picture a timeline? Has this lesson shown you that your imagination may be limiting your understanding of God's transcendence? How can you train your intellect to accept what Allah has revealed even when your mind struggles to picture it?

What would it take to submit your imagination to divine truth?
?
Ghazali gives the example of the finitude of dimensions: reason says finite, but imagination cannot picture it. In your own life, where is your imagination resisting a truth that your intellect has accepted? Will you continue to let your 'wahm' dictate your belief, or will you train it to follow the 'aql' and revelation?

Philosophers' Time Argument and Ghazali's Spatial Rebuttal
Philosophers argue for eternal time; Ghazali counters with spatial analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God's power to create earlier implies pre-existing time and how Ghazali's spatial analogy refutes it.

Core Idea
Power to act does not require pre-existing time or space.

This passage presents a second formulation of the philosophers' argument for the eternity of time: since God could have created the world earlier, an infinite duration must have existed before creation. Ghazali counters with a clever analogy: just as God's power to create the world larger does not prove extra space exists, His power to create earlier does not prove time pre-exists. Let's explore this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers claim that God was able to create the world at any prior moment, implying an infinite temporal duration before creation.
Notice they equate 'ability to create earlier' with the existence of time.
2
Understand Ghazali's spatial analogy
Read Ghazali's response: he compares time to space, arguing that God's ability to create a thicker world does not prove an extra spatial dimension exists.
The philosophers deny extra space beyond the world, yet their reasoning on time would force them to accept it.
3
Connect the analogy to the refutation
Analyze how Ghazali's spatial analogy undermines the philosophers' argument: ability does not entail actual existence of the object of that ability.
This is the key insight: power is about possibility, not about the actual existence of preconditions.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this means for the nature of time and creation: time itself is created and does not need to be eternal for God to have the power to create at any moment.
This supports the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo and the temporality of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صيغة ثانية للفلاسفة فى إلزام قدم الزمان . . . قالوا : لا شك أن الله كان قادراً على أن يخلق العالم قبل خلته بمدة ، وكان قادراً على خلقه قبل تلك المدة بمدة متفاوت المقدار مع الله ، وهو الزمان قال الغزالي: إن أقرب طريق فى دفع ذلك هو مقابلة الزمان بالمكان. فلا شك أن الله كان قادراً على أن يخلق الخلق في سمك أكبر مما هو عليه ، بذراع ... وبذراعين وهكذا إلى غير نهاية ، وإلا لزم عجز الله فيتأدى هذا إلى إثبات بعد وراء العالم وهم ينكرونه .. يرى الغزالى أن العالم إذا لم يكن قابلا لأن يكون أكبر مما هو عليه
A second formulation for the philosophers in necessitating the eternity of time. They said: 'There is no doubt that God was able to create the world before its creation [خلته؟] by a duration, and He was able to create it before that duration by a duration of varying measure with God, which is time.' Al-Ghazali said: 'The nearest way to repel this is to compare time with space. There is no doubt that God was able to create the creation in a thickness greater than what it is, by a cubit... by two cubits, and so on without end, otherwise the incapacity of God would be necessary. This would lead to establishing a dimension beyond the world, which they deny. Al-Ghazali holds that the world is not capable of being greater than it is...'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents a second formulation of the philosophers' argument for the eternity of time, along with Ghazali's response. The philosophers argue: God was able to create the world before He actually did, and before that earlier time, He could have created it even earlier, and so on ad infinitum. This implies an infinite duration before creation, which is time itself. Therefore, time must be eternal. Ghazali counters by drawing an analogy with space. He says: God was able to create the world with a greater thickness (سمك) than it has, by a cubit, two cubits, and so on without limit. If He could not, that would imply incapacity. But the philosophers themselves deny that there is any space (بعد) beyond the world. Yet the same reasoning would force them to admit an infinite space beyond, which they reject. Ghazali's point is that the ability to do something does not entail the actual existence of the object of that ability (here, time or space). Just as God's power to create a larger world does not require an actual extra space, so His power to create earlier does not require an actual pre-existing time. The world is not capable of being larger than it is, meaning its actual size is fixed, and similarly its actual temporal beginning is fixed.

Key Terms
زمان (time)
مكان (space)
قدرة (power)
سمك (thickness)
بعد (dimension)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's ability to create earlier means that time must have existed before creation, making time eternal.
Correction: Ghazali shows that ability does not require actual existence: just as God's power to create a larger world does not require extra space, His power to create earlier does not require pre-existing time.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to counter the philosophers' argument that God's ability to create earlier proves time is eternal?
A) God's power to create a greater number of angels.
B) God's power to create the world with greater thickness and the denial of extra space beyond it.
C) God's power to create the world from nothing.
D) God's power to create the world in an instant.

Key Takeaway
God's power does not require the actual pre-existence of the object of that power, as Ghazali shows with the spatial analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt bound by time or missed an opportunity, thinking it was too late for God to act? How does knowing that God's power is not limited by time change the way you pray for what seems impossible to happen now?

The philosophers assumed that God's ability to create earlier must translate into actual infinite time. Are there areas in your life where you assume that your potential is limited by past failures or the scarcity of time? Will you repent of that mindset and trust in God's unconstrained power?
?
Ghazali's rebuttal shows that even the most logical human reasoning can be fallible when applied to God. In what ways have you trusted your own reasoning over what revelation says about God's attributes, and what step will you take to correct this starting today?

The Philosophers' Argument: God's Power Implies Pre-Eternal Time
Philosophers present the second formulation: God's power to create earlier implies pre-eternal time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second argument for the pre-eternity of time based on God's ability to create earlier by any duration.

Core Idea
If God could create earlier, then time must have existed before creation.

The philosophers press a powerful objection: if God was able to create the world any amount of time earlier, then time must be pre-eternal as a measure of that possibility. This argument seems to demand that time is not created but exists alongside God. Let's examine their claim closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the claim that God's power to create earlier necessitates time.
The passage begins with a discussion on time and presents the second formulation.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the premise: God was able to create the world a period before its creation, and even before that period, ad infinitum.
3
Understand their conclusion
Understand why they conclude that this potential delay must be measured by something, which they call time.
They infer that time is pre-eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث في وجود الزمان * صيغة ثانية للفلاسفة فى إلزام قدم الزمان . . . قالوا : لا شك أن الله كان قادراً على أن يخلق العالم قبل خلته بمدة ، وكان قادراً على خلقه قبل تلك المدة بمدة متفاوت المقدار مع الله ، وهو الزمان قال الغزالي: إن أقرب طريق فى دفع ذلك هو مقابلة الزمان بالمكان. فلا شك أن الله كان قادراً على أن يخلق الخلق في سمك أكبر مما هو عليه ، بذراع ... وبذراعين وهكذا إلى غير نهاية ، وإلا لزم عجز الله فيتأدى هذا إلى إثبات بعد وراء العالم وهم ينكرونه ..
A discussion on the existence of time. A second formulation of the philosophers for necessitating the pre-eternity of time... They said: There is no doubt that God was able to create the world a period before its creation, and He was able to create it before that period by a period of varying magnitude with God, and that is time. Al-Ghazali said: 'The closest way to repel that is to compare time with space. For there is no doubt that God was able to create the creation in a thickness greater than what it is, by a cubit... and by two cubits, and so on to infinity. Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This is a second objection by the philosophers and Ghazali's response. The philosophers argue that since God could have created the world earlier by any amount of time, time must be pre-eternal as a measure of that potential delay. Ghazali retorts by comparing time to space: just as God could have created the world larger in size by any amount, but we do not infer an infinite space beyond the world (which they deny), so too potential temporal delay does not prove an actual pre-eternal time. The argument is parallel, and the philosophers cannot accept the spatial conclusion, so they should reject the temporal one.

Key Terms

قادر (qādir)

Ghazali's Response: The Space Analogy
Ghazali counters by comparing time to space: potential increase does not imply actual infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rejection of the philosophers' argument by drawing an analogy between time and space.

Core Idea
God's power to create larger does not prove infinite space, so His power to create earlier does not prove pre-eternal time.

Ghazali responds with a clever analogy: if we accept the philosophers' reasoning about time, we must also accept that God's power to create a larger world proves infinite space, which they deny. This parallel shows the flaw in their inference. Let's see his counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's response
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on his comparison of time to space.
2
Identify the space analogy
Notice how Ghazali points out that God could have created the world thicker by any amount, analogous to creating it earlier.
He uses cubits as an example of incremental size.
3
Understand the refutation
Understand that if we infer infinite space from potential size, we reach an absurdity the philosophers reject. Thus, the same logic should not apply to time.
The parallel shows that potential does not entail actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث في وجود الزمان * صيغة ثانية للفلاسفة فى إلزام قدم الزمان . . . قالوا : لا شك أن الله كان قادراً على أن يخلق العالم قبل خلته بمدة ، وكان قادراً على خلقه قبل تلك المدة بمدة متفاوت المقدار مع الله ، وهو الزمان قال الغزالي: إن أقرب طريق فى دفع ذلك هو مقابلة الزمان بالمكان. فلا شك أن الله كان قادراً على أن يخلق الخلق في سمك أكبر مما هو عليه ، بذراع ... وبذراعين وهكذا إلى غير نهاية ، وإلا لزم عجز الله فيتأدى هذا إلى إثبات بعد وراء العالم وهم ينكرونه ..
A discussion on the existence of time. A second formulation of the philosophers for necessitating the pre-eternity of time... They said: There is no doubt that God was able to create the world a period before its creation, and He was able to create it before that period by a period of varying magnitude with God, and that is time. Al-Ghazali said: 'The closest way to repel that is to compare time with space. For there is no doubt that God was able to create the creation in a thickness greater than what it is, by a cubit... and by two cubits, and so on to infinity. Otherwise, God's inability would follow, and this would lead to proving a dimension beyond the world, which they deny.'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This is a second objection by the philosophers and Ghazali's response. The philosophers argue that since God could have created the world earlier by any amount of time, time must be pre-eternal as a measure of that potential delay. Ghazali retorts by comparing time to space: just as God could have created the world larger in size by any amount, but we do not infer an infinite space beyond the world (which they deny), so too potential temporal delay does not prove an actual pre-eternal time. The argument is parallel, and the philosophers cannot accept the spatial conclusion, so they should reject the temporal one.

Key Terms
زمان (zamān)
مكان (makān, space)
قادر (qādir)
خلق (khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies God's power to create earlier.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny the power; he denies that the power implies a pre-existing time.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to refute the philosophers' second argument?
A) The analogy of God's knowledge
B) The analogy of space and size
C) The analogy of causality
D) The analogy of the soul

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' argument proves too much, as it would also require infinite space, which they reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever assume that because God could do something, He must have already done it? How does this lesson challenge you to trust His wisdom in His actual choices rather than His possibilities?
?
When you imagine God's power, do you impose limits based on your own logic—like thinking time or space must be infinite for God to be powerful? What would it take for you to humbly accept that His power transcends your categories?
?
Ghazali shows that potential does not equal actual. Where in your own thinking about your life—your abilities, relationships, or future—have you mistaken what you could do for what you must do? How will you realign your expectations with God's actual decree?

Eternal Possibility Does Not Mean Eternal Existence
Ghazali refutes the claim that eternal possibility of the world implies its eternal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why eternal possibility does not necessitate eternal existence, and how Ghazali uses an analogy to refute the philosophers' argument.

Core Idea
Possibility is not actuality; eternal possibility does not imply eternal existence.

In this passage, Ghazali presents his response to the philosophers' third proof for the pre-eternity of the world. The philosophers argue that if the world is eternally possible, it must have always existed. Ghazali counters with a clear analogy: just as the possibility of infinite expansion does not mean infinite space exists, the eternal possibility of the world does not mean it is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and the English translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the key terms: إمكان and أزلي.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage has two parts: the philosophers' claim and Ghazali's refutation. Identify each.
3
Analyze the analogy
Ghazali uses an analogy about the size of the world. Explain how it refutes the philosophers' claim.
The analogy shows that possibility does not necessitate actuality.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Reflect on why possibility and actuality are different. How does this relate to the pre-eternity debate?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث للفلاسفة على قدم العالم بحث في أزلية الإمكان * * ويرى الغزالى أن الإمكان الأزلي لا يستلزم الوجود الأزلى ، مثلما لم يستلزم إمكان زيادة لا نهاية لها في حجم العالم ، إمكان وجود ملاء لا نهاية له
A third proof of the philosophers for the pre-eternity of the world: a discussion on the eternity of possibility. And al-Ghazali holds that eternal possibility does not entail eternal existence, just as the possibility of an infinite increase in the size of the world does not entail the existence of an infinite plenum.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This is a short but complete argument. Philosophers claim that since the possibility of the world is eternal, the world itself must be eternal (otherwise possibility would be unrealized). Ghazali refutes by analogy: the possibility of the world being infinitely larger does not mean there is an infinite actual void (plenum). Similarly, the eternal possibility of the world does not require its eternal actual existence. Possibility does not necessitate actuality.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān, possibility)
أزلي (azalī, eternal)
وجود (wujūd, existence)
ملاء (malā', plenum/fullness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal possibility necessarily leads to eternal actuality.
Correction: Ghazali shows that possibility does not necessitate actuality, using the analogy of infinite size.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to refute the claim that eternal possibility requires eternal existence?
A) The possibility of the world being smaller
B) The possibility of infinite increase in size not requiring actual infinite space
C) The possibility of God's knowledge
D) The possibility of time

Key Takeaway
Eternal possibility does not entail eternal existence because possibility and actuality are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How often do you assume that because something is possible for you—like a good deed or a change in character—it will happen without deliberate effort? Does Ghazali's distinction between possibility and actuality expose a comfortable lie you've been telling yourself? What one action will you take today to turn a possibility into an actuality?
?
Eternal possibility does not mean eternal existence. In your own life, what possibilities have you left unrealized, assuming they would eventually become real? What does that reveal about your reliance on intentions rather than actions? Will you let this lesson unsettle you into real change?
?
If you believe that God's power makes all things possible, does that mean all possibilities must actualize? How does this lesson protect you from the error of thinking that God's will is limited by what is possible? Examine your heart: do you secretly doubt God's wisdom when possibilities remain unrealized? How will you trust Him more starting now?

Fourth Proof: Possibility Needs a Substrate
Philosophers argue that possibility requires an eternal substrate: matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' fourth proof for the pre-eternity of the world and how it argues that because possibility is a relational attribute requiring a substrate, matter must be eternal.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a thing; it is a relation that needs a substrate, says the philosopher.

In this passage, the philosophers present their fourth argument for the pre-eternity of the world. They claim that since the world's possibility is eternal and possibility is a relational attribute needing a substrate, matter must be eternal. This argument is a key point of contention in the Tahafut, and Ghazali will later challenge the assumption that possibility is a real attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Note the concise, syllogistic style.
This is a single argument compressed into one sentence.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion
Break the argument into its logical parts: possibility is eternal, possibility is a relational attribute, relational attributes need a substrate, the only possible substrate is matter, therefore matter is eternal.
3
Reflect on the controversy
Consider why this argument is problematic. What does it assume about possibility? How might a mutakallim object?
Ghazali's response (not included here) denies that possibility is a real attribute needing a substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل رابع للفلاسفة على قدم العالم قالوا : إمكان العالم قديم ، والإمكان وصف إضافى لا قوام له بنفسه ، فلابد له من محل يضاف إليه ، ولا محل إلا المادة فالمادة قديمة
A fourth proof of the philosophers for the pre-eternity of the world. They said: The possibility of the world is eternal, and possibility is a relational attribute that has no subsistence by itself, so it must have a substrate to which it is attributed, and there is no substrate except matter; therefore matter is eternal.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' fourth argument in this section. They claim that the possibility of the world is eternal. Since possibility is a relational attribute (it depends on something), it needs a substrate (maḥall) that exists. The only possible substrate is matter, so matter must be eternal. This argument is incomplete in the text; Ghazali's refutation is not provided here. The reader should note that this is only the objection.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān, possibility)
قديم (qadīm, eternal/pre-eternal)
وصف إضافى (waṣf iḍāfī, relational attribute)
محل (maḥall, substrate/locus)
مادة (mādda, matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possibility must have a real substrate because it is a real attribute.
Correction: In kalam, possibility is not a real attribute; it is a mental consideration. Thus the argument fails.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' fourth proof, why must matter be eternal?
A) Because possibility is a substance
B) Because possibility is a relational attribute that needs a substrate, and only matter can be that substrate
C) Because matter is necessary
D) Because possibility is created

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' fourth proof claims that because possibility is eternal and needs a substrate, matter must be eternal — a claim Ghazali rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that matter is eternal and does not need a Creator, how would that change the way you thank Allah for the blessings you receive? Do you sometimes live as if things exist on their own, forgetting that their existence is only possible through Allah's will? What would it take for you to start every day with that awareness?
?
The philosophers say possibility needs a substrate like matter. In your life, do you place your trust in 'substrates'—like money, health, or relationships—as if they are eternal and self-sufficient? How does this lesson challenge you to shift your reliance solely to Allah, the Creator of all substrates?
?
This argument treats the world's possibility as an eternal attribute. But the Qur'an says, 'He created the heavens and the earth in truth' (16:3). When you look around at the universe, do you see it as a contingent, created reality that depends on Allah at every moment, or do you fall into the illusion that it is self-sufficient? What one concrete change can you make today to remind yourself that everything is 'possible' and thus dependent on Allah?

The Philosophers' Argument from the Sun's Perpetuity
Ghazali presents the philosophers' first proof and gives his first counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first proof for the world's perpetuity based on the sun's lack of decay, and Ghazali's objection that it wrongly limits the causes of annihilation to withering.

Core Idea
Ghazali challenges the assumption that all annihilation is due to withering.

This lesson introduces the philosophers' claim that the world is perpetual, just as it is eternal. They offer a proof: the sun has never shown any signs of decay, so the world must be perpetual. Ghazali responds by pointing out that this proof only works if all causes of annihilation are confined to withering—an unestablished claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the philosophers' argument and Ghazali's first response.
Notice how Ghazali turns the proof's assumption against it.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The proof says: no decay observed → perpetual. Ghazali says: proof assumes decay only by withering.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What would be needed to prove that withering is the only cause of annihilation?
This is a logical criticism, not empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في إبطال قولهم: في أبدية العالم - والزمان والحركة. يرى الفلاسفة أنه كما أن العالم قديم لا أول لوجوده، فهو باق لا آخر لبقائه. والأدلة الأربعة التي قيلت على لسانهم في إثبات القدم، تجري في إثبات البقاء. ويرى الغزالي أن نفس الاعتراضات التي أوردت على أدلة إثبات القدم، تجري على أدلة إثبات الأبدية. يقول الغزالي إن للفلاسفة دليلين خاصين بإثبات الأبدية: الدليل الأول: يقرر أن العالم لم يبد عليه أي أثر لفناء، فالشمس مثلاً لم يلحقها ذبول قط، فهي إذن باقية. ويجيب الغزالي على هذا بجوابين: الجواب الأول: أن هذا الدليل غير منتج إلا إذا ثبت أن أسباب الفناء منحصرة في الذبول. الجواب الثاني: لو سلم أنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول، فمن أين لهم أن الشمس لم يصبها ذبول قط، فقد يكون هناك ذبول جزئي قد أصابها، ولكن خفي علينا إدراكه. والجواب الثاني: أن العالم لا تنعدم جواهره؛ لأنه لا يعقل سبب معدم لها؛ لأن إرادة حادثة لإعدامه تؤدي إلى تغير الله سبحانه، وإرادة قديمة تقتضي أن يكون العدم قديماً؛ لأنه لا فصل بين العلة التامة والمعلول.
A problem concerning refuting their statement on the perpetuity of the world—time and motion. The philosophers hold that just as the world is eternal with no beginning to its existence, so it is perpetual with no end to its duration. The four proofs that were stated on their behalf in proving eternity also apply in proving perpetuity. And al-Ghazali holds that the same objections that were raised against the proofs of eternity apply to the proofs of perpetuity. Al-Ghazali says that the philosophers have two specific proofs for establishing perpetuity: The first proof: it establishes that no trace of annihilation has appeared upon the world, so the sun, for example, has never been affected by any withering, so it is therefore perpetual. Al-Ghazali answers this with two responses: The first response: that this proof is not productive unless it is established that the causes of annihilation are confined to withering. The second response: even if it is granted that there is no corruption except by withering, how do they know that the sun has never been affected by any withering? For there might be a partial withering that has affected it, but its perception escaped us. And the second response: that the substances of the world do not become nonexistent; because no efficient cause for their annihilation is conceivable; since a temporally originated will to annihilate it would lead to a change in God, Exalted, and an eternal will would require that non-existence be eternal; because there is no separation between the complete cause and the effect.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that the world is perpetual (abadiyya). It begins by stating the philosophers' view: as the world is eternal without beginning, so it is perpetual without end. They argue that the same four proofs for eternity also prove perpetuity. Ghazali counters that the same objections raised against the proofs of eternity apply here. He then presents two specific proofs the philosophers use for perpetuity. The first proof: the world shows no sign of decay—e.g., the sun has never withered—so it must be perpetual. Ghazali gives two responses: (1) the proof only works if all causes of annihilation are limited to withering (dhubul), which is not established; (2) even if withering is the only cause, they cannot prove the sun has never suffered partial withering that escaped observation. The passage then introduces a second argument (labeled 'the second response' in the text, but likely a separate proof from the philosophers): that the world's substances cannot become nonexistent because there is no conceivable efficient cause for their annihilation. Any temporal will to annihilate would imply change in God, while an eternal will would require non-existence to be eternal (since the complete cause and effect are simultaneous). The text ends there without Ghazali's own response, but the context implies the argument is being presented for refutation.

Key Terms
أبدية العالم
ذبول
فناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali accepts the world's perpetuity because he does not deny the sun's lack of decay.
Correction: Ghazali does not accept perpetuity; he refutes the proof by questioning its underlying assumption.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's first response to the philosophers' proof of perpetuity?
A) He denies that the sun never withers
B) He says the proof assumes all annihilation is due to withering, which is unproven
C) He argues that the world must have a beginning
D) He accepts the proof

Key Takeaway
Ghazali exposes an unstated assumption in the philosophers' first proof, showing that logical scrutiny can dismantle empirical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'sun'—some cherished belief or habit in your life—do you consider permanent simply because you've never seen it fail? What hidden assumptions (like 'only withering causes annihilation') might you be relying on without proof, and how can you test them?
?
Ghazali's first response targets an unproven premise. Think of a conviction you hold about your faith or yourself that feels obvious but actually depends on an assumption you've never examined. Would you be willing to scrutinize that assumption, even if it unsettles you? What might it cost you to do so?

Ghazali's Second Response and the Second Philosophical Proof
Ghazali continues with his second response and presents the philosophers' second argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second response to the first proof and the philosophers' second argument concerning the impossibility of conceiving a cause for the world's annihilation.

Core Idea
Even if the sun never seemed to wither, we cannot rule out hidden decay; and a cause for annihilation would require a change in God or an eternal non-existence.

In this lesson, we continue where the previous left off. Ghazali offers a second reply to the first proof: even if we accept that withering is the only cause of annihilation, we cannot be certain that the sun has not suffered partial withering that escaped our observation. Then, he presents the philosophers' second argument: the world's substances cannot become nonexistent because no cause for annihilation is conceivable — a temporal will would change God, and an eternal will would require non-existence to be eternal. This argument is left for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see Ghazali's second response and the second philosophical argument.
Note that the 'second response' in the text actually transitions into the second proof.
2
Analyse the second response
Ghazali points out that even if withering is the only cause, the sun might have suffered hidden partial withering.
This is an epistemological objection.
3
Analyse the second proof
The philosophers argue that no cause can annihilate the world; a temporal will changes God, an eternal will makes non-existence eternal.
This is a metaphysical argument based on divine attributes.
4
Reflect
Consider how Ghazali might respond to this second proof based on his previous arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Sun's Apparent Permanence: Ghazali's Two Responses
Ghazali presents the philosophers' first proof for the world's eternity and gives two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first proof for the world's eternity based on the sun's lack of observed decay, and Ghazali's two refutations of that proof.

Core Idea
Observing no decay does not prove eternal permanence.

This passage sets up a key debate about whether the world is everlasting. The philosophers point to the sun as an empirical example: it never shows signs of decay, so it must be eternal. Ghazali challenges this inference not by rejecting the observation, but by questioning what it can really prove. His two responses reveal the limits of empirical reasoning in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali first states the philosophers' position and then introduces their proof and his responses.
The proof is based on the sun's observed lack of withering.
2
Identify the philosophers' proof
The philosophers argue: the sun has never shown any withering (decay), therefore it is everlasting. Identify this reasoning in the text.
3
Examine Ghazali's first response
Ghazali says the proof is only valid if the only cause of decay is withering. He challenges this premise. Understand why this weakens the argument.
He is not accepting that decay only comes from withering.
4
Examine Ghazali's second response
Even if decay only comes from withering, how do we know the sun never suffered any withering? It might have had a partial one we cannot perceive. This attacks the empirical claim itself.
This shows the limits of human observation.
5
Reflect on the method
Ghazali does not deny the possibility of the world being eternal; he only shows that this particular proof is inconclusive. Reflect on how he uses logical and epistemic critiques.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
10 - مسألة في إبطال قولهم : فى أبدية العالم - والزمان والحركة
يرى الفلاسفة أنه كما أن العالم قديم لا أول لوجوده ، فهو باق لا آخر لبقائه . والأدلة الأربعة التي قيلت على لسانهم في إثبات القدم ، تجرى في إثبات البقاء
ويرى الغزالى أن نفس الاعتراضات التى أوردت على أدلة إثبات القدم ، تجرى على أدلة إثبات الأبدية
يقول الغزالي إن للفلاسفة دليلين خاصين بإثبات الأبدية :
الدليل الأول : يقرر أن العالم لم يبد عليه أي أثر لفناء ، فالشمس مثلاً لم يلحقها ذبول قط ، فهي إذن باقية.
ويجيب الغزالي على هذا بجوابين :
