الجواب الثاني : لو سلم أنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول ، فمن أين لهم أن الشمس لم يصبها ذبول قط ، فقد يكون هناك ذبول جزئى قد أصابها ، ولكن خفى علينا إدراكه
10 - Problem in refuting their statement about the eternity of the world, time, and motion. The philosophers hold that just as the world is eternal with no beginning to its existence, it is everlasting with no end to its permanence. And the four proofs that were cited on their tongues in proving pre-eternity apply in proving everlastingness. And al-Ghazali holds that the very objections that were raised against the proofs of pre-eternity apply to the proofs of eternity. Al-Ghazali says that the philosophers have two specific proofs for establishing eternity: The first proof: It establishes that no trace of annihilation has appeared upon the world; for example, the sun has never been affected by any withering at all, so it is therefore everlasting. And al-Ghazali responds to this with two answers: The first answer: That this proof is not productive unless it is established that the causes of annihilation are confined to withering. The second answer: If we grant that there is no corruption except by withering, then how do they know that the sun has never been affected by any withering at all? For there may be a partial withering that has affected it, but its perception has been hidden from us.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion on the philosophers' claim that the world is everlasting (أبدي) just as it is eternal (قديم). Ghazali first states the general position: the same arguments used for pre-eternity serve for eternity. He then introduces two specific proofs the philosophers offer for eternity. The first proof is empirical: no sign of decay is observed in the sun, so it must be everlasting. Ghazali gives two responses. The first challenges the premise that only withering causes corruption; perhaps other causes exist. The second even if decay only comes from withering, how can we be sure the sun never suffered any withering? It might have experienced an imperceptible partial withering. Thus the proof is inconclusive.

Correction: Ghazali does not deny the sun's permanence; he only questions the certainty of the empirical evidence used to prove it.

What is Ghazali's second response to the philosophers' first proof for the world's eternity?
A) The causes of annihilation are not limited to withering.
B) Even if decay only comes from withering, the sun may have suffered a hidden partial withering.
C) The philosophers' arguments are irrelevant.
D) The world is not everlasting at all.

Key Takeaway
Empirical observations of no decay do not suffice to prove eternal permanence; deeper philosophical scrutiny of the premises and the limits of perception is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If even the sun, a magnificent sign of Allah, might have hidden decay that escapes perception, what does that say about your own sense of security in your good deeds? Will you investigate the unseen state of your soul before the Day when all is revealed?

The Philosophers' Second Proof: No Cause for Annihilation
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second proof against annihilation of substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof for the world's everlastingness, which argues that no conceivable cause can annihilate the world's substances.

Core Idea
Annihilation is impossible because no cause for it is rational.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the second philosophical proof for the everlastingness of the world. The philosophers assert that the substances of the world cannot become non-existent because no efficient cause for their annihilation can be conceived. They argue that whether God's will for annihilation is new or eternal, both lead to absurdities. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophical argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that this is the philosophers' proof, not Ghazali's own view.
The passage is labeled 'الجواب الثانى' but it is actually a proof, not an answer.
2
Examine the impossibility of a new will
Identify why the philosophers reject a new will for annihilation: it would imply change in God, which they consider impossible.
A new will would mean God's attributes change, contradicting divine immutability.
3
Examine the impossibility of an eternal will
Understand why an eternal will for non-existence would entail that non-existence is eternal, coexisting with the world's existence — a contradiction.
If the cause is eternal, the effect must be eternal; but non-existence cannot coexist with existence.
4
Reflect on the additional argument
Note the philosophers' claim that non-existence is not an act that can be an effect of any cause.
This reinforces that annihilation cannot be caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب الثانى : أن العالم لا تنعدم جواهره ؛ لأنه لا يعقل سبب معدم لها ؛ لأن إرادة حادثة لإعدامه تؤدى إلى تغير الله سبحانه ، وإرادة قديمة تقتضى أن يكون العدم قديماً ؛ لأنه لا فصل بين العله التامة والمعلول
وأيضاً العدم ليس فعلا يعقل أن يكون أثرا.
The second answer: That the substances of the world do not become non-existent because no efficient cause of their annihilation is conceivable; because a new will for annihilating it would lead to a change in God, exalted is He, and an eternal will would entail that non-existence is eternal, since there is no separation between the complete cause and the effect. Also, non-existence is not an act that can be conceived as an effect.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of the philosophers for the world's everlastingness, though it is introduced as 'the second answer' (likely a mislabel). The proof argues that the world's substances cannot perish (تنعدم) because there is no intelligible cause that would annihilate them. Ghazali presents the philosophers' reasoning: if God willed annihilation with a new will (إرادة حادثة), that would imply change in God, which is impossible. If He willed it with an eternal will, then the non-existence would be eternal, coexisting with the world's existence, which is contradictory. Additionally, non-existence (العدم) is not an act (فعل) that can be produced as an effect. Therefore, annihilation is impossible. Ghazali does not immediately refute this here; he proceeds to discuss the theologians' views on annihilation.

Key Terms

عدم
علة تامة
أثر

Misconception: The reader might think that this argument is Ghazali's own answer or position.
Correction: This is actually the philosophers' proof. Ghazali is presenting their reasoning, which he will later critique using the views of the theologians (mutakallimun).

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' second proof, why is it inconceivable that God annihilates the world with a new will?
A) Because a new will would imply change in God.
B) Because God's will is eternal.
C) Because the world is self-sufficient.
D) Because non-existence is an act.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' second proof asserts that annihilation of substances is impossible because no cause for it can be conceived: a new will changes God, an eternal will makes non-existence eternal, and non-existence is not a caused act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the philosophers' claim that no cause can annihilate you, do you truly rely on God's unchanging will for your continued existence, or do you fear that something else—your health, circumstances, or others—could snatch away your life? What would it mean to live with the certainty that only God's eternal will sustains you, and how would that change your daily worries? Ask yourself: Do I live as if my existence hangs on the will of Allah alone, or do I place my trust in created means? Make a concrete change in your reliance this week, such as reciting 'Hasbunallahu wa ni'mal wakeel' with full conviction before a fear-inducing situation.
?
The argument states that a new will in God would imply change—something impossible for the Perfect. But reflect: Have you ever wished that God would change His decree for you, that He would alter His will to match your desires? If so, what does that reveal about your understanding of His perfection and the finality of His wisdom? How can you cultivate contentment with His eternal will, especially when it contradicts your own plans? Commit tonight—before sleep—to thanking Him for one decree you previously wished were different, and ask for the strength to trust His unchanging wisdom.
?
Non-existence is not an act that can be caused, the philosophers say. Yet you may live as though your existence is fragile, at the mercy of atoms and accidents. Does your soul truly grasp that annihilation is not a created thing—it is simply the cessation of God's creative act? When you fear death, do you fear the end of your deeds or the end of yourself? How would your life change if you believed that your very being is continually renewed by God's will, moment by moment, and that nothing can undo that except His own decision? This week, take one concrete step: pause five times a day to say 'Alhamdulillahi alladhi amsana bihi wa amsana alayhi' and feel the weight of your dependence on His continuous act of keeping you in existence.

The Dispute Over the Meaning of 'Fa'il'
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' use of 'fa'il' by distinguishing meaning from label.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' use of the term 'fa'il' and why he insists that true agency requires will.

Core Idea
Words without reality cannot sustain religion.

Ghazali has argued that the philosophers' notion of God as an agent is empty because it lacks will. Now he addresses the objection that this is merely a dispute over names. He clarifies that the issue is not terminology but the very meaning of 'act' and 'making'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali first concedes a point, then turns the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: فاعل, فعل, صنع, إرادة, تلبيس.
2
Identify Ghazali's two moves
First, he concedes that if the opponent refuses to call God a 'fa'il' after the meaning is clear, there is no quarrel over names. Then, he turns: the real issue is that the philosophers deny the true meaning of 'act' (what proceeds from will) while using the word to appear Muslim.
This is a classic argumentative strategy.
3
Understand the concept of 'talbis'
Ghazali concludes that the whole discussion aims to unveil the deception (talbis). Reflect on why Ghazali considers the philosophers' use of language deceptive.
Deception here is using a word without its essential meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الخصم يأبى أن يسمى هذا فاعلا، فلا مشاحة في الأسامي، بعد ظهور المعنى. قال الغزالي: إن غرضنا أن هذا المعنى لا يسمى فعلا وصنعاً، وإنما المعنى بالصنع والفعل ما يصدر عن الإرادة حقيقة، وقد نفيتم حقيقة معنى الفعل، ونطقتم بلفظه تجملا بالإسلاميين، ولا يتم الدين بإطلاق الألفاظ الفارغة عن المعاني. والمقصود من هذه المسألة الكشف عن هذا التلبيس.
If the opponent refuses to call this an agent (fa‛il), there is no quarrel over names after the meaning has become clear. Al-Ghazali said: Our purpose is that this meaning is not called an act (fi‛l) or a making (ṣun‛); rather, the meaning of making and act is what proceeds from will (irāda) in reality. You have denied the reality of the meaning of act, and uttered its word merely as a cosmetic toward the Muslims. Religion is not perfected by uttering words empty of meanings. The aim of this issue is to unveil this deception.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God is an agent (fa‛il) of the world. Ghazali first concedes that if the opponent refuses to call God an agent in the sense he (Ghazali) has explained, there is no need to argue over terminology once the meaning is clear. However, he then turns the argument: his real purpose is to show that the philosophers do not genuinely mean by 'act' what Muslims mean. For Muslims, an act (fi‛l) and a making (ṣun‛) are defined as that which proceeds from will (irāda) in reality. The philosophers have denied that God acts by will, so they have denied the true meaning of act while still using the word to please Muslims. Ghazali concludes that the religion (dīn) is not established by empty words; the purpose of this entire discussion is to expose this deception (talbīs).

Key Terms

فعل
صنع
إرادة
تلبيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the dispute is only about words.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the philosophers deny the reality of the meaning, not just the label.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the true meaning of 'act' (fi‛l)?
A) Whatever is caused by an agent, regardless of will.
B) What proceeds from will in reality.
C) Mere coexistence of agent and effect.
D) Bringing something from non-existence to existence.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali insists that the philosophers' use of 'fa'il' is deceptive because they strip it of its essential meaning (will), reducing religion to empty words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ghazali warns that religion is not perfected by uttering words empty of meanings. Think of a phrase you recite frequently in worship (e.g., 'Allahu Akbar'). Have you ever said it while your heart was absent from its meaning? What will you do today to ensure that your words are filled with reality?
?
The philosophers used Islamic terms to appear aligned with Muslims while denying their essence. In your own life, are there any beliefs or practices you claim outwardly but have internally stripped of their true spirit? What step will you take this week to align your inner reality with your outer profession?

Act as Bringing into Existence
Ghazali presents his definition of act and the philosophers' counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of act (fi‘l) as bringing into existence (iḥdāth) and why it entails that an eternal world cannot be considered an act of God.

Core Idea
If act means bringing into existence, an eternal world cannot be an act.

Ghazali begins his second argument by clarifying what 'act' truly means. For him, an act is not just any making but specifically bringing something from non-existence to existence. This definition immediately challenges the philosophers' claim that the world is God's act, because they hold the world to be eternal — never having been non-existent. In this passage, you will see the initial formulation of this argument and the philosophers' first response.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of act
Notice how Ghazali defines fi‘l as iḥdāth, bringing into existence. Contrast this with the philosophers' view that the world is eternal (qadīm).
2
Understand the philosophers' redefinition
The philosophers reinterpret 'originated' (ḥādith) as 'existing after non-existence' and argue that the agent's effect is existence itself, which can be eternal.
They distinguish between origination and mere existence.
3
Grasp the need for a sustainer
The philosophers assert that a possible existent at every moment needs a sustainer, but that does not require origination.
This sets up the later debate on divine preservation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: من أوجه بطلان قولهم: إن الله فاعل العالم وصانعه، من جهة الفعل نفسه. لأن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث، والعالم عندهم قديم وليس بحادث. ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود، بإحداثه. قالت الفلاسفة: إن معنى الحادث الموجود بعد عدم، ولا أثر للفاعل في العدم السابق، ولا في مجموع الوجود والعدم، فبقى أن أثره الوجود، ولا مانع أن يكون هذا الأثر قديما، ولا يستشكل على هذا بأن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده؛ لأنه إن أريد أنه لا يستأنف له وجود، فصحيح؛ ولا يدعي الفلاسفة ذلك. وإن أريد أنه في حال كونه موجوداً لا يكون موجوداً بموجد، فغير صحيح؛ لأن الإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً، وكون المفعول موجوداً، والموجود الممكن في كل آنات وجوده محتاج إلى من يمسك عليه الوجود. قال الغزالي: إن الفاعل يتعلق بالفعل عندنا في حال الحدوث فقط، لا في ثاني حال الحدوث. قالت الفلاسفة: اعترفتم بجواز كون الفعل مع الفاعل غير متأخر عنه، فيلزم منه أن يكون الفعل قديماً إن كان الفاعل قديما. قال الغزالي: لا نحيل أن يكون الفعل مع الفاعل بعد كونه حادثاً.
The second aspect: from the aspects of the falsity of their saying that God is the agent (fā‛il) and maker (ṣāni‛) of the world, from the perspective of the act itself. For the act (fi‛l) is an expression for bringing into existence (iḥdāth). The world according to them is eternal (qadīm) and not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of act is bringing a thing out of non-existence into existence by bringing it into existence. The philosophers said: The meaning of originated (ḥādith) is that which exists after non-existence. The agent has no effect in the prior non-existence, nor in the aggregate of existence and non-existence. So it remains that His effect is existence. There is no obstacle to this effect being eternal. And there is no difficulty in this because the existent cannot be brought into existence—if what is meant is that it does not have existence initiated anew, that is correct, and the philosophers do not claim that. But if what is meant is that while it exists it is not existent by an existentiator, that is false; because bringing into existence (ījād) is concomitant with the agent being an existentiator and the effect being existent, and the possible existent at every moment of its existence is in need of one who maintains its existence. Al-Ghazali said: The agent is related to the act according to us only at the moment of origination (ḥudūth), not at the second moment after origination. The philosophers said: You have conceded the permissibility of the act being simultaneous with the agent not posterior to it. It follows that the act is eternal if the agent is eternal. Al-Ghazali said: We do not deem it impossible that the act is simultaneous with the agent after it has become originated.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is the second argument—from the perspective of the act itself—that the world cannot be God's act on the philosophers' own principles. Ghazali begins by defining act (fi‛l) as bringing into existence (iḥdāth), which means bringing something from non-existence to existence. Since the philosophers hold the world to be eternal (qadīm) and not originated (ḥādith), the world cannot be an act in this sense. The philosophers respond by redefining the originated as 'that which exists after non-existence' and argue that the agent's effect is merely existence, which can be eternal. They then address an objection: that an existent cannot be brought into existence. They clarify: if this means it does not receive existence anew, that is fine, as they do not claim origination; but if it means that an existent does not need an existentiator while existing, that is false, because a possible being needs a sustainer at every moment. Ghazali then counters: according to the Ash‛arites, the agent's relation to the act is only at the moment of origination, not afterward. The philosophers object: if the act can be simultaneous with the agent without temporal posteriority, then if the agent is eternal, the act must be eternal. Ghazali replies that they do not consider it impossible for the act to be simultaneous with the agent after it has become originated—meaning the act is originated even if coexistent. Thus Ghazali maintains that true acts must be originated, and the philosophers' eternal world cannot be an act.

Key Terms
فعل
إحداث
قديم
حادث
إيجاد
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the world is eternal, it does not need God.
Correction: The philosophers argue that even an eternal possible existent needs a sustainer at every moment.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is their definition of 'originated' (ḥādith)?
A) That which exists after non-existence.
B) That which is brought into existence from nothing.
C) That which is created ex nihilo.
D) That which is dependent on an agent.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that if act means bringing into existence, an eternal world cannot be an act; the philosophers counter by redefining the effect as existence itself, which can be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your existence is sustained by God at every moment, not just at birth, how would that change your awareness of Him throughout your day? What specific habit could you adopt to remind yourself that you are being created anew each second?
?
The philosophers argued that existence itself can be eternal but still needs a sustainer. Do you ever live as if you are self-sufficient in your daily life, forgetting your constant dependence on God? What would it look like to start your day acknowledging that your next breath is a gift from Him?
?
Ghazali insists that act means bringing into existence. If you only thank God for past blessings but ignore your ongoing need for His sustenance, what are you missing? What is one concrete way you can express reliance on Him right now, not just in theory?

Simultaneity of Agent and Act: Does It Imply Eternity?
Ghazali defends the possibility of an originated act simultaneous with an eternal agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's Ash‘arite position that the agent relates to the act only at the moment of origination, and the philosophers' objection that if act can be simultaneous with agent, then an eternal agent implies an eternal act.

Core Idea
Even if act is simultaneous with agent, it can still be originated.

In this second part of the argument, Ghazali presents his own theological position: the agent's relation to the act is only at the moment of origination (ḥudūth), not after. The philosophers seize on the idea of simultaneity, arguing that if the act can be simultaneous with the agent without temporal posteriority, then an eternal agent must have an eternal act. Ghazali replies that simultaneity does not negate origination—the act can be originated even if coexistent.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ghazali's position on the agent's relation
Ghazali states that for him, the agent is related to the act only at the moment of origination, not afterward.
2
Understand the philosophers' inference
The philosophers argue: if the act can be simultaneous with the agent, and the agent is eternal, then the act must be eternal too.
This is a logical move based on their assumption of eternal agency.
3
Grasp Ghazali's final reply
Ghazali denies that simultaneity implies eternity; the act can be originated yet simultaneous with the agent.
This preserves the idea that the world is created even if it coexists with God from eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: من أوجه بطلان قولهم: إن الله فاعل العالم وصانعه، من جهة الفعل نفسه. لأن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث، والعالم عندهم قديم وليس بحادث. ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود، بإحداثه. قالت الفلاسفة: إن معنى الحادث الموجود بعد عدم، ولا أثر للفاعل في العدم السابق، ولا في مجموع الوجود والعدم، فبقى أن أثره الوجود، ولا مانع أن يكون هذا الأثر قديما، ولا يستشكل على هذا بأن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده؛ لأنه إن أريد أنه لا يستأنف له وجود، فصحيح؛ ولا يدعي الفلاسفة ذلك. وإن أريد أنه في حال كونه موجوداً لا يكون موجوداً بموجد، فغير صحيح؛ لأن الإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً، وكون المفعول موجوداً، والموجود الممكن في كل آنات وجوده محتاج إلى من يمسك عليه الوجود. قال الغزالي: إن الفاعل يتعلق بالفعل عندنا في حال الحدوث فقط، لا في ثاني حال الحدوث. قالت الفلاسفة: اعترفتم بجواز كون الفعل مع الفاعل غير متأخر عنه، فيلزم منه أن يكون الفعل قديماً إن كان الفاعل قديما. قال الغزالي: لا نحيل أن يكون الفعل مع الفاعل بعد كونه حادثاً.
The second aspect: from the aspects of the falsity of their saying that God is the agent (fā‛il) and maker (ṣāni‛) of the world, from the perspective of the act itself. For the act (fi‛l) is an expression for bringing into existence (iḥdāth). The world according to them is eternal (qadīm) and not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of act is bringing a thing out of non-existence into existence by bringing it into existence. The philosophers said: The meaning of originated (ḥādith) is that which exists after non-existence. The agent has no effect in the prior non-existence, nor in the aggregate of existence and non-existence. So it remains that His effect is existence. There is no obstacle to this effect being eternal. And there is no difficulty in this because the existent cannot be brought into existence—if what is meant is that it does not have existence initiated anew, that is correct, and the philosophers do not claim that. But if what is meant is that while it exists it is not existent by an existentiator, that is false; because bringing into existence (ījād) is concomitant with the agent being an existentiator and the effect being existent, and the possible existent at every moment of its existence is in need of one who maintains its existence. Al-Ghazali said: The agent is related to the act according to us only at the moment of origination (ḥudūth), not at the second moment after origination. The philosophers said: You have conceded the permissibility of the act being simultaneous with the agent not posterior to it. It follows that the act is eternal if the agent is eternal. Al-Ghazali said: We do not deem it impossible that the act is simultaneous with the agent after it has become originated.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is the second argument—from the perspective of the act itself—that the world cannot be God's act on the philosophers' own principles. Ghazali begins by defining act (fi‛l) as bringing into existence (iḥdāth), which means bringing something from non-existence to existence. Since the philosophers hold the world to be eternal (qadīm) and not originated (ḥādith), the world cannot be an act in this sense. The philosophers respond by redefining the originated as 'that which exists after non-existence' and argue that the agent's effect is merely existence, which can be eternal. They then address an objection: that an existent cannot be brought into existence. They clarify: if this means it does not receive existence anew, that is fine, as they do not claim origination; but if it means that an existent does not need an existentiator while existing, that is false, because a possible being needs a sustainer at every moment. Ghazali then counters: according to the Ash‛arites, the agent's relation to the act is only at the moment of origination, not afterward. The philosophers object: if the act can be simultaneous with the agent without temporal posteriority, then if the agent is eternal, the act must be eternal. Ghazali replies that they do not consider it impossible for the act to be simultaneous with the agent after it has become originated—meaning the act is originated even if coexistent. Thus Ghazali maintains that true acts must be originated, and the philosophers' eternal world cannot be an act.

إحداث
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if X exists as long as God exists, then X is eternal.
Correction: Ghazali argues that existence can be originated even if it is simultaneous with an eternal agent.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's final reply to the philosophers' objection about simultaneity?
A) That the act must be eternal if the agent is eternal.
B) That the act can be originated yet simultaneous with the agent.
C) That the agent cannot be eternal.
D) That the act is not simultaneous at all.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali rejects the inference from simultaneity to eternity; an act can be originated yet coexist with an eternal agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The philosophers argued that if something is always with God, it must be eternal. But Ghazali says it can still be new. Is there something in your life—a habit, a relationship, a belief—that you have assumed must be permanent just because it has always been there? Are you willing to see it as a fresh origination from God every moment?
?
You are reading this lesson now. Can you sense that your very consciousness and ability to reason are being created anew by the Eternal Agent at this instant? What would it mean to respond to that realization with gratitude and surrender, not just intellectually but in your next action?

Third Aspect: Emanation from the Simple First Principle
Ghazali argues the composite world cannot emanate from a simple God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the composite world cannot emanate from a simple First Principle, and why he rejects the philosophers' appeal to intermediary intellects as arbitrary.

Core Idea
One cannot derive multiplicity from absolute unity.

In this passage, Ghazali presents his third argument against the philosophers' emanation theory. He starts with their own principle that from the One only one can proceed, and then points out that the world is composite, so it cannot come directly from God. The philosophers offer an elaborate scheme of multiple emanations from the first intellect, but Ghazali dismisses this as arbitrary and unproven.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ghazali's core objection
Read the opening lines where Ghazali states the philosophers' principle and draws the conclusion that the composite world cannot proceed from a simple God.
Focus on the logical structure: premise → conclusion.
2
Understand the philosophers' response
The philosophers introduce the concept of a first intellect that proceeds directly from God, and through which multiplicity arises. Note how they explain the multiplication.
Pay attention to the three aspects of the first intellect.
3
Analyze Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali labels the philosophers' explanation as 'arbitrary judgments' (taḥakkumāt). Consider why he finds it unconvincing and what he means by 'layers of darkness'.
Think about the criteria for a valid rational explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث، من أوجه استحالة كون العالم فعلا لله على أصول الفلاسفة، من جهة نسبة العالم إلى الله. إن المبدأ الأول واحد من كل وجه عند الفلاسفة، والواحد من كل وجه لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد عندهم، والعالم مركب من مختلفات، فلا يمكن صدوره عن الله. قالت الفلاسفة: العالم بجملته ليس صادراً من الله تعالى بالمباشرة، بل الصادر منه موجود واحد، هو أول المخلوقات، وهو عقل مجرد، وتكثرت الموجودات بوساطته. قال الغزالي: فيلزم أن يكون العالم سلسلة آحاد لا تركيب فيها: السابق منها علة للاحق، والمتأخر معلول للمتقدم. فماذا يقولون في الجسم الذي هو مركب من هيولى وصورة؟ قالت الفلاسفة: إذا عرف مذهبنا اندفع الإشكال، فإن مذهبنا أن الصادر الأول له ثلاث جهات: أنه يعقل مبدأه، ويعقل نفسه، وهو باعتبار ذاته ممكن، وعن كل جهة من هذه الجهات نشأ شيء، وهي بداية تكثر الموجودات. قال الغزالي: هذه تحكمات، وهي على التحقيق ظلمات فوق ظلمات.
The third aspect, from the aspects of the impossibility of the world being an act of God according to the principles of the philosophers, from the perspective of the relation of the world to God. The First Principle according to the philosophers is one from every aspect, and from what is one from every aspect only one proceeds according to them. The world is composite of various things, so it cannot proceed from God. The philosophers said: The world as a whole does not proceed from God directly; rather, what proceeds from Him is one existent, which is the first of created things, and it is a separate intellect (‛aql mujarrad). By its mediation, existents became multiple. Al-Ghazali said: Then it follows that the world is a chain of individuals with no composition in it: the prior is a cause of the posterior, and the posterior is an effect of the prior. So what do they say about the body (jism) which is composed of matter (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra)? The philosophers said: If our doctrine is known, the difficulty is removed. For our doctrine is that the first proceeding (al-ṣādir al-awwal) has three aspects: it intellects its principle, it intellects itself, and it is in itself possible (mumkin). From each of these aspects, something arose, and that is the beginning of the multiplication of existents. Al-Ghazali said: These are arbitrary judgments (taḥakkumāt), and they are in truth layers of darkness upon darkness.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is the third argument—from the perspective of the relation of the world to God—that the world cannot be God's act on the philosophers' own principles. Ghazali begins by stating the philosophers' principle that the First Principle is one from every aspect, and from what is one from every aspect only one thing can proceed. Since the world is composite of diverse things, it cannot proceed directly from God. The philosophers respond by explaining their emanation scheme: the world as a whole does not proceed directly from God; rather, a single existent—the first intellect—proceeds directly, and the rest of existents multiply through its mediation. Ghazali then points out a consequence: if the world is a chain of singular causes and effects, there is no room for composition. He asks specifically about bodies, which are composed of matter (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra). The philosophers reply that when their doctrine is understood, the difficulty disappears. They explain that the first proceeding (the first intellect) has three aspects: it intellects its principle (God), it intellects itself, and it is in itself possible. From each of these aspects, a different being arises, and this is the beginning of multiplicity. Ghazali dismisses this explanation as arbitrary judgments (taḥakkumāt) and declares them to be layers of darkness upon darkness.

واحد من كل وجه
عقل مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' emanation scheme through intermediary intellects provides a rational explanation for the multiplicity of the world.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the scheme is not rationally necessary but an arbitrary imposition (taḥakkumāt). The three aspects of the first intellect are not proven to cause specific effects; thus the explanation is no better than 'layers of darkness'.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what proceeds directly from the First Principle?
A) The entire world.
B) The first intellect, which is a separate intellect.
C) The celestial spheres.
D) Matter and form together.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' attempt to derive multiplicity from a simple First Principle through an intermediary intellect is rejected by Ghazali as a set of unproven and arbitrary claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about how God creates the world, do you rely on logical schemes that seem neat, or do you accept that His ways are beyond human comprehension? This lesson shows that even the philosophers' elaborate scheme is rejected as 'layers of darkness'. Is there any part of your aqeedah that rests on human reasoning rather than clear revelation? Identify one such belief and commit to replacing it with what the Quran and Sunnah explicitly teach.
?
The philosophers thought they had solved the problem of multiplicity by inventing a first intellect with three aspects. But Ghazali called this arbitrary. In your own life, do you ever 'fill in the gaps' of your understanding of God with your own intellectual constructs? Read the source again: 'layers of darkness upon darkness.' Will you abandon all pretensions of rationally deriving God's actions and submit to the mystery of His sheer, simple unity?

The Philosophers' Eternal Act Argument and Ghazali's Response
Ghazali debates the philosophers over whether an eternal agent must produce an eternal act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that an eternal agent necessitates an eternal act and Ghazali's critical response, including the redefinition of 'act' and the rebuttal.

Core Idea
Eternal cause does not force eternal effect; temporal acts can coexist with eternal agents.

In this passage, Ghazali engages with the philosophers' claim that if an act is simultaneous with its agent, then an eternal agent must have an eternal act. Ghazali challenges this by allowing temporal acts to be simultaneous with an eternal agent. The philosophers then clarify that by 'act' they mean perpetual causal relation, not origination in time. Ghazali exposes that this redefinition empties the term of its real meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' argument
Read their initial claim that if act is simultaneous with agent, then eternal agent implies eternal act.
Note the logical structure of their argument.
2
See Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali accepts simultaneity but insists the act can be temporal, meaning it occurs at a specific time.
He does not reject the premise of simultaneity.
3
Grasp the philosophers' clarification
They redefine 'act' as a perpetual relation of effect to cause, not a temporal production.
They argue terminology is secondary to meaning.
4
Analyze Ghazali's counter
Ghazali argues that this is not a genuine act but merely a courtesy term used to appease Muslims.
He insists on the real meaning of 'act' as temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : اعترفتم بجواز كون الفعل مع الفاعل غير متأخر عنه ، فيلزم منه أن يكون الفعل قديماً إن كان الفاعل قديما قال الغزالي : لا نحيل أن يكون الفعل مع الفاعل بعد كونه حادثاً قالت الفلاسفة : إنا لا نعنى يكون العالم فعلا الله إلا كونه معلولا دائم النسبة إلى الله تعالى ؛ فإن لم تسموا هذا فعلا، فلا مضايقة في التسمية بعد ظهور المعنى قال الغزالي : إن غرضنا أن نظهر أن العالم ليس فعل الله تحقيقاً عندكم ، وأنكم إنما تسمونه فعلا تجملا بالإسلاميين
The philosophers said: You have admitted the permissibility of the act being with the agent not posterior to it, so it follows from this that the act is eternal if the agent is eternal. Al-Ghazali said: We do not rule out that the act is with the agent after it occurs as temporal. The philosophers said: By 'the world is an act of God' we mean nothing except that it is an effect perpetually related to God, the Exalted; if you do not call this an act, there is no harm in terminology after the meaning is clear. Al-Ghazali said: Our purpose is to show that the world is not truly an act of God according to you, and that you only call it an act as a courtesy to the Muslims.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is an exchange between the philosophers and Ghazali over the nature of the world as an act of God. The philosophers argue: since you (theologians) admit that an act can be simultaneous with its agent (not posterior), then if the agent is eternal, the act must also be eternal. Ghazali responds: we do not reject that the act is with the agent, but it occurs as temporal (i.e., it comes into existence at a specific time, not eternally). The philosophers then clarify their position: by 'act' they only mean a perpetual relation of effect to cause, not a temporal production. If you refuse to call this 'act', then the terminology is irrelevant once the meaning is understood. Ghazali retorts: our aim is to demonstrate that, according to your principles, the world is not truly God's act in the real sense; you merely use the term 'act' as a concession to Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an agent is eternal, its act must also be eternal.
Correction: An act can be temporal yet simultaneous with the eternal agent in the sense of causal relation, not temporal coexistence.

Checkpoint

A) That God temporally created the world after it was not
B) That the world is an effect perpetually related to God
C) That the world is composed of atoms and accidents
D) That the world is a necessary emanation from God

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that an eternal agent produces an eternal act is rejected by Ghazali, who maintains that temporal acts can proceed from an eternal agent and that the philosophers' redefinition of 'act' is inadequate.

?
If you truly believed that God's act of creation is a temporal, free, and willful event—not a necessary emanation—how would your sense of gratitude, awe, and accountability be different right now? What one step can you take this week to live out that belief?

The Third Argument: From Oneness to Emanation
Ghazali presents the philosophers' emanation theory and his dismissal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third argument against the philosophers' emanation theory, their response, and his critique of the three-aspects explanation.

Core Idea
From the absolutely one, only one can proceed—so how can a diverse world arise?

This lesson examines Ghazali's third argument, which challenges the philosophers' claim that the world emanates from God. He argues that if the First Principle is absolutely one, only one being can directly proceed from it, yet the world is full of diversity. The philosophers respond with a sophisticated emanation scheme, but Ghazali dismisses it as arbitrary and incoherent. We will follow the exchange step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali's argument, philosophers' response, Ghazali's objection, philosophers' elaboration, Ghazali's final dismissal.
2
Identify the core problem
The central issue: from the absolutely one, only one can proceed. How then does the diverse world exist? The philosophers claim it happens through a chain of intellects.
3
Examine the philosophers' three-aspects theory
The philosophers explain multiplicity arises from the first intellect having three aspects: knowing its principle, knowing itself, and being possible. From each, a different being originates. Ask yourself: is this explanation convincing?
Note the technical term: 'جهات' (aspects).
4
Consider Ghazali's dismissal
Ghazali calls these 'arbitrary judgments' and 'layers of darkness'. Reflect on why he finds this explanation unsatisfactory and what it reveals about his overall critique.
This is a strong rhetorical move by Ghazali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث ، من أوجه استحالة كون العالم فعلا الله على أصول الفلاسفة ، من جهة نسبة العالم إلى الله إن المبدأ الأول واحد من كل وجه عند الفلاسفة ، والواحد من كل وجه لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد عندهم ، والعالم مركب من مختلفات ، فلا يمكن صدوره عن الله قالت الفلاسفة : العالم بجملته ليس صادراً من الله تعالى بالمباشرة بل الصادر منه موجود واحد ، هو أول المخلوقات ، وهو عقل مجرد ، وتكثرت الموجودات بوساطته قال الغزالي : فيلزم أن يكون العالم سلسلة آحاد لا تركب فيها : السابق منها علة للاحق، والمتأخر معلول للمتقدم ، فماذا يقولون في الجسم الذي هو مركب من هيولى وصوة ؟ قالت الفلاسفة : إذا عرف مذهبنا اندفع الإشكال، فإن مذهبنا أن الصادر الأول له ثلاث جهات : أنه يعقل مبدأه ، ويعقل نفسه، وهو باعتبار ذاته ممكن ، وعن كل جهة من هذه الجهات نشأ ء شيء، وهى بداية تكثر الموجودات قال الغزالي : هذه تحكمات ، وهى على التحقيق ظلمات فوق ظلمات ، لو حكاها الإنسان عن منام رآه ، لاستدل به على سوء مزاجه
The third aspect, from the aspects of the impossibility of the world being an act of God according to the principles of the philosophers, from the perspective of the relation of the world to God. The First Principle is one in every respect according to the philosophers, and from the one in every respect only one can issue according to them, yet the world is composed of diverse things, so its issuance from God is impossible. The philosophers said: The entire world does not issue from God, the Exalted, directly; rather, only one being issues from Him, which is the first of created things, and it is a pure intellect, and the existents multiply through its mediation. Al-Ghazali said: So it follows necessarily that the world would be a chain of indivisible unities with no composition: the prior among them is a cause of the posterior, and the posterior is an effect of the prior. What then do they say about the body which is composed of matter and form? The philosophers said: If our doctrine is known, the problem is removed, for our doctrine is that the first emanation has three aspects: it intellects its principle, it intellects itself, and in respect of its essence it is possible; and from each of these aspects a thing arises, and this is the beginning of the multiplication of existents. Al-Ghazali said: These are arbitrary judgments, and in truth they are layers of darkness upon layers of darkness; if one were to relate them from a dream he saw, it would indicate a disturbed temperament.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's third argument against the possibility of the world being God's act on the philosophers' principles, focusing on the relation of the world to God. The argument runs: The First Principle is absolutely one; from the absolutely one only one can proceed; but the world is composed of many different things, so it cannot proceed directly from God. The philosophers respond by explaining their emanation theory: the first emanation is a single intellect, not the entire world; multiplicity arises through that intellect. Ghazali objects: this would make the world a chain of simple, indivisible entities, each being cause of the next, none composite. But what about the body, which is obviously composite of matter and form? The philosophers then elaborate: the first emanation (the first intellect) has three aspects: (1) it knows its principle, (2) it knows itself, (3) it is possible in itself. From each aspect a different being emerges, introducing multiplicity. Ghazali dismisses all this as arbitrary contrivances, calling them 'layers of darkness upon layers of darkness' and says such claims sound like the delusions of a disturbed person.

Key Terms
المبدأ الأول
واحد من كل وجه
الصادر الأول
العقل المجرد
جهات
إمكان
تحكمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is rejecting all forms of emanation from God.
Correction: Ghazali is specifically critiquing the philosophers' arbitrary account of how multiplicity arises from the first intellect. He does not necessarily reject any causal relation, but he shows that their explanation is incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the philosophers' response to Ghazali's objection that from one only one can proceed?
A) They deny that the first principle is one
B) They claim multiplicity arises from three aspects of the first intellect
C) They say the world is created directly by God
D) They argue that the body is not composite

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's third argument shows that the philosophers' emanation theory, when pressed, resorts to arbitrary distinctions to explain multiplicity, which undermines its rational coherence.

The Problem of Possibility and Existence in the First Emanation
Ghazali questions the identity of possibility and existence in the first emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the distinction between possibility and existence in the first emanation leads to contradictions in the philosophers' system.

Core Idea
If possibility and existence come from different sources, how can they be one?

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' emanation scheme by questioning the status of the first emanation's possibility. Is the possibility of its existence the same as its existence or different? The philosophers' own assumptions force a dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the question Ghazali poses about the first emanation: is its possibility identical to its existence or other than it?
Notice that Ghazali presents the objection concisely.
2
Follow the hypothetical reply
Identify the hypothetical response: the philosophers might say possibility belongs to the emanation from itself, while existence comes from another. Ask yourself how this would make them one?
3
See the counter
Notice how Ghazali turns the logic back on the philosophers by appealing to their own claim about the Necessary Being: they say necessity of existence is identical to existence. If so, why can't possibility and existence be identical in the first emanation?
This reveals an inconsistency in their position.

Source Text

ثم قال : ومداخل الاعتراض على مثلها لا تنحصر منها : كونه ممكن الوجود ، عين وجوده ، أم غيره ؟ . . فإن قيل : إمكان الوجود له من ذاته ، ووجوده من غيره ، فكيف يكون ماله من ذاته، وماله من غيره واحداً . . ؟ قلنا : فكيف قلتم : إن وجوب الوجود ، هو عين الوجود ؟
Then he said: The avenues of objection to such things are not limited; among them: its being possible of existence — is it identical to its existence or other than it? ... If it is said: the possibility of existence belongs to it from itself, and its existence from another, then how can what belongs to it from itself and what belongs to it from another be one? ... We say: then how can you say that the necessity of existence is identical to existence?
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is an objection raised by Ghazali against the philosophers' account of the first emanation. He asks: regarding the first emanation's being possible in existence (imkan al-wujud), is this possibility the same as its existence or different? If one replies that possibility belongs to it from itself while existence comes from another, then how can two different sources produce one and the same thing? Ghazali then turns the argument back on the philosophers: if they say that for the Necessary Being, necessity of existence is identical to existence, then this same logic would apply to the first emanation, leading to contradiction. The objection targets the coherence of the distinction between essence and existence.

Ghazali's objection shows that the philosophers' distinction between possibility and existence in the first emanation leads to an inconsistency when compared to their own doctrine about the Necessary Being.

Ghazali's Dilemma on Intellection and Essence
Ghazali argues that if the first effect's knowledge is identical to its essence, it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma Ghazali poses to the philosophers regarding the identity of intellection and essence in the first effect, and how it undermines their doctrine.

Core Idea
A simple dilemma: if knowledge is identical to essence, absurdity; if different, the same applies to God.

This lesson presents a razor-sharp objection from Ghazali's Tahafut. The philosophers claim the first effect knows itself, but they face a dilemma about whether that knowledge is identical to its essence. Ghazali forces them into an impossible choice, revealing a contradiction in their system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, argumentative style of Ghazali.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two options: (1) the intellection is identical to the essence, (2) it is other. Find where Ghazali explains each consequence.
The dilemma is stated in a question form.
3
Understand why this dilemma is fatal
Ghazali argues that both options lead to a problem for the philosophers. The first is impossible, and the second forces an unwanted conclusion about God. Reflect on why this is a strong objection.
This connects to the broader critique of emanationist metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : أن عقل المعلول الأول ذات نفسه ، عين ذاته ، أو غيره؟ فإن قيل عينه ، فذلك محال ؛ لأن العلم غير المعلوم ؛ وإن قيل غيره ، فليكن كذلك في المبدأ الأول
And among them: That the intellection of the first effect of its own self — is it identical to its essence or other? If it is said identical, that is impossible, because knowledge is not the known; and if it is said other, then let it be likewise in the First Principle.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This is a concise objection. Ghazali asks about the first effect (the first intellect): is its intellection of its own self identical to its essence or different? If identical, that is absurd, for knowledge (al-ilm) is not the same as the known object (al-malum). If different, then the same principle should apply to the First Principle, meaning God's knowledge of Himself is distinct from His essence, which the philosophers would reject. So the philosophers are caught in a dilemma.

Key Terms
عقل
ذات
العلم غير المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the first effect knows itself, that knowledge must be identical to its essence.
Correction: Ghazali shows that knowledge is distinct from the known, so identity is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) That is impossible because knowledge is not the known
C) That would make it eternal
D) That would mean it is necessary

Key Takeaway
The dilemma demonstrates that the philosophers are forced into a contradiction on the nature of the first effect's self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The dilemma shows that knowledge is distinct from its object. In your relationship with God, are you content with knowledge about Him, or are you seeking to know Him directly? What does this distinction mean for your spiritual journey?

Objection: Three Aspects Insufficient for the Heavens
Ghazali argues that three aspects cannot produce the composite heavens.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' three-aspect theory cannot account for the intricate composition and specific features of the heavens.

Core Idea
The composite heavens demand more than three aspects to explain their features.

In this final objection, Ghazali targets the heart of the philosophers' emanation scheme. They claim that from the first intellect, three aspects produce the first heaven. But Ghazali points out that the first heaven is far more complex—it has form, matter, a specific size, and even the poles occupy particular positions. These require additional specifiers, showing the inadequacy of the triplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's objection carefully
Read the passage and its translation. Note the two specific features Ghazali highlights.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two reasons
Ghazali gives two separate features of the heavens that necessitate a specifier beyond the three aspects. Find them.
One is about the composition and size, the other about the poles.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Ask yourself: If the philosophers cannot explain these features, what does that mean for their theory of emanation? This objection reinforces the need for a Creator who actively specifies.
This connects to the broader theme of the Tahafut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : أن التثليث فى المعلول الأول لا يكفى : فإن السماء الأولى اعتبرت عن جهة واحدة فيه مع أنها مركبة من : صورة ، وهيولى ، وهي على حد مخصوص فى الكبر . . . وهذا يقتضى مخصصاً ... ثم إن اختصاص نقطة القطبين بمكانهما يقتضى مخصصا.

This necessitates a specifier... Furthermore, the specification of the point of the two poles in their position necessitates a specifier.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
In this final objection, Ghazali points out that the philosophers' theory, which derives multiplicity from three aspects of the first intellect, cannot account for the actual composition of the heavens. The first heaven, they claim, arises from one aspect of the first effect, but in reality the heaven is composite (form and matter) and has a specific size, requiring a specifier (mukhassis). Moreover, the location of the celestial poles (the axis points) also needs a specifier. Thus, the three aspects are insufficient to explain the complex features of the physical universe.

Correction: Ghazali shows that the heavens have composite structure, specific size, and specific pole locations that require additional specifiers, proving the three aspects are insufficient.

The First Heaven's Composition Requires a Specifier
Ghazali objects that the first heaven's composition requires a specifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the trinity of emanation from the first intellect cannot account for the specific features of the first heaven, and why a specifier is necessary.

Core Idea
Composition demands a specifier beyond simple emanation.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' emanation scheme by focusing on the first heaven. He argues that its composition—form, matter, and specific magnitude—cannot arise from a mere threefold emanation. This leads to the need for an external specifier, which undermines the sufficiency of the philosophers' causal chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Ghazali's objection about the insufficiency of the trinity.
Notice the key terms: 'tathlith' (trinity), 'ma'lul awwal' (first caused), 'mukhaṣṣiṣ' (specifier).
2
Identify the components of the first heaven
Ghazali lists the components: form, arising (nash'ah?), and hyle (matter), plus a specific magnitude and the poles' positions.
Consider why these details matter.
3
Understand why these require a specifier
The emanation from the first intellect is simple and uniform, but the first heaven has many contingent features. Each feature needs a reason for being as it is, which cannot come from a simple emanation.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Reflect on the philosophical impact
If a specifier is needed, then the emanation scheme is incomplete. This weakens the philosophers' claim of a necessary causal chain.
Connect to the broader debate in Tahafut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : أن التثليث فى المعلول الأول لا يكفى : فإن السماء الأولى اعتبرت عن جهة واحدة فيه مع أنها مركبة من : صورة ، ناشئة وهيولى ، وهي على حد مخصوص فى الكبر . . . وهذا يقتضى مخصصاً ... ثم إن اختصاص نقطة القطبين بمكانهما يقتضى مخصصا. فإن قالت الفلاسفة : ألعل فى المبدأ أنواعاً من الكثرة ظهر لنا منها ثلاثة أو أربعة والباقى لم نطلع عليه . . . قيل : فقولوا إذن : إن الموجودات كلها صدرت من المعلول الأول
And among them: That the trinity (threefold emanation) in the first caused is not sufficient: for the first heaven was considered from one aspect in it, while it is composed of form, arising (nash'ah?) and hyle, and it is upon a specific measure in magnitude... And this necessitates a specifier. Then, the specification of the position of the two poles requires a specifier. Then if the philosophers said: Perhaps in the First Principle there are types of multiplicity, of which three or four have appeared to us, and the rest we have not come to know. It was said: Then say, therefore, that all existents emanated from the first caused.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage combines two objections. First, the emanation of three things from the first caused intellect (the 'trinity') is insufficient to account for the composition of the first heaven. The first heaven is composite, having form, matter, and specific magnitude, and it requires a specifier to determine its particular characteristics, such as the location of the celestial poles. This specifier cannot be explained by the simple emanation scheme. The philosophers respond by suggesting that the First Principle may contain many types of multiplicity beyond the three or four we know, thus potentially explaining the details. Ghazali's retort is that if that is the case, then they should simply say that all existents emanate directly from the first caused, rendering their emanation hierarchy unnecessary.

سماء أولى
مخصص
قطبين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The threefold emanation is sufficient to explain the entire universe, including the first heaven.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the first heaven's contingent details (magnitude, pole positions) require a specifier, which the simple emanation cannot provide.

Checkpoint

A) Its motion
B) Its composition and the positions of its poles
C) Its light
D) Its name

Key Takeaway
The first heaven's contingent features prove that mere emanation cannot account for its existence; a specifier is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hidden Multiplicities and Ghazali's Retort
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' appeal to hidden multiplicities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' potential defense—that the First Principle may have hidden multiplicities—and Ghazali's retort that this would render the emanation hierarchy unnecessary.

Core Idea
If hidden multiplicities explain everything, why not skip the middle causes?

Ghazali anticipates the philosophers' response: perhaps the First Principle contains many kinds of multiplicity beyond the known three or four, which could account for the first heaven's details. He counters that if that were true, then all existents could emanate directly from the first caused, undermining the entire hierarchical scheme.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' hypothetical response
Read the portion of the passage where the philosophers suggest hidden multiplicities in the First Principle.
Focus on 'al-mabda' (the First Principle).
2
Analyze Ghazali's retort
Ghazali says: if there are hidden multiplicities, then say everything emanates from the first caused. Why the intermediate hierarchy?
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the logical force
Ghazali's retort shows that the philosophers' appeal to hidden multiplicities is ad hoc and makes their emanation scheme redundant.

Objection on the Similarity Between the First Caused and a Human
Ghazali questions why the same emanative effects do not occur in a human as in the first caused

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the philosophers' attributes for emanation in the first caused should also produce emanation in humans, revealing an inconsistency.

Core Idea
Same attributes must produce same effects—unless the philosophers are inconsistent.

This passage is a classic example of Ghazali's method of exposing internal contradictions in the philosophers' system. He takes their own reasoning and applies it to a human, showing that if the attributes are sufficient for emanation, then every human should produce effects. The burden is on the philosophers to explain the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers say the first caused emanates three things due to three aspects: possibility, self-knowledge, and knowledge of its principle. Read the Arabic to find these aspects.
The three aspects are explicitly listed.
2
Spot the analogy
Notice that Ghazali points out that a human also has possibility, self-knowledge, and knowledge of God. He asks why the same emanation does not occur.
This is an argument by parity of reasoning.
3
Reflect on the force of the objection
Ask yourself: If the attributes are identical, why would the effects differ? The philosophers would need to introduce an extra factor, but they haven't.
The objection reveals an inconsistency in the philosophers' system.
4
Consider a possible response
Think about how a philosopher might reply—perhaps by saying the first caused is a pure intellect while humans are not. Does that answer satisfy? Ghazali anticipates such moves.
The commentary hints at this.

Source Text

Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful analogy. Ghazali points out that a human being also is possible (contingent), knows himself, and knows his principle (God). So why does not the same emanation occur from a human? If the attributes are the same, the effect should be the same. This exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' reasoning: they arbitrarily attribute creative power to the first caused while denying it to humans, despite shared attributes.

Ghazali's Objection to the Philosophers on Infinite Regress
Ghazali questions why regress in causes is impossible while regress in temporal events is allowed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' distinction between essential and accidental infinite regress and why he considers their position arbitrary.

Core Idea
Ghazali exposes inconsistency: why allow infinite regress in time but not in causes?

This passage jumps into the heart of Ghazali's attack on the philosophers' causal argument for God's existence. He challenges their core distinction between two types of infinite regress—one impossible, the other possible. Using their own theory of time and a surprising example about human souls, Ghazali argues that their position is not rationally grounded but arbitrary. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's opening objection
Start with Ghazali's first move: he asks why causes cannot go back infinitely, just like time has no beginning.
Notice the analogy with time—he uses the philosophers' own view of time against them.
2
Understand the philosophers' distinction
The philosophers respond by distinguishing regress in causes (essential order) from regress in events (accidental order). Only the former is impossible because coexisting ordered infinite is absurd.
This is their defense—memorize it.
3
Follow Ghazali's counter-argument about souls
Ghazali hits back with human souls: they are infinite in number and coexist simultaneously according to the philosophers. Why isn't that impossible?
Souls are a strong test case because they have no order (tartib).
4
See the philosophers' reply and Ghazali's dismissal
The philosophers try to salvage their position by saying souls have no order. Ghazali calls this arbitrary—a mere assertion without proof.
This is the key move: if order is the criterion, prove it, don't just assert it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي : لم يبعد أن تكون العلل ذاهبة في جانب الماضي إلى غير نهاية ، من غير وقوف عند علة أخيرة ؟ فكما أن الزمان عندكم له آخر وهو الآن الراهن ، ولكن لا أول له ، فما من وقت إلا وقبله وقت، فلتكن الموجودات عللا ومعلولات هكذا ، لا أول لها من جهة الماضي فإن قال الفلاسفة : نفرق بين التسلسل فى العلل ، والتسلسل في الحوادث ، والأول هو الممنوع ، والثاني ليس بممنوع ؛ لأن الحوادث ليست موجودة معاً فى الحال ، ولا في بعض الأحوال، والمعدوم لا يوصف بالتناهي ، ولا بعدم التناهي قيل لهم : فما تقولون فى النفوس البشرية ، وهى مجتمعة معاً في الوجود وهى غير متناهية عندكم ؟ فإن قال الفلاسفة : النفوس ليس بينها ترتب ، والمحال اجتماع موجودات لا نهاية لها إذا كان بينها ترتب قال لهم الغزالي : هذا التحكم ليس طرده أولى من عكسه
Al-Ghazali said: Why is it far-fetched that causes go back into the past without end, without stopping at a final cause? Just as, according to you, time has an end, namely the present moment, but has no beginning—for there is no time except that a time preceded it—so let existents be causes and effects in this manner, with no beginning on the side of the past. If the philosophers say: We distinguish between regress in causes and regress in events; the first is prohibited, the second is not prohibited because events are not present together at the present moment, nor at any moment, and non-existence is not described as finite or infinite. They were asked: What do you say about human souls? They are present together in existence and are, according to you, infinite? If the philosophers say: There is no order among souls, and it is impossible for infinite existents to coexist if there is order between them. Al-Ghazali said to them: This is arbitrary; its consistent application is no more valid than its opposite.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. Ghazali first objects to the philosophers' claim that infinite regress of causes is impossible. He draws an analogy with time: according to the philosophers, time is eternal (has no beginning) but has an end (the present). So if time can be infinite in one direction, why cannot causes also extend infinitely into the past without a first cause? The philosophers respond by distinguishing two types of regress: regress in causes (essential order) and regress in temporal events (accidental order). The former is impossible because it involves simultaneously existing ordered causes; the latter is possible because events do not coexist. Ghazali then counter-questions about human souls: the philosophers believe souls are infinite in number and yet they all exist simultaneously in the present. The philosophers reply that souls have no essential order (tartib), and the impossibility of an infinite simultaneous series only applies when there is order. Ghazali dismisses this as arbitrary (تحكم)—a mere assertion without proof, and thus unconvincing.

الحوادث
النفوس البشرية
الترتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali believes infinite regress is possible.
Correction: Ghazali does not affirm infinite regress; he attacks the philosophers' inconsistent application of the principle.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers claim that infinite regress of causes is impossible but infinite regress of events is possible?
A) Causes are simultaneous while events are sequential
B) Causes are eternal while events are temporal
C) Causes are one-to-one while events are many
D) Causes are mental while events are physical

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection shows that the philosophers' distinction between essential and accidental infinite regress is not rationally justified but is an arbitrary assertion.

?
Ghazali calls the philosophers' distinction 'arbitrary.' Is there an area of your faith or life where you cling to a distinction that protects a comfortable belief but cannot be rationally defended? Will you have the courage to test it and follow the truth wherever it leads?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Philosophers' Infinite Regress Argument
Ghazali responds to the philosophers' argument that they do not need an infinite series to prove the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that they can avoid infinite regress in proving the Necessary Existent, and why their own doctrine of eternal time undermines their argument.

Core Idea
Accepting eternal time commits you to accepting an infinite chain of causes.

Ghazali's debate with the philosophers on causality and infinite regress is a classic moment in Islamic thought. The philosophers argue they can prove the Creator without an infinite series by analyzing each cause as either necessary or possible. Ghazali uncovers a flaw in their reasoning and shows that their own cosmological commitments force them to accept an infinite regress after all. In this lesson, we walk through the key exchange and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' initial argument
The philosophers claim they don't need an infinite regress. They say each cause is either necessary (uncaused) or possible (needs a cause). If all are possible, the whole aggregate needs an external cause, which is the Necessary Existent.
Notice they assume they can avoid infinite regress by this method.
2
Examine Ghazali's redefinition
Ghazali responds by redefining how we apply 'necessary' and 'possible'. He says each individual cause is possible (has a cause), but the whole aggregate might not be possible (does not have an additional cause) because the chain of causes could go on infinitely.
This subtle point challenges the philosophers' assumption.
3
Analyze the philosophers' objection
The philosophers object that this would make the Necessary Existent subsist by impossible possibles (i.e., an infinite chain of dependent beings), which they deem absurd.
They think this is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See Ghazali's analogy and conclusion
Ghazali compares this to the philosophers' own acceptance of eternal time with temporal cycles. He argues that if they allow beginningless temporal events, they cannot deny endless causal chains. Thus their objection fails.
This shows their position is self-contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالت الفلاسفة : لا حاجة بنا إلى استعمال التسلسل في إثبات الصانع ؛ فإن في وسعنا أن نقول : كل واحدة من آحاد العلل ممكنة في نفسها ؟ أو واجبة ؟ فإن كانت واجبة فهى بغير حاجة إلى علة . وإن كانت ممكنة فالكل موصوف بالإمكان ، وكل ممكن فهو بحاجة إلى علة خارجة عن ذاته ، والموجود الخارج عن ذات الممكن ليس إلا الواجب
قال لهم الغزالي : إن أردتم بالواجب ما لا علة لوجوده ، وبالممكن ما لوجوده علة زائدة على ذاته ، فنقول : كل واحد ممكن ، على معنى أن له علة زائدة على ذاته. والكل ليس بممكن ، على معنى أنه ليس له علة زائدة على ذاته
فإن قالت الفلاسفة : هذا يؤدى إلى أن يتقوم واجب الوجود بممكنات محال الوجود ، وهو
قال لهم الغزالي : هذا كقول القائل يستحيل أن يتقوم القديم بالحوادث ، مع أنه واقع عند الفلاسفة ؛ إذ يقولون : الزمان قديم ، وآحاد الدورات حادثة
قال الغزالي : فتبين أن من يجوز حوادث لا أول لها ، لا يتمكن من إنكار علل لا نهاية لها . ويخلص من هذا أنه لا سبيل لهم إلى الوصول إلى إثبات المبدأ الأول
Then the philosophers said: We have no need to use infinite regress in proving the Maker; for it is within our capacity to say: each one of the individual causes is either possible in itself or necessary? If it is necessary, then it is without need of a cause. And if it is possible, then the whole is described by possibility, and every possible is in need of a cause external to its essence, and the existent external to the essence of the possible is nothing but the Necessary. Al-Ghazali said to them: If by 'necessary' you mean that which has no cause for its existence, and by 'possible' that which has a cause additional to its essence, then we say: each individual is possible, in the sense that it has a cause additional to its essence. And the whole is not possible, in the sense that it does not have a cause additional to its essence. Then the philosophers said: This leads to the self-subsistence of the Necessary Existent by impossible possibles, and it is... Al-Ghazali said to them: This is like the saying of one who says it is impossible for the eternal to subsist by temporal things, while according to the philosophers it is actual; for they say: time is eternal, and the individual cycles are temporal. Al-Ghazali said: So it becomes clear that whoever allows temporal events which have no beginning is unable to deny causes which have no end. And from this it follows that they have no way to arrive at proving the First Principle.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between the philosophers and al-Ghazali on the need for an infinite regress in proving the existence of the Creator. The philosophers argue that they do not need to use an infinite series; instead, they can simply examine each individual cause: each is either necessary or possible. If necessary, it needs no cause; if possible, then the whole aggregate of possibilities is also possible and thus requires an external cause, which is the Necessary Existent. Al-Ghazali responds by pointing out a subtlety in their definitions: if by 'necessary' they mean uncaused, and by 'possible' they mean having a cause, then each individual cause is possible (i.e., has a cause), but the whole aggregate might be considered not possible (i.e., does not have an additional cause) because the cause of the whole is the Necessary Existent which is external, but they had defined the whole as also possible? Actually, Ghazali's point is that the totality of possible beings may not itself require a cause beyond the individual causes, because the cause of each individual is something else, and the chain could go back infinitely. The philosophers retort that this would make the Necessary Existent (the ultimate cause) consist of impossible possibles, which is absurd. Ghazali compares this to the philosophers' own acceptance of an eternal time with temporal events, which they consider possible. He concludes that those who accept eternal temporal events (like the cycles of the heavens) cannot logically deny an infinite chain of causes. This refutes their claim that they can avoid infinite regress.

Correction: Ghazali shows that their definition of 'possible' for each individual leads to a problem with the whole, and more importantly, their acceptance of beginningless temporal cycles forces them to accept an infinite chain of causes.

Key Takeaway
Whoever allows temporal events without beginning cannot deny causes without end, thus the philosophers' own cosmology commits them to infinite regress.

In your own search for meaning, do you sometimes settle for immediate explanations (work, family, society) instead of facing the ultimate cause of your existence? What would it look like to confront that dependency today?
?
Ghazali's refutation shows that accepting eternal time forces acceptance of infinite regress. Do you ever live as if the world is eternal and you have unlimited time, ignoring that your time is limited and each moment is a new creation from God?
