The Heavens as an Animal: Reason Cannot Prove It
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' rational proof that the heavens are an animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers cannot rationally prove the heavens are a living animal, and the flaw in their syllogism.

Core Idea
Reason cannot prove the heavens are an animal; only revelation can.

In this passage, Ghazali takes on the philosophers' claim that the heavens are a living animal with a soul. He does not deny the possibility, but he firmly contends that rational proof is insufficient. The philosophers attempt to prove it through a classification of motion, but Ghazali exposes a logical error in their reasoning. This lesson explores the limits of reason in metaphysical matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note Ghazali's stance
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Ghazali first states the philosophers' claim, then gives his response: possibility but no rational proof.
Pay attention to how he distinguishes between what is possible and what is provable.
2
Analyze the philosophers' syllogism
The philosophers argue that the heaven's motion must be either by coercion, nature, or will. Since it is not coercion or nature, it must be will, thus the heaven is an animal. Identify each premise.
Consider why they think nature and coercion are impossible for the heavens.
3
Understand Ghazali's objection
Ghazali says the division into these three categories is false (الحصر باطل). Think about why the categories might not be exhaustive or mutually exclusive. This is the key flaw.
Reflect on whether there could be other types of motion or combinations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٢ - مسألة
في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أن السماء حيوان مطيع الله تعالى بحركته الدورية
قالت الفلاسفة : إن السماء حيوان ، وإن لها نفسا ، نسبتها إلى بدن السماء كنسبة نفوسنا إلى أبداننا
قال الغزالي : ومذهبهم في هذه المسألة مما لا ينكر إمكانه . . ولكنا ندعى عجزهم عن معرفة ذلك بدليل العقل فإن هذا إن كان صحيحاً ، فلا يطلع عليه إلا الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - بإلهام من الله تعالى ، أو بوحى ، وقياس العقل ليس يدل عليه
قالت الفلاسفة : السماء جسم متحرك ، وكل جسم متحرك ، فله محرك والحركة إما أن تكون منبعثة عن ذات المتحرك كالطبيعة في حركة الحجر إلى أسفل ، والإرادة كحركة الحيوان مع القدرة . وإما أن تكون الحركة آتية من خارج ، ويحرك عن طريق القسر كدفع الحجر إلى فوق . و باطل أن تكون حركتها بالقسر أو بالطبع فبقى أن تكون بالإرادة
قال الغزالي : إن الحصر في هذه الأقسام باطل ثم انتهى إلى : قوله : فالحكم على السماء بأنها حيوان تحكم محض لا مستند له
22 - The Question: On their inability to establish the proof that the heaven is an animal obedient to God Almighty through its circular motion. The philosophers said: The heaven is an animal, and it has a soul, whose relation to the body of the heaven is like the relation of our souls to our bodies. Al-Ghazali said: Their doctrine in this question is not denied in its possibility... But we claim their inability to know that by rational proof. For if this is true, it is only known by the prophets – peace be upon them – through inspiration from God Almighty, or revelation, and rational analogy does not indicate it. The philosophers said: The heaven is a moving body, and every moving body has a mover. Movement is either emanating from the essence of the mover, like nature in the motion of a stone downward, or will like the movement of an animal with power. Or the movement comes from outside, and it is moved by way of coercion, like pushing a stone upward. And it is false that its movement is by coercion or by nature, so it remains that it is by will. Al-Ghazali said: The exclusive division into these categories is false. Then he concluded: His statement: So the judgment that the heaven is an animal is a pure judgment with no basis.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage introduces the twenty-second question (مسألة) of the Tahafut, which challenges the philosophers' ability to prove that the heaven is a living animal obedient to God through its circular motion. The philosophers claim that the heaven is an animal with a soul analogous to our souls. Ghazali does not deny the possibility but argues that reason cannot prove it; only prophets can know such things through inspiration or revelation. The philosophers then present a proof: the heaven is a moving body, so it must have a mover. The mover's action is either from within (by nature or will) or from outside (by coercion). They argue it cannot be coercion or nature, so it must be will, hence the heaven is an animal. Ghazali counters that this exhaustive division (الحصر) is false. He concludes that calling the heaven an animal is a baseless judgment.

Correction: Ghazali only denies that reason can prove it; he admits its possibility.

The Chain of Causes: Celestial Motions and Future Knowledge
The philosophers' proof using the chain of causes and effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that knowledge of celestial motions yields knowledge of all earthly events through the chain of causes.

Core Idea
All earthly events are chained to celestial motions; knowing those motions is knowing the future.

The philosophers claim that the souls of the heavens are informed of future events. Their first proof relies on causality: every event in the sublunary world is ultimately caused by periodic celestial motions. Therefore, whoever knows these motions knows all their consequences down the chain. In this lesson, we examine this causal argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The chain of causes and effects in the world of generation and corruption terminates at the particular periodic celestial motions.
This is the foundational claim: earthly events depend on celestial movements.
2
See the inference
Whoever conceives the motions conceives their necessary consequences, and the consequences of those consequences, to the end of the chain.
Knowledge of the motions implies knowledge of all their effects, since causation is deterministic.
3
Consider the human analogy
The philosophers note that humans do not know the future because they do not know all causes. If they knew all causes, they would have intuitive knowledge (ḥads) of effects.
This illustrates the principle: limited causes give limited knowledge; complete causes give complete knowledge.
4
Reflect on the scope
The argument implies that the souls of the heavens, which know the celestial motions perfectly, have comprehensive knowledge of all future earthly events.
This is the conclusion the philosophers draw for their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استدلال الفلاسفة على أن نفوس السموات مطلعة على ما سيحدث في العالم بأن قالوا : إن سلسلة الأسباب والمسببات في عالم الكون والفساد تنتهى إلى الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية ، والمتصور للحركات متصور للوازمها ، ولوازم لوازمها إلى آخر السلسلة. قالوا : نحن إنما لا نعلم المستقبل ، لأننا لا نعلم جميع أسبابه ، ولو جميع المسببات ؛ ولأننا نعلم علمنا جميع الأسباب ، لعلمنا بعض الأسباب ، فيقع لنا حدس بوقوع المسببات. قالوا : وإن النائم يرى فى نومه ما يكون فى المستقبل ، وذلك لاتصاله باللوح المحفوظ أثناء النوم ، وقد تظل صورة الشيء في نفسه كما رآها ، وربما سارعت المخيلة إلى محاكاته ، وهذه هي الرؤيا التي تحتاج إلى تعبير. قالوا : والاتصال بنفوس السموات مبذول ، ليس بيننا وبينها حجاب ، ولكننا في يقظتنا مشغولون بما تورده الحواس والشهوات علينا ، فإذا خف ضغط المحسات علينا أثناء النوم ، تهيأت لنا فرصة الاتصال بالسماويات ، فكانت الرؤى التي تحتاج إلى تعبير ، والتي لا تحتاج .
The philosophers' proof that the souls of the heavens are informed of what will occur in the world is that they said: The chain of causes and effects in the world of generation and corruption terminates at the particular periodic celestial motions, and the one who conceives the motions conceives their necessary consequences, and the consequences of their consequences to the end of the chain. They said: We do not know the future only because we do not know all its causes; if we knew all the causes, we would know some causes, and then an intuitive knowledge (ḥads) of the occurrence of the effects would occur to us. They said: The sleeper sees in his sleep what will be in the future, and that is because of his connection to the Preserved Tablet during sleep, and the image of the thing may remain in his soul as he saw it, and perhaps the imagination hastens to imitate it, and these are the dreams that need interpretation. They said: Connection to the souls of the heavens is available; there is no veil between us and them, but in our wakefulness we are occupied by what the senses and desires bring upon us. When the pressure of sensory things is lightened during sleep, we have an opportunity to connect with the celestial beings, and then come the dreams that need interpretation and those that do not.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' proof for their claim that the souls of the heavens are aware of future events. Their proof consists of two main arguments. First, they argue from the chain of causes and effects: all events in the sublunary world ultimately depend on the celestial motions. Therefore, whoever knows these motions knows all their consequences down the causal chain. They compare this to our own limited knowledge: if we knew all causes, we would have intuitive knowledge of effects. Second, they appeal to dreams: a sleeper sometimes sees future events because his soul connects to the Preserved Tablet (identified with the souls of the heavens) during sleep. The image may persist and be interpreted or even directly manifested. They explain that connection to the celestial souls is always possible, but during wakefulness we are distracted by senses and desires. During sleep, when these distractions lessen, we can connect and receive true dreams. This supports the idea that the heavens' souls have comprehensive knowledge of all particulars.

Key Terms
سلسلة الأسباب والمسببات
الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية
الحدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers claim that humans can know all future events through causal reasoning.
Correction: The philosophers state that humans do not know the future because we lack knowledge of all causes; only the heavens have complete causal knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Because the future is inherently unknowable
B) Because we do not know all the causes of future events
C) Because we are distracted by sensory desires
D) Because celestial souls hide it from us

Key Takeaway
The heavens' knowledge of the future is deduced from their knowledge of the celestial motions that cause all earthly events.

Dreams and Celestial Connection: Sleep Reveals the Future
The philosophers' argument from dream visions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers use dream visions to support the claim that the souls of the heavens are aware of future events.

Core Idea
Dreams are glimpses of the future because during sleep our souls connect to the heavenly Preserved Tablet.

The philosophers' second proof for the heavens' knowledge of future events comes from the phenomenon of true dreams. They argue that during sleep, when sensory distractions fade, the human soul can connect to the celestial souls or the Preserved Tablet, receiving images of future events. This lesson examines this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the observation
Sleepers sometimes see future events in their dreams. This is a common experience that needs explanation.
The philosophers take this as evidence that knowledge of the future is accessible.
2
Understand the explanation
During sleep, the soul connects to the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ), which contains all events.
The Preserved Tablet is identified with the souls of the heavens in their system.
3
Note the role of imagination
The image of the future event may remain in the soul, and the imaginative faculty may imitate or interpret it, resulting in dreams that need interpretation.
This distinguishes true direct visions from symbolic dreams.
4
Recognize the condition
Connection to celestial souls is always available, but wakefulness blocks it due to sensory and desire-based distractions. Sleep reduces these distractions, allowing connection.
This explains why true dreams occur during sleep, not wakefulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استدلال الفلاسفة على أن نفوس السموات مطلعة على ما سيحدث في العالم بأن قالوا : إن سلسلة الأسباب والمسببات في عالم الكون والفساد تنتهى إلى الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية ، والمتصور للحركات متصور للوازمها ، ولوازم لوازمها إلى آخر السلسلة. قالوا : نحن إنما لا نعلم المستقبل ، لأننا لا نعلم جميع أسبابه ، ولو جميع المسببات ؛ ولأننا نعلم علمنا جميع الأسباب ، لعلمنا بعض الأسباب ، فيقع لنا حدس بوقوع المسببات. قالوا : وإن النائم يرى فى نومه ما يكون فى المستقبل ، وذلك لاتصاله باللوح المحفوظ أثناء النوم ، وقد تظل صورة الشيء في نفسه كما رآها ، وربما سارعت المخيلة إلى محاكاته ، وهذه هي الرؤيا التي تحتاج إلى تعبير. قالوا : والاتصال بنفوس السموات مبذول ، ليس بيننا وبينها حجاب ، ولكننا في يقظتنا مشغولون بما تورده الحواس والشهوات علينا ، فإذا خف ضغط المحسات علينا أثناء النوم ، تهيأت لنا فرصة الاتصال بالسماويات ، فكانت الرؤى التي تحتاج إلى تعبير ، والتي لا تحتاج .
The philosophers' proof that the souls of the heavens are informed of what will occur in the world is that they said: The chain of causes and effects in the world of generation and corruption terminates at the particular periodic celestial motions, and the one who conceives the motions conceives their necessary consequences, and the consequences of their consequences to the end of the chain. They said: We do not know the future only because we do not know all its causes; if we knew all the causes, we would know some causes, and then an intuitive knowledge (ḥads) of the occurrence of the effects would occur to us. They said: The sleeper sees in his sleep what will be in the future, and that is because of his connection to the Preserved Tablet during sleep, and the image of the thing may remain in his soul as he saw it, and perhaps the imagination hastens to imitate it, and these are the dreams that need interpretation. They said: Connection to the souls of the heavens is available; there is no veil between us and them, but in our wakefulness we are occupied by what the senses and desires bring upon us. When the pressure of sensory things is lightened during sleep, we have an opportunity to connect with the celestial beings, and then come the dreams that need interpretation and those that do not.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' proof for their claim that the souls of the heavens are aware of future events. Their proof consists of two main arguments. First, they argue from the chain of causes and effects: all events in the sublunary world ultimately depend on the celestial motions. Therefore, whoever knows these motions knows all their consequences down the causal chain. They compare this to our own limited knowledge: if we knew all causes, we would have intuitive knowledge of effects. Second, they appeal to dreams: a sleeper sometimes sees future events because his soul connects to the Preserved Tablet (identified with the souls of the heavens) during sleep. The image may persist and be interpreted or even directly manifested. They explain that connection to the celestial souls is always possible, but during wakefulness we are distracted by senses and desires. During sleep, when these distractions lessen, we can connect and receive true dreams. This supports the idea that the heavens' souls have comprehensive knowledge of all particulars.

Key Terms
اللوح المحفوظ
الرؤيا
المخيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dreams are true and directly from the heavens without any imaginative processing.
Correction: The philosophers state that some dreams are symbolic due to imaginative imitation, requiring interpretation; not all are direct visions.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why do true dreams occur during sleep rather than wakefulness?
A) Because the Preserved Tablet is only active at night
B) Because sensory distractions are reduced during sleep, allowing connection to celestial souls
C) Because the soul leaves the body during sleep
D) Because dreams are random neural firings

Key Takeaway
True dreams are evidence that human souls can connect to the celestial source of knowledge when freed from sensory distractions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that your waking desires and sensory distractions block your soul's connection to higher knowledge. What specific desire or distraction in your life right now is most actively shutting out that connection—and are you willing to reduce it tonight?
?
If you have ever had a dream that came true, how has this passage challenged your explanation? Will you now acknowledge that it might be evidence of a real connection beyond your own mind, and if so, what responsibility does that place on you to seek truth beyond the senses?
?
The philosophers claim there is no veil between you and the celestial souls except your own preoccupations. Imagine you are sleeping tonight: what are you most afraid might be revealed about your life if that connection were fully open? And what are you most hopeful for?

The Causal Chain Argument for Heavenly Knowledge
Philosophers assert a causal chain from celestial motions to all events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that knowledge of celestial motions yields knowledge of all future events in the sublunary world.

Core Idea
All events trace back to celestial motions; knowing them reveals the future.

The philosophers propose a deterministic causal chain: everything in the world of generation and corruption depends on the periodic movements of the heavens. If one fully grasps those motions, one can know all their effects and thus the future.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal chain passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the philosophers' claim.
Notice that the passage opens with 'We demand of them the proof' as a challenge.
2
Identify the chain's endpoint
The chain of causes and effects terminates in 'particular periodic heavenly motions' (الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية). This is key.
3
Understand the implication
Whoever knows the motions knows their 'concomitants' (لوازم) and further concomitants. This implies complete knowledge of the future if the full chain is known.
The term 'لازم' is important – it means necessary concomitant.
4
Connect to our ignorance
The philosophers explain our ignorance as due to incomplete knowledge of causes. Partial causes yield only conjecture (حدس).
This sets up the later argument about dreams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنطالبهم بالدليل استدلال الفلاسفة على أن نفوس السموات مطلعة على ما سيحدث في العالم بأن قالوا : إن سلسلة الأسباب والمسببات في عالم الكون والفساد تنتهى إلى الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية ، والمتصور للحركات متصور للوازمها ، ولوازم لوازمها إلى آخر السلسلة. قالوا : نحن إنما لا نعلم المستقبل ، لأننا لا نعلم جميع أسبابه ، ولو جميع المسببات ؛ ولأننا نعلم بعض الأسباب ، فيقع لنا حدس بوقوع المسببات. قالوا : وإن النائم يرى فى نومه ما يكون فى المستقبل ، وذلك لاتصاله باللوح المحفوظ أثناء النوم ، وقد تظل صورة الشيء في نفسه كما رآها ، وربما سارعت المخيلة إلى محاكاته ، وهذه هي الرؤيا التي تحتاج إلى تعبير. قالوا : والاتصال بنفوس السموات مبذول ، ليس بيننا وبينها حجاب ، ولكننا في يقظتنا مشغولون بما تورده الحواس والشهوات علينا ، فإذا خف ضغط المحسات علينا أثناء النوم ، تهيأت لنا فرصة الاتصال بالسماويات ، فكانت الرؤى التي تحتاج إلى تعبير ، والتي لا تحتاج . قالوا : والأنبياء لقوة نفوسها ، تستطيع التخلص من ضغط الحواس في اليقظة ، لهذا يتيسر لها أن ترى في اليقظة ما لا يراه غيرهم إلا في النوم
“We demand of them the proof” — the proof of the philosophers that the souls of the heavens are informed of what will occur in the world, by their saying: “The chain of causes and effects in the world of generation and corruption terminates with the particular periodic heavenly motions, and one who conceives the motions conceives their concomitants, and the concomitants of their concomitants, to the end of the chain.” They said: “We only do not know the future because we do not know all its causes, nor all the effects; and because we know some causes, a conjecture occurs to us of the occurrence of the effects.” They said: “And the sleeper sees in his sleep what will be in the future, and that is due to his connection to the Preserved Tablet during sleep, and the image of the thing may remain in his soul as he saw it, and perhaps the imagination hastens to imitate it, and this is the dream that needs interpretation.” They said: “And connection to the souls of the heavens is available; there is no veil between us and them, but in our wakefulness we are occupied with what the senses and desires bring upon us. So when the pressure of sensibles lightens upon us during sleep, an opportunity is prepared for us to connect to the heavenly objects, and so there are dreams that need interpretation and those that do not.” They said: “And the prophets, by the strength of their souls, can free themselves from the pressure of the senses in wakefulness; therefore it is made easy for them to see in wakefulness what others do not see except in sleep.”
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' detailed proof and reasoning for their claim that the heavenly souls know future events. First, they assert a causal chain: all occurrences in the sublunary world ultimately depend on periodic celestial motions. Whoever knows those motions knows all their effects and the effects of those effects. Second, they explain our ignorance of the future as due to ignorance of the full causal chain; partial knowledge yields only conjecture. Third, they appeal to dreams: the sleeper connects to the Preserved Tablet and retains images, which the imagination may transform into symbolic dreams. Fourth, they argue that connection to the heavenly souls is always possible but blocked in wakefulness by sensory distractions; sleep removes this block. Finally, they claim that prophets, due to the strength of their souls, can overcome sensory distraction even while awake, thus seeing in wakefulness what others only see in sleep. This entire argument is presented as the philosophers' proof, which Ghazali will critique in subsequent lessons.

Key Terms
سلسلة الأسباب والمسببات
الحركات الجزئية الدورية السماوية
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why don't we normally know the future?
A) Because we do not know all the causes of future events
B) Because the future is inherently uncertain
C) Because God has hidden it from us
D) Because we are not strong enough spiritually

Key Takeaway
According to the philosophers, total knowledge of the causal chain starting from celestial motions would yield complete knowledge of the future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers say that total causal knowledge yields certainty, while we only have conjecture. In what area of your life are you most guilty of mistaking your guesswork for certainty? Will you confess to Allah your reliance on conjecture instead of His all-encompassing knowledge?
?
You make plans for tomorrow as if you know the future. Have you ever truly felt the weight of your ignorance—that you do not know a single cause, let alone the whole chain? How would your attitude change if you lived each moment acknowledging that only Allah knows what will happen?
?
The philosophers attribute complete knowledge of future events to the souls of the heavens. But who alone deserves that attribute? When you read this lesson, does your heart tremble at the thought that you seek knowledge that belongs only to Allah? What step will you take today to surrender your plans to the All-Knowing?

Dreams as Evidence for Connection to Heavenly Souls
Philosophers argue that dreams involve connection to the Preserved Tablet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that dreams provide evidence of a connection to the Preserved Tablet and the souls of the heavens.

Core Idea
Dreams show our souls can tap into heavenly knowledge when senses are quiet.

The philosophers use the phenomenon of veridical dreams to support their claim that human souls can access the knowledge of the heavenly souls. They explain that during sleep, the soul is less preoccupied with sensory input and can connect to the Preserved Tablet.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the explanation of how dreams of the future occur.
2
Identify the mechanism
The sleeper's connection to the Preserved Tablet (اللوح المحفوظ) during sleep is the cause. The image of the thing seen may remain or be transformed by imagination.
Dreams needing interpretation are those where imagination has altered the image.
3
Understand the general claim
Connection to heavenly souls is available to everyone, but wakefulness blocks it due to sensory distractions. Sleep reduces that block.
This explains why dreams can be true.
4
Relate to earlier argument
This empirical evidence supports the idea that humans can access the knowledge of the celestial souls, which ties back to the causal chain.

Prophets' Wakeful Knowledge of the Heavens
Philosophers explain prophets' wakeful insight by soul strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that prophets, due to the strength of their souls, can access heavenly knowledge even while awake.

Core Idea
Prophets achieve in wakefulness what others only achieve in sleep.

The final part of the philosophers' proof addresses the special case of prophets. They argue that the same connection available to ordinary people during sleep is available to prophets all the time because their souls are strong enough to overcome sensory distractions even while awake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about prophets
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the phrase 'قوة نفوسها' (strength of their souls).
2
Understand the mechanism
Prophets can free themselves from the pressure of the senses (ضغط الحواس) during wakefulness, which is normally only possible during sleep.
This explains why they receive revelation while awake.
3
Relate to the previous arguments
This completes the philosophers' naturalistic explanation of knowledge of the future, from causal chain to dreams to prophecy.
Prophecy is thus not supernatural but a result of a perfected soul.

Refutation of Necessary Causality: Fire and Cotton
Ghazali refutes necessary causation, attributing conjunction to God's decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of necessary causality and his alternative explanation that the conjunction of cause and effect is due to God's decree.

Core Idea
Causal necessity is an illusion; only God's will creates conjunction.

Ghazali begins his famous critique of causality by challenging the common belief that cause and effect are necessarily linked. He asserts that what we observe is only habitual conjunction, not logical necessity. This passage sets the stage for his argument using the example of fire and cotton.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice the heading 'Natural Sciences. 25th Question: Causes and Effects.'
2
Identify the central claim
Find where Ghazali states that the conjunction is not necessary. Underline the phrase 'ليس ضرورياً عندنا'.
3
Understand the alternative explanation
Ghazali says the conjunction is due to God's prior decree creating them together. Note the phrase 'تقدير الله سبحانه يخلقها معا'.
4
Note the philosophers' position
Ghazali mentions that the philosophers deny this possibility and claim impossibility. This sets up the debate.
5
Consider the chosen example
Ghazali selects the burning of cotton as a specific example. Think about why this example is useful for the argument.

الطبيعيات
٢٥ - مسألة الأسباب والمسببات
قال الغزالي : الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً ، وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا
فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه يخلقها معا ، لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه
قال الغزالى : وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه ، وادعو استحالته
قال الغزالي : والنظر فى هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول فلنعين مثالا واحدا . هو الاحتراق في القطن مثلا
Natural Sciences. 25th Question: Causes and Effects. Al-Ghazali said: The conjunction between what is habitually believed to be a cause and what is believed to be an effect is not necessary according to us. For their conjunction is due to the prior decree of God, may He be exalted, creating them together, not because it is necessary in itself. Al-Ghazali said: And the philosophers denied its possibility and claimed its impossibility. Al-Ghazali said: And the investigation into these things beyond enumeration is lengthy, so let us specify a single example: it is the burning of cotton, for example.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage opens Ghazali's famous discussion on causality. It begins with the section heading: 'Natural Sciences. 25th Question: Causes and Effects.' Ghazali states that the conjunction between what is habitually taken as a cause and what is taken as an effect is not necessary (ليس ضروريا) in itself. Rather, the conjunction occurs because of God's prior decree (تقدير الله) that He creates them together (يخلقها معا). The philosophers, however, deny this possibility and claim that the causal connection is necessary and impossible otherwise. Ghazali then notes that the discussion of all such cases is lengthy, so he chooses a single example: the burning of cotton when it comes into contact with fire. This example will be used to argue that the burning is not a necessary effect of fire but is created directly by God.

Key Terms
الاقتران
سبب
مسبب
ضرورياً
تقدير الله
يخلقها معا
استحالته
الاحتراق
القطن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali denies causality entirely, but he only denies its necessity, not the observed regularity.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that conjunction is real but denies it is necessary; it is due to God's will.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does fire burn cotton?
A) Because fire necessarily causes burning by its nature
B) Because of an inherent necessary relation
C) Because God creates the burning at the time of contact
D) Because the philosophers proved it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the conjunction between cause and effect is not necessary but is created directly by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every effect—from the warmth of the sun to the beat of your heart—is directly from God's will, or do you sometimes forget His role and rely on 'natural laws' as if they were independent? What one habit can you change today to remember His constant decree?
?
If you genuinely believed that the fire's burning of cotton is not necessary but is God's direct creation each time, would you fear or trust Him differently? How would your heart change if you saw every 'cause' as a veil for His action?
?
This passage challenges you to see God's hand in every detail. Is there an area of your life where you rely on 'cause and effect' without acknowledging His immediate power? What step can you take this week to consciously attribute your daily outcomes to Him?

Ghazali's Denial of Necessary Causality
Ghazali argues that causality is not necessary but by divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the observed connection between cause and effect is not necessary but is by divine decree, and how he illustrates this with the example of cotton burning.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are linked by divine decree, not by necessity.

In this passage, Ghazali presents his foundational critique of causality as understood by the philosophers. He argues that the habitual conjunction we observe is not logically necessary but is a result of Allah's prior decree. This sets the stage for his occasionalist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice Ghazali's concise style.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Find where Ghazali states that the connection is not necessary (ḍarūrī) and what he says instead.
The key term is 'al-iqtirān' (concomitance).
3
Understand the objection and example
Read the philosophers' denial and the specific example of cotton burning. Consider why this example is chosen.
The example illustrates the habitual sequence of fire and burning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي : الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً ، وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضرورياً عندنا · · فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه يخلقها معا ، لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه · قال الغزالى : وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه ، وادعو استحالته · قال الغزالي : والنظر فى هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول فلنعين مثالا واحدا . هو الاحتراق في القطن مثلا
Al-Ghazali said: The concomitance between what is habitually believed to be a cause and what is believed to be an effect is not necessary according to us. Indeed, their concomitance is due to a prior decree of God the Exalted creating them together, not because it is necessary in itself. Al-Ghazali said: The philosophers denied its possibility and claimed its impossibility. Al-Ghazali said: And discussing these matters which are beyond enumeration would be lengthy, so let us specify a single example: it is the burning of cotton, for instance.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's foundational thesis on causality. He states that the observed conjunction (al-iqtirān) between what people customarily consider a cause and what they consider an effect is not necessary (ḍarūrī) in itself. Rather, it occurs by Allah's prior decree (taqdīr) that He creates both together. The philosophers object, claiming it is impossible (istiḥāla) for the effect to occur without the cause. Ghazali then narrows the discussion to a concrete example: the burning of cotton when fire contacts it, which he will analyze in detail. This sets up his occasionalist position: God is the true agent, and the habitual sequence is not intrinsic.

Key Terms
الاقتران
تقدير الله
السبب
المسبب
الاحتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies any correlation between cause and effect altogether.
Correction: Ghazali only denies metaphysical necessity; he affirms empirical regularity as a habit created by Allah.

Checkpoint
What reason does Ghazali give for the observed connection between cause and effect?
A) It is logically necessary.
B) It is due to a prior decree of God.
C) It is an illusion.
D) It is from the nature of things.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali holds that what we call 'cause' and 'effect' are only habitually conjoined by Allah's decree, not intrinsically necessary.

Ghazali's First Station: Fire Is Not the Sole Agent of Burning
Ghazali introduces the first station and critiques the philosophers' evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's core argument against the philosophers' claim that fire alone causes burning, and the distinction between 'occurrence at the time of' and 'occurrence by'.

Core Idea
Observation shows burning with fire, not by fire.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Imam al-Ghazali begins his critique of causal necessity by addressing the claim that fire is the sole agent of burning. He distinguishes between what observation actually proves—temporal concomitance—and what it does not prove—causal agency. This sets the stage for his famous assertion that God is the true agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the ellipsis and dashes in the Arabic; they reflect the original manuscript.
2
Identify the three stations
The passage opens by stating there are three stations. This is the first. Recognize the overarching structure of the discussion.
3
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent (philosophers) claim that fire is the sole agent (fā'il) of burning. This is the claim Ghazali will refute.
4
Analyze Ghazali's denial and counterclaim
Ghazali denies that fire is the agent and asserts that God is the agent. He then examines the only evidence offered: observation.
His denial is categorical: 'This is what we deny.'
5
Master the epistemological distinction
The key move: observation shows burning occurs upon contact with fire (al-ḥuṣūl 'indahā), not that it occurs by fire (al-ḥuṣūl bihā). Understand why this distinction is critical.
This distinction invalidates the philosophers' proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالى : والكلام فى هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات : المقام الأول أن يدعى الخصم أن فاعل الاحتراق هو النار فقط · قال الغزالي : وهذا مما ننكره ، بل نقول : فاعل الاحتراق هو الله تعالى ... وليس لهم دليل إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار . . . والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها، ولا تدل على الحصول بها
Al-Ghazali said: The discussion on this issue has three stations. The first station: that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire. Al-Ghazali said: This is what we deny; rather we say: the agent of burning is God the Exalted. They have no evidence except observing that burning occurs upon contact with fire. Observation indicates occurrence at the time of it, not occurrence by it.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage introduces the three stations (maqāmāt) of the discussion on causation via the burning example. The first station: the opponent (the philosophers) claims that fire is the sole agent (fā'il) of burning. Ghazali categorically denies this and asserts that God is the true agent. He points out that their only evidence is observation of burning upon contact with fire. However, observation only proves temporal concomitance (al-ḥuṣūl 'indahā), not that fire is the cause (al-ḥuṣūl bihā). This is a crucial epistemological distinction: concomitance does not prove agency.

Key Terms
المقام الأول
فاعل الاحتراق
المشاهدة
الحصول عندها
الحصول بها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is denying fire has any role in burning; he only denies it being the sole agent.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny that fire is involved; he denies that fire is the sole agent and that observation proves causation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does observation of burning upon contact with fire prove?
A) That fire is the cause of burning.
B) That burning occurs at the time of contact.
C) That fire creates heat.
D) That the agent is the fire.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that observation proves temporal association, not causal agency; only God is the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you attribute the outcomes of your actions to the apparent causes (like your efforts) or do you truly recognize that only God is the agent? How would your trust in God change if you genuinely believed that no cause acts except by His will? And how can you train yourself to constantly remember that God is the true agent behind every result?
?
Think of a time when you felt that something was 'bound to happen' due to natural causes (e.g., a medical diagnosis, a financial failure). Did you consider that the actual Agent is God, and that He could have prevented it? This lesson challenges you to not take the 'laws of nature' for granted. What steps can you take to shift your perspective from seeing causes as independent to seeing them as instruments of God's will?
?
If you truly internalized that observation only shows concomitance, not causation, how would it affect your fear of people, events, or circumstances? Write down one specific fear you have about a future situation and consciously remind yourself that the true agent is God alone. Are you willing to live with that conviction today?

The Second Station: Divine Will and Causal Necessity
Ghazali addresses the second station and responds to the philosophers' objection about causal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection to denying causal necessity and Ghazali's initial response that God acts by will, not necessity.

Core Idea
Denying causal necessity does not lead to chaos; God's will is deliberate and orderly.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' view on causality. They accept that events have principles but insist that those principles act by necessity, not divine will. This leads them to reject miracles like Prophet Ibrahim's survival in fire. Ghazali counters by affirming that God acts by choice, and he also presents the philosophers' worry that without causal necessity, anything could happen—a charge he will address later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two sides
Notice the philosophers' claim: principles emit events by necessity, not choice. Then find Ghazali's first response: God acts by will, not necessity.
Ghazali references his earlier refutation on the world's origination.
3
Understand the objection about absurdities
Read the philosophers' objection: if effects are not necessary, then anything could happen (e.g., books turning into beasts). Ask yourself how this challenges Ghazali's position.
This objection sets up Ghazali's later response about God's custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثانى مع من يسلم أن هذه الحوادث تفيض من مبادئ الحوادث ولكن الاستعداد لقبول الصور يحصل بهذه الأسباب المشاهدة ... إلا أن تلك المبادئ أيضاً تصدر عنها الأشياء باللزوم لا على سبيل التروى والاختيار ... ولهذا أنكر وا وقوع إبراهيم - صلوات الله عليه وسلامه - فى النار مع عدم الاحتراق ، ومع بقاء النار نارا · قال الغزالي : والجواب الثانى له مسلكان : المسلك الأول : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن المبادئ ليست تفعل بالاختيار ، وأن الله تعالى لا يفعل بالإرادة . . . وقد فرغنا من إبطال دعواهم في ذلك في مسألة حدوث العالم · قال الفلاسفة : إن إنكار لزوم المسببات عن أسبابها يؤدى إلى ارتكاب محالات شنيعة ، فإذا أسندت الأفعال إلى إرادة ليس لها منهج مخصوص ، فليجوز كل واحد منا أن ينقلب ما بين يديه من كتب إلى سباع ضارية ، وجبال راسية ، وأعداء مسلحة
The second station: with one who concedes that these events flow from the principles of events, but that the preparedness to receive forms is obtained through these observed causes... except that those principles also emit things by necessity, not by deliberation and choice... Therefore they denied the occurrence of Ibrahim (peace be upon him) in the fire without burning, while the fire remained fire. Al-Ghazali said: The second answer has two approaches. The first approach: that we say: we do not concede that the principles do not act by choice, and that God the Exalted does not act by will... We have already finished refuting their claim about that in the issue of the temporal origination of the world. The philosophers said: Denying the necessity of effects from their causes leads to committing horrible absurdities. For if actions are attributed to a will that has no specific method, then everyone of us could believe that what is in his hands could turn into predatory beasts, firm mountains, and armed enemies.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage covers the second station of the discussion. Here the opponent grants that events flow from higher principles (mabādi') but argues that preparedness comes from observable causes, and that the principles act by necessity, not choice. Hence they deny the possibility of miracles like Prophet Ibrahim (AS) not burning in fire. Ghazali responds with a second answer (al-jawāb al-thānī) having two approaches. The first approach: he rejects the premise that these principles do not act by choice, asserting that God acts by will (irāda); he refers back to his previous refutation of their position in the discussion on the temporal origination of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). Then the philosophers raise an objection: denying necessary causal connection leads to absurdities—if God's will has no fixed pattern, then anything could happen without order, like books turning into beasts. Ghazali seems to be setting up a fuller response, but the text cuts off here. This objection is crucial because it forces Ghazali to clarify how occasionalism does not lead to chaos, but rather God's custom (ʿāda) provides stability.

مبادئ الحوادث
الاستعداد
باللزوم
التروى والاختيار
المسلك الأول

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' objection, what would happen if effects are not necessarily connected to causes?
A) Everything would remain stable.
B) Absurdities like books turning into beasts would be possible.
C) Prophets would not exist.
D) Fire would lose its heat.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali counters the philosophers' claim of necessary causation by affirming God's will and choice, and he records their objection that this leads to absurdities—a challenge he will answer with the concept of God's custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fallacy of Necessary Causation
Ghazali refutes the claim that fire is the sole agent of burning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that empirical observation only shows conjunction, not necessary causation.

Core Idea
Observation shows occurrence with fire, not by fire.

In this passage, Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers' claim that fire is the sole agent of burning. He draws a crucial distinction between 'with' and 'by' to show that causality is not empirically proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Ghazali claims that the true agent of burning is Allah, not fire. Find the sentence where he states this.
3
Understand the 'with' vs 'by' distinction
Ghazali argues that observation indicates burning at the time of fire's presence (عندها), not by fire (بها). This distinction is key to his refutation.
This distinction allows for divine intervention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالى : والكلام فى هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات : المقام الأول أن يدعى الخصم أن فاعل الاحتراق هو النار فقط. قال الغزالي : وهذا مما ننكره ، بل نقول : فاعل الاحتراق هو الله تعالى ... وليس لهم دليل إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار . . . والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها، ولا تدل على الحصول بها. المقام الثانى مع من يسلم أن هذه الحوادث تفيض من مبادئ الحوادث ولكن الاستعداد لقبول الصور يحصل بهذه الأسباب المشاهدة ... إلا أن تلك المبادئ أيضاً تصدر عنها الأشياء باللزوم لا على سبيل التروى والاختيار ... ولهذا أنكروا وقوع إبراهيم - صلوات الله عليه وسلامه - فى النار مع عدم الاحتراق ، ومع بقاء النار نارا. قال الغزالي : والجواب الثانى له مسلكان : المسلك الأول : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن المبادئ ليست تفعل بالاختيار ، وأن الله تعالى لا يفعل بالإرادة . . . وقد فرغنا من إبطال دعواهم في ذلك في مسألة حدوث العالم. قال الفلاسفة : إن إنكار لزوم المسببات عن أسبابها يؤدى إلى ارتكاب محالات شنيعة ، فإذا أسندت الأفعال إلى إرادة ليس لها منهج مخصوص ، فليجوز كل واحد منا أن ينقلب ما بين يديه من كتب إلى سباع ضارية ، وجبال راسية ، وأعداء مسلحة. قال الغزالي : والجواب أن الله قد خلق لنا علماً بأن هذه الممكنات لم تقع ، واستمرار العادة على نمط واحد يرسخ في أذهاننا جريانها على نهج ثابت ، برغم إمكان تخلفه. المسلك الثانى : قال الغزالى : نسلم أن النار خلقت خلقة تجعلها تحرق ما تلاقيه ، ولكن مع هذا يجوز أن يلقي نبي في النار فلا يحترق : إما بتغيير صفة النار . أو بتغيير صفة النبي عليه السلام. والمحال : هو ما يرجع إلى الجمع بين النفى والإثبات
Al-Ghazali said: The discussion on this issue has three stations: The first station is that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire. Al-Ghazali said: This we deny, rather we say: The agent of burning is Allah Almighty ... And they have no proof except observation of the occurrence of burning upon contact with fire. ... And observation indicates occurrence with it, and does not indicate occurrence by it. The second station is with one who concedes that these events flow from the principles of events, but the preparedness to receive forms occurs through these observed causes ... except that those principles also bring forth things by necessity, not by deliberation and choice ... And for this reason they denied the occurrence of Ibrahim – peace be upon him – in the fire without burning, while the fire remained fire. Al-Ghazali said: The second answer has two approaches: The first approach: We say: We do not concede that the principles do not act by choice, and that Allah Almighty does not act by will ... And we have already finished refuting their claim in that in the issue of the creation of the world. The philosophers said: Denying the necessary connection of effects from their causes leads to committing hideous impossibilities. For if actions are attributed to a will that has no specific method, then let any one of us be allowed that what is before him of books turns into predatory beasts, solid mountains, and armed enemies. Al-Ghazali said: The answer is that Allah has created for us knowledge that these possibilities did not occur, and the continuation of habit in one pattern establishes in our minds their running on a fixed course, despite the possibility of its being otherwise. The second approach: Al-Ghazali said: We concede that fire is created with a constitution that makes it burn what it contacts, but along with this it is possible that a prophet be thrown into fire and not burn: either by changing the attribute of fire, or by changing the attribute of the prophet, peace be upon him. And the impossible is that which goes back to combining negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim of necessary causation. Ghazali lays out three stations (maqamat) of the discussion, though only the first two are detailed here. In the first station, the opponent asserts that fire alone is the agent of burning. Ghazali replies that observation only shows that burning occurs upon contact with fire, not that it occurs by fire. This distinction between 'with' (عند) and 'by' (ب) is crucial: empirical evidence proves conjunction, not causation. In the second station, the opponent concedes that events flow from principles but claims these principles act by necessity, not choice, and therefore deny the miracle of Ibrahim being protected in the fire. Ghazali responds with two approaches. The first approach denies that the principles act without choice, referring to his earlier proof in the discussion of the world's temporal origination. He then addresses the philosophers' objection that denying necessary connection leads to absurdities by stating that Allah has instilled in us knowledge that certain habitual patterns will continue, but this does not preclude occasional exceptions through divine power. The second approach concedes that fire has a nature to burn but asserts that a prophet can be preserved by altering either the fire's attribute or the prophet's attribute. Finally, Ghazali defines the impossible (al-muhal) as the combination of affirmation and negation, indicating that the miraculous preservation does not entail a logical contradiction.

Key Terms
فاعل الاحتراق
مشاهدة
عند
ب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observation proves that fire causes burning.
Correction: Observation only shows that burning occurs when fire is present, not that fire is the cause.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say observation indicates?
A) That fire is the agent
B) That burning occurs with fire, not by fire
C) That fire is the cause
D) That miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
Observation of burning upon contact with fire does not prove that fire is the agent; it only shows conjunction, not causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a success in your life to a worldly cause (your intelligence, effort, luck) without recognizing Allah as the sole agent? How would acknowledging that the true agent is Allah change your gratitude and humility?
?
If you truly believed that fire does not burn by itself but only by Allah's will, would your fear of it be different? What does your actual reaction reveal about your level of tawheed?
?
Think of one 'cause' you rely on daily—your job, your health, your family. Are you trusting in that cause or in the One who creates the effect? What concrete action can you take today to shift your trust to Allah alone?

Divine Will vs. Necessary Causation
Ghazali responds to those who accept causality but deny miracles due to necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that principles act by choice, not necessity, thus allowing miracles.

Core Idea
Principles act by choice, not necessity, so miracles are possible.

In the second station, Ghazali addresses opponents who accept causality but deny miracles because they think principles act by necessity. He presents his first approach: denying that principles lack choice and reaffirming divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent accepts that events flow from principles but claims these principles act by necessity, not choice. Find this in the text.
3
Understand Ghazali's first approach
Ghazali denies that the principles act without choice. He asserts that Allah acts by will and references his earlier proof about the world's creation.
This connects to Ghazali's earlier arguments in the issue of the world's temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالى : والكلام فى هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات : المقام الأول أن يدعى الخصم أن فاعل الاحتراق هو النار فقط. قال الغزالي : وهذا مما ننكره ، بل نقول : فاعل الاحتراق هو الله تعالى ... وليس لهم دليل إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار . . . والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها، ولا تدل على الحصول بها. المقام الثانى مع من يسلم أن هذه الحوادث تفيض من مبادئ الحوادث ولكن الاستعداد لقبول الصور يحصل بهذه الأسباب المشاهدة ... إلا أن تلك المبادئ أيضاً تصدر عنها الأشياء باللزوم لا على سبيل التروى والاختيار ... ولهذا أنكروا وقوع إبراهيم - صلوات الله عليه وسلامه - فى النار مع عدم الاحتراق ، ومع بقاء النار نارا. قال الغزالي : والجواب الثانى له مسلكان : المسلك الأول : أن نقول : لا نسلم أن المبادئ ليست تفعل بالاختيار ، وأن الله تعالى لا يفعل بالإرادة . . . وقد فرغنا من إبطال دعواهم في ذلك في مسألة حدوث العالم. قال الفلاسفة : إن إنكار لزوم المسببات عن أسبابها يؤدى إلى ارتكاب محالات شنيعة ، فإذا أسندت الأفعال إلى إرادة ليس لها منهج مخصوص ، فليجوز كل واحد منا أن ينقلب ما بين يديه من كتب إلى سباع ضارية ، وجبال راسية ، وأعداء مسلحة. قال الغزالي : والجواب أن الله قد خلق لنا علماً بأن هذه الممكنات لم تقع ، واستمرار العادة على نمط واحد يرسخ في أذهاننا جريانها على نهج ثابت ، برغم إمكان تخلفه. المسلك الثانى : قال الغزالى : نسلم أن النار خلقت خلقة تجعلها تحرق ما تلاقيه ، ولكن مع هذا يجوز أن يلقي نبي في النار فلا يحترق : إما بتغيير صفة النار . أو بتغيير صفة النبي عليه السلام. والمحال : هو ما يرجع إلى الجمع بين النفى والإثبات
Al-Ghazali said: The discussion on this issue has three stations: The first station is that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire. Al-Ghazali said: This we deny, rather we say: The agent of burning is Allah Almighty ... And they have no proof except observation of the occurrence of burning upon contact with fire. ... And observation indicates occurrence with it, and does not indicate occurrence by it. The second station is with one who concedes that these events flow from the principles of events, but the preparedness to receive forms occurs through these observed causes ... except that those principles also bring forth things by necessity, not by deliberation and choice ... And for this reason they denied the occurrence of Ibrahim – peace be upon him – in the fire without burning, while the fire remained fire. Al-Ghazali said: The second answer has two approaches: The first approach: We say: We do not concede that the principles do not act by choice, and that Allah Almighty does not act by will ... And we have already finished refuting their claim in that in the issue of the creation of the world. The philosophers said: Denying the necessary connection of effects from their causes leads to committing hideous impossibilities. For if actions are attributed to a will that has no specific method, then let any one of us be allowed that what is before him of books turns into predatory beasts, solid mountains, and armed enemies. Al-Ghazali said: The answer is that Allah has created for us knowledge that these possibilities did not occur, and the continuation of habit in one pattern establishes in our minds their running on a fixed course, despite the possibility of its being otherwise. The second approach: Al-Ghazali said: We concede that fire is created with a constitution that makes it burn what it contacts, but along with this it is possible that a prophet be thrown into fire and not burn: either by changing the attribute of fire, or by changing the attribute of the prophet, peace be upon him. And the impossible is that which goes back to combining negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim of necessary causation. Ghazali lays out three stations (maqamat) of the discussion, though only the first two are detailed here. In the first station, the opponent asserts that fire alone is the agent of burning. Ghazali replies that observation only shows that burning occurs upon contact with fire, not that it occurs by fire. This distinction between 'with' (عند) and 'by' (ب) is crucial: empirical evidence proves conjunction, not causation. In the second station, the opponent concedes that events flow from principles but claims these principles act by necessity, not choice, and therefore deny the miracle of Ibrahim being protected in the fire. Ghazali responds with two approaches. The first approach denies that the principles act without choice, referring to his earlier proof in the discussion of the world's temporal origination. He then addresses the philosophers' objection that denying necessary connection leads to absurdities by stating that Allah has instilled in us knowledge that certain habitual patterns will continue, but this does not preclude occasional exceptions through divine power. The second approach concedes that fire has a nature to burn but asserts that a prophet can be preserved by altering either the fire's attribute or the prophet's attribute. Finally, Ghazali defines the impossible (al-muhal) as the combination of affirmation and negation, indicating that the miraculous preservation does not entail a logical contradiction.

Key Terms
مبادئ
اللزوم
الاختيار
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why do philosophers deny the miracle of Ibrahim?
A) Because they believe fire cannot burn
B) Because they think principles act by necessity not choice
C) Because they deny Allah's existence
D) Because they think Ibrahim was not a prophet

Key Takeaway
Philosophers deny miracles because they believe principles act by necessity, but Ghazali affirms that Allah acts by will, making miracles possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can intervene in your life at any moment, or do you subconsciously think that He is bound by the 'laws' of nature? When was the last time you prayed for something that seemed impossible, and did you expect it to happen?
?
What area of your life have you convinced yourself is 'fixed' by circumstances, forgetting that Allah acts by choice and can change it? How does this lesson challenge your fatalism?
?
If you believe that Allah's will is not constrained by necessity, why do you still worry about outcomes as if they are inevitable? What would change in your daily life if you fully trusted His will over your plans?

The Philosophers' Objection and the Defense of Miracles
Ghazali addresses the objection that denying necessary causation leads to absurdities, and presents his second approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's responses to the objection that denying necessary causation leads to absurdity, and his second approach concerning miracles.

Core Idea
Allah's custom is not absolute; only logical contradiction is impossible.

The philosophers raise a strong objection: if necessary causation is denied, then anything could happen arbitrarily, leading to absurdity. Ghazali responds by explaining the role of habit (adah) in our knowledge and then presents his second approach, conceding fire's nature but allowing miracles through divine intervention.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the philosophers' objection
They argue that denying necessary causation leads to absurd possibilities like books turning into beasts. Find this argument in the text.
3
Examine Ghazali's two answers
First, Ghazali explains that Allah has created knowledge of habitual patterns, so absurdities don't happen. Second, he concedes fire's nature but allows miracles by changing attributes. Finally, he defines the impossible as logical contradiction.
The definition of impossible is key: only combining affirmation and negation is impossible.

The Answer: God Created Knowledge that the Possible Did Not Occur
Ghazali gives the answer regarding God's habit and created knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali reconciles God's habitual pattern of creation with the possibility of miracles, and why habit does not imply necessity.

Core Idea
Habit ingrains expectation, but God's power keeps all possibilities open.

In this passage, Ghazali directly addresses the tension between observed regularity and divine omnipotence. He argues that God has created in us a knowledge that certain possible events—like a prophet not being burned by fire—did not actually occur. Yet, the constant repetition of the same pattern (habit) becomes deeply rooted in our minds, making us assume that events must follow a fixed course. But Ghazali reminds us that this fixed course is not necessary; it remains open to interruption by God's will, which is the basis of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the author's own words set the context before you analyze.
2
Identify the two key claims
Ghazali makes two claims: (1) God created knowledge that the possible events did not occur; (2) habit firmly establishes a fixed course in our minds despite the possibility of deviation. Notice how he distinguishes between divine creation and human perception.
3
Understand 'habit' in this context
Ask yourself: What does Ghazali mean by 'habit'? It is not a law of nature but God's typical way of acting. Recognize that 'habit' here is descriptive, not prescriptive.
The term 'al-ʿādah' (habit) is central to Ghazali's occasionalist framework.
4
Reflect on the possibility of deviation
Ghazali emphasizes 'despite the possibility of its deviation'. This is the key point: the habitual pattern can be broken by God's direct intervention. Consider how this allows for miracles without denying normal experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الغزالي : والجواب أن الله قد خلق لنا علماً بأن هذه الممكنات لم تقع ، واستمرار العادة على نمط واحد يرسخ في أذهاننا جريانها على نهج ثابت ، برغم إمكان تخلفه
Al-Ghazali said: The answer is that God has created for us knowledge that these possibilities did not occur, and the continuation of habit upon a single pattern firmly establishes in our minds their running upon a fixed course, despite the possibility of its deviation.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Ghazali's answer to the question of miracles and natural law. Ghazali states that God created in humans a knowledge that certain possibilities (e.g., the fire not burning a prophet) did not actually take place. However, the habitual continuation of events in a consistent pattern becomes deeply ingrained in our minds, making us perceive events as running on a fixed course. Yet Ghazali emphasizes that despite this ingrained habit, the possibility of deviation (i.e., a miracle) still exists. Thus, the habitual order is not necessary; it is subject to God's will and can be interrupted.

Key Terms
العادة (habit)
الممكنات (possibilities)
الخلق (created knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Habit implies a necessary connection between cause and effect, making miracles impossible.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that habit is only a firm belief instilled by God, not a logical necessity; the possibility of deviation (i.e., miracles) always remains.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge that they occur
B) Knowledge that they did not occur
C) That they are impossible
D) That they are necessary

Key Takeaway
God's habitual pattern in nature is not a necessary law; it is a consistent but breakable custom, allowing for miracles while explaining our expectation of regularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you pray for a miracle, do you expect God to break His habit, or do you feel it is impossible? What does this reveal about your understanding of God's power and His relationship to creation?
?
Consider a routine you rely on—your health, provision, or relationships. How does viewing it as God's habitual gift rather than a natural necessity affect your gratitude and your fear of losing it?

The Miracle of Fire: Ghazali's Second Approach
Ghazali presents his second approach allowing miracles by property change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali reconciles natural causality with miracles by allowing divine alteration of properties.

Core Idea
Miracles do not deny nature; they change properties.

In this short but pivotal passage, Ghazali presents his second approach to the problem of miracles. He fully concedes that fire naturally burns, but he opens the door for divine intervention without breaking natural law. The key is that God can change the property of either the fire or the prophet, preserving both causality and miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice how Ghazali first concedes ('nusallimu') then introduces the possibility ('yajuzu').
The structure is concession then exception.
2
Understand the concession
Ghazali explicitly admits that fire has a nature (khilqa) that causes it to burn. This is important because he is not denying causality.
3
Identify the two possibilities
The miracle occurs either by changing the property (sifa) of the fire or the prophet. List both and compare them.
Changing the fire means it no longer burns; changing the prophet makes him immune.
4
Connect to the bigger argument
Ask: How does this defend the possibility of miracles against the philosophers who deny them? Ghazali shows that miracles are not violations of logic but divine adjustments.
This refutes the view that miracles are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثانى : قال الغزالى : نسلم أن النار خلقت خلقة تجعلها تحرق ما تلاقيه ، ولكن مع هذا يجوز أن يلقي نبي في النار فلا يحترق : إما بتغيير صفة النار . أو بتغيير صفة النبي عليه السلام
The second approach: Al-Ghazali said: We concede that fire was created with a nature that makes it burn what it meets, but despite this, it is permissible that a prophet be cast into the fire and not burn: either by changing the property of the fire, or by changing the property of the prophet, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the second approach in the discussion of miracles. Ghazali concedes that fire has a natural property to burn what it comes into contact with. However, he argues that it is still possible for a prophet to be thrown into fire and not be burned. This can happen in two ways: either God changes the property of the fire (so it no longer burns), or God changes the property of the prophet (making him immune to fire). This does not require denying the natural property of fire; rather, it acknowledges that God can alter that property or protect the prophet. Thus, miracles are not violations of logic but divine interventions.

Key Terms
النار (fire)
الخلقة (nature)
النبي (prophet)
تغيير الصفة (changing the property)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles require denying that fire naturally burns.
Correction: Ghazali accepts fire's nature but says God can change its property or the prophet's property.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how can a prophet be saved from burning?
A) By denying the nature of fire
B) By changing the property of fire or the prophet
C) By making fire non-existent
D) By logical impossibility

Key Takeaway
Ghazali reconciles natural causality with miracles by allowing God to change either the property of the fire or the prophet, preserving both regularity and divine intervention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God can change the property of fire or of your own body in an instant, would you still fear the things that threaten you? What would change in your life if you internalized that?
?
The prophet in the fire was thrown by his own people. What 'fire' have you been thrown into by others' words or actions? Do you trust that God can change the nature of that situation to protect you?
?
Ghazali concedes that fire normally burns. How often do you assume that what normally happens must always happen, and forget that the One who set the laws can suspend them? What comfort or challenge does that bring you today?

Ghazali's Refutation of the First Proof for the Soul's Immortality
Ghazali presents the philosophers' first proof and refutes it with four objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's four objections to the philosophers' first proof that the soul cannot perish, and how each objection undermines the proof's logical structure.

Core Idea
Ghazali dismantles the philosophers' proof by exposing its hidden assumptions.

The philosophers argue that the soul cannot perish because non-existence can only happen through death, a contrary, or divine power—all impossible for the soul. Ghazali responds with four incisive objections, each targeting a different weakness in their reasoning. This passage shows his method of refuting philosophical arguments by challenging their premises and demanding stricter proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' proof
Read the opening lines where the philosophers present their three-fold argument that the soul cannot become non-existent. Notice the three possible causes of annihilation they consider.
The proof is based on exhaustive enumeration of ways something can cease to exist.
2
Examine Ghazali's first two objections
Ghazali attacks the proof's dependence on the soul being an immaterial substance, and points out inconsistency regarding the soul's relation to the body.
The first objection references an earlier refutation; the second highlights Ibn Sina's view versus Plato's.
3
Analyze the third and fourth objections
Ghazali asserts that God can annihilate the soul by His power, and demands a proof that non-existence only occurs through those three means.
This challenges the completeness of the philosophers' enumeration and appeals to divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الأول : قولهم : إن عدمها لا يخلو : إما أن يكون بموت البدن، أو بضد يطرأ عليها أو بقدرة القادر. وباطل أن تنعدم بموت البدن؛ فإن البدن ليس محلا لها، بل هو آلة. وفساد الآلة لا يوجب فساد مستعمل الآلة. وباطل أنها تنعدم بالضد؛ إذ الجواهر لا ضد لها. وباطل أنها تفنى بالقدرة؛ إذ العدم ليس شيئاً حتى يتصور وجوده. قال الغزالى : والاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الوجه الأول : أن القول بأنها لا تنعدم بموت البدن، مبنى على أنها كائن مجرد، وقد تقدم فى المسألة التي قبل هذه، إبطال ذلك. الوجه الثاني : أنه مع كون النفس لا تحل البدن عندهم، فلها علاقة بالبدن، حتى لم تحدث إلا بحدوث البدن، وهذا ما اختاره ابن سينا، وخالف به أفلاطون الذى ذهب إلى أن النفس قديمة. الوجه الثالث : لا يبعد أن يقال : تنعدم بقدرة الله تعالى، ما قررناه في مسألة أبدية العالم. الوجه الرابع : مطالبتهم بالدليل على أن عدم الشيء لا يتصور إلا بطريق من هذه الطرق الثلاث.
The first proof: their statement: 'Its non-existence cannot be except in one of three ways: either through the death of the body, or through a contrary that befalls it, or through the power of the Omnipotent.' And it is false that it becomes nonexistent through the death of the body, for the body is not a locus (mahall) for it, but rather an instrument (āla). And the corruption of the instrument does not necessitate the corruption of the user of the instrument. And it is false that it becomes nonexistent through a contrary, because substances (jawāhir) have no contrary. And it is false that it perishes through [divine] power, because non-existence is not a thing such that its existence could be conceived. Al-Ghazālī said: The objection to this is from several aspects: The first aspect: that the statement that it does not become nonexistent through the death of the body is based on its being an immaterial substance (kā’in mujarrad), and the refutation of this has preceded in the issue before this one. The second aspect: that while the soul does not inhere in the body according to them, it has a relation (ʿalāqa) to the body, such that it only originated with the origination of the body, and this is what Ibn Sīnā chose, differing thereby from Plato who held that the soul is pre-eternal. The third aspect: there is no improbability in saying that it becomes nonexistent by the power of God Most High, as we established in the issue of the world's eternity. The fourth aspect: demanding from them a proof that the non-existence of a thing can only be conceived through one of these three ways.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first proof for the impossibility of the soul's annihilation and Ghazali's fourfold refutation. The philosophers argue that non-existence can only occur through death of the body, a contrary, or divine power, and they claim each is impossible for the soul. Ghazali's objections: (1) The argument depends on the soul being an immaterial substance, which was already refuted in the preceding issue. (2) Even if the soul does not inhere in the body, it has a relation to it such that it originates with the body's origination; thus death could cause its annihilation. Ghazali notes that Ibn Sina held this view, differing from Plato who thought the soul pre-existing. (3) It is not far-fetched that God could annihilate it by His power, as argued in the discussion on the world's eternity. (4) They are challenged to prove that non-existence can only occur through these three avenues. This refutation undermines the logical basis of the philosophers' claim.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
عدم (nonexistence)
فناء (annihilation)
ضد (contrary)
قدرة القادر (power of the Omnipotent)
جوهر مجرد (immaterial substance)
علاقة (relation)
بدن (body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali's first objection is merely about the soul's immateriality, but it is a reference to a prior refutation, showing the proof's dependence on an unproven premise.
Correction: The first objection specifically points out that the philosophers' argument relies on the soul being an immaterial substance, a claim Ghazali claims to have already refuted, thereby undermining the proof's foundation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of Ghazali's objections to the first proof?
A) The soul's non-existence through death is possible because the body is its locus.
B) The soul cannot be annihilated by God's power because non-existence is not a thing.
C) The argument presupposes the soul is immaterial, which was already refuted.
D) The soul has no relation to the body, so death does not affect it.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's refutation reveals that the philosophers' proof for the soul's immortality relies on unproven premises about the soul's nature and the possible modes of annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen Ghazali expose hidden assumptions in the philosophers' argument. What hidden assumptions do you hold about your own soul's immortality that you have never questioned? Are you willing to examine them as critically as Ghazali does?
?
If God has the power to annihilate your soul—as Ghazali argues—how does that affect your sense of security? Does it increase your reliance on Him or your fear? What will you do today to strengthen your connection to the One who holds your existence at every moment?
?
The philosophers’ proof seemed logically sound until Ghazali demanded proof for its starting assumption. In your own life, where are you building your beliefs on unproven assumptions? What would it take for you to demand proof of yourself as Ghazali demands of the philosophers?
